wine . . . became drunk: Wine is a gift from God ([Deut 14:26](https://ref.ly/Deut14:26); [Ps 104:15](https://ref.ly/Ps104:15); [Isa 55:1](https://ref.ly/Isa55:1); see [Luke 22:14–20](https://ref.ly/Luke22:14-Luke22:20); [John 2:1–11](https://ref.ly/John2:1-John2:11)). Scripture is clear, however, that excessive consumption of alcohol is a perilous sin ([Prov 23:20–21](https://ref.ly/Prov23:20-Prov23:21), [29–35](https://ref.ly/Prov23:29-Prov23:35); [1 Cor 6:10](https://ref.ly/1Cor6:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 9:21

## Genesis 9:22 (id: 12986)

the father of Canaan: See study note on 9:18. • Ham’s behavior was shameful. He gazed upon his naked father and, rather than covering him and keeping the matter secret, robbed him of his dignity by announcing it to his brothers (see [Exod 21:15](https://ref.ly/Exod21:15), [17](https://ref.ly/Exod21:17); [Lam 4:21](https://ref.ly/Lam4:21); [Hab 2:15](https://ref.ly/Hab2:15)). An ancient Near Eastern tale says that a son is expected to come to his father’s aid when he is drunk (*Tale of Aqhat*; cp. [Isa 51:17–18](https://ref.ly/Isa51:17-Isa51:18)). Ham’s neglect of familial duty explains why Noah praised Shem and Japheth but cursed Ham ([Gen 9:24–27](https://ref.ly/Gen9:24-Gen9:27)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 9:22
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 9:18 (ID: `12981@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 9:25 (id: 12987)

Then he cursed Canaan: Noah foresaw Ham’s actions as morally representative of Ham’s descendants through Canaan.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 9:25

## Genesis 9:26 (id: 12988)

Noah refers to God as the Lord, who formed the covenant with Israel. Shem was the privileged forefather of the Israelites (see [10:21–32](https://ref.ly/Gen10:21-Gen10:32)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 9:26

## Genesis 9:27 (id: 12989)

May Japheth share the prosperity of Shem: Japheth’s descendants would live among Shem’s descendants and share Shem’s prosperity (cp. [Rom 11:17–18](https://ref.ly/Rom11:17-Rom11:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 9:27

## Genesis 10:1 (id: 12249)

Many children were born . . . after the great flood: This fulfilled the renewed creation mandate ([9:1](https://ref.ly/Gen9:1), [9](https://ref.ly/Gen9:9); cp. [1:28](https://ref.ly/Gen1:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:1

## Genesis 10:1–11.9 (id: 12244)

The fifth account ([10:1](https://ref.ly/Gen10:1)) in Genesis (see study note on 2:4) unites the Table of Nations ([10:2–32](https://ref.ly/Gen10:2-Gen10:32)) and the Babel story ([11:1–9](https://ref.ly/Gen11:1-Gen11:9)) around the theme of scattering the nations ([10:5](https://ref.ly/Gen10:5), [18](https://ref.ly/Gen10:18); [11:4](https://ref.ly/Gen11:4), [8–9](https://ref.ly/Gen11:8-Gen11:9)). The Table of Nations precedes the Babel story even though the Babel incident caused the geopolitical situation reflected in the Table of Nations. By reversing the order, Genesis links the repopulation of the earth with the blessing conferred upon Noah and his sons (see [9:1](https://ref.ly/Gen9:1) and study note) and shows that Abram’s call ([12:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:3)) was God’s solution to the problem of human estrangement from God as reflected in the Babel story ([11:1–9](https://ref.ly/Gen11:1-Gen11:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:1–GEN 11:9
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 2:4 (ID: `12412@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Genesis 9:1 (ID: `12982@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 10:2 (id: 12258)

The seven sons of Japheth settled in the region of Anatolia (the western plateau lands of Turkey) and spoke Indo\-European languages. • Gomer was the ancestor of the later Cimmerians who lived north of the Black Sea. • Magog was probably the ancestor of those who settled in the region of Lydia (see [Ezek 38:2](https://ref.ly/Ezek38:2)). • The descendants of Madai were the later Medes of northwest Iran (see [2 Kgs 17:6](https://ref.ly/2Kgs17:6); [Jer 51:11](https://ref.ly/Jer51:11); [Dan 5:28](https://ref.ly/Dan5:28)). • The descendants of Javan were the later Ionian Greeks. • The descendants of Tubal and Meshech were sometimes allies in battle ([Ezek 38:2](https://ref.ly/Ezek38:2)). Both were possibly from the coastal regions of Anatolia (see [Ezek 27:13](https://ref.ly/Ezek27:13)). • The descendants of Tiras possibly became the Thracians that lived near the Aegean Sea.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:2

## Genesis 10:2–32 (id: 12250)

This section describes the ancestral origin of the nations of the ancient Near East. Ham was at the center ([10:6–20](https://ref.ly/Gen10:6-Gen10:20)), while the descendants of Japheth and Shem spread out to the surrounding regions of Greece, Crete, Asia Minor, Mesopotamia, Madai, the Arabian peninsula, and northeast Africa. The list selectively highlights nations relevant to Israel. The total of seventy (seven times ten) names indicates completeness (see [46:27](https://ref.ly/Gen46:27); [Deut 32:8](https://ref.ly/Deut32:8)) and symbolizes the totality of the world, which would later be blessed by the descendants of Abraham ([Gen 18:18](https://ref.ly/Gen18:18)). • Although Shem is mentioned first in [10:1](https://ref.ly/Gen10:1), he is addressed last in the Table because of his connection to Abram ([10:21–31](https://ref.ly/Gen10:21-Gen10:31); [11:10–32](https://ref.ly/Gen11:10-Gen11:32); [12:1](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1)). Although God established the boundaries of all nations (see [Deut 32:8](https://ref.ly/Deut32:8); [Amos 9:7](https://ref.ly/Amos9:7); [Acts 17:26](https://ref.ly/Acts17:26)), Israel was his special creation—a microcosm of seventy people ([Gen 46:27](https://ref.ly/Gen46:27)) called to be a blessing to a world of seventy nations (see [12:3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:2–GEN 10:32

## Genesis 10:3 (id: 12260)

The descendants of Gomer came from near the Upper Euphrates region north of the Black Sea (cp. [Ezek 38:1–9](https://ref.ly/Ezek38:1-Ezek38:9)). • The descendants of Ashkenaz were the later Scythians who inhabited the region between the Black and Caspian Seas. • Riphath is near Carchemish. • The descendants of Togarmah are associated with Til\-garimmu, the capital of Kammanu in modern Armenia (see [Ezek 38:6](https://ref.ly/Ezek38:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:3

## Genesis 10:4 (id: 12261)

Elishah is probably Cyprus. • Tarshish is possibly southwest Spain (see study note on Jon 1:3). • The Kittim were inhabitants of southern Cyprus. • The Rodanim were inhabitants of the island of Rhodes, later a territory of Greece.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:4
* **Associated Articles:** Jonah 1:3 (ID: `19284@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 10:5 (id: 12262)

seafaring peoples . . . various lands: They settled around the Mediterranean and on various islands. • language: This occurred after the Tower of Babel episode ([11:1–9](https://ref.ly/Gen11:1-Gen11:9); see study note on 10:1–11:9).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:5
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 10:1-11.9 (ID: `12244@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 10:6 (id: 12263)

The peoples descended from Ham’s four sons (Egyptians, Babylonians, Assyrians, Canaanites) were Israel’s most hostile neighbors. • Cush was possibly in Ethiopia or ancient Nubia (northern Sudan). • Mizraim was the ancient name for Egypt ([50:11](https://ref.ly/Gen50:11)). • Put was in Libya. • Canaan encompassed southern Syria, Phoenicia, and Palestine west of the Jordan River. In Moses’ time, Egypt and Canaan were provinces of the same empire. Ham’s descendants were excluded from the blessing of Shem’s line ([9:20–28](https://ref.ly/Gen9:20-Gen9:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:6

## Genesis 10:7 (id: 12264)

The sons of Cush and Raamah together total seven. • Seba was in northern Africa (see [Isa 43:3](https://ref.ly/Isa43:3); [45:14](https://ref.ly/Isa45:14)). • Havilah was in southwest Arabia. • Sabtah was in southern Arabia, in ancient Hadramaut, near the Persian Gulf. • Raamah was in southwest Arabia near Najran. • Sabteca was in ancient Samudake near the Persian Gulf. • Sheba was a kingdom in southwest Arabia with commercial colonies (see [1 Kgs 10:1–29](https://ref.ly/1Kgs10:1-1Kgs10:29)). • Dedan was in northern Arabia.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:7

## Genesis 10:8–12 (id: 12265)

Special attention is given to the early history of Babylonia and Assyria, the Mesopotamian empires that would conquer and exile Israel and Judah.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:8–GEN 10:12

## Genesis 10:9 (id: 12266)

Nimrod attained great fame by conquest and terror; his empire extended from Babylonia in the south to Assyria in the north ([10:10–12](https://ref.ly/Gen10:10-Gen10:12)). • the greatest hunter in the world: Assyrian monarchs glorified their own power, often depicting themselves as valiant hunter\-conquerors.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:9

## Genesis 10:10–12 (id: 12245)

Babylonia is the area surrounding the Tigris and Euphrates in southern Mesopotamia. This kingdom eventually reached into northern Mesopotamia (Assyria). • Of the cities mentioned, Babylon is most important because of its role in building the Tower of Babel (see [11:4](https://ref.ly/Gen11:4) and study note). • Erech was ancient Uruk and is now Warka in southern Iraq (see [Ezra 4:9–10](https://ref.ly/Ezra4:9-Ezra4:10)). • Akkad was the ancient Agade north of Babylon, home of the famous ruler Sargon (2370–2295 BC). • The location of Calneh is uncertain, though it is presumably one of Nimrod’s cities located north of Aram\-naharaim in southern Mesopotamia (cp. [Amos 6:2](https://ref.ly/Amos6:2)). • building . . . Nineveh: Like Cain, Nimrod built cities (see [Gen 4:17](https://ref.ly/Gen4:17) and comments). Nineveh was an ancient Assyrian city on the east bank of the Tigris River in northern Iraq. • Rehoboth\-ir was a daughter\-city of Nineveh or was located nearby. • Calah is modern Tell Nimrud, south of Nineveh. • Resen is possibly modern Selamiyeh, northwest of Tell Nimrud.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:10–GEN 10:12
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 11:4 (ID: `12280@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 10:13–14 (id: 12246)

* *the Ludites* were Lydian tribes located west of the Nile delta.
* The identity of *the Anamites* is unclear. They might have been Egyptians near Cyrene, west of Egypt.
* *the Lehabites* were probably a Libyan tribe.
* *the Naphtuhites* lived in northern Egypt.
* *the Pathrusites* lived in southern Egypt.
* *the Casluhites* might have lived in an Egyptian area also known as "Cyrenaica."
* *the Caphtorites* were Cretans (see [Jeremiah 47:4](https://ref.ly/Jer47:4); [Amos 9:7](https://ref.ly/Amos9:7)).
* *the Philistines* from Crete were warriors and traders who traveled by ship from Crete to settle in southwest Canaan. They lived there during the time when the Israelites left Egypt and in later periods ([Exodus 13:17](https://ref.ly/Exod13:17); [Amos 9:7](https://ref.ly/Amos9:7)). During the early years of Israel's kings, the Philistines were one of Israel's strongest enemies (see 1–2 Samuel).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:13–GEN 10:14

## Genesis 10:15–18 (id: 12247)

Sidon settled in Phoenicia, north of Canaan. • Hittites: The Hittites in Genesis were a coalition of cities within Canaan (see [26:34–35](https://ref.ly/Gen26:34-Gen26:35); [27:46](https://ref.ly/Gen27:46); [Ezek 16:3](https://ref.ly/Ezek16:3)). They were probably not the same as the Hittites of Anatolia (Asia Minor), whose empire was one of the great empires of antiquity during the patriarchal period. • The Jebusites were ancient inhabitants of Jerusalem ([Josh 15:63](https://ref.ly/Josh15:63); [Judg 19:10–11](https://ref.ly/Judg19:10-Judg19:11); [2 Sam 5:6–9](https://ref.ly/2Sam5:6-2Sam5:9)). • The Amorites lived throughout the mountains of Palestine in Canaan (see [Gen 15:16](https://ref.ly/Gen15:16); [48:22](https://ref.ly/Gen48:22); [Num 13:29](https://ref.ly/Num13:29); [Deut 3:8](https://ref.ly/Deut3:8); [Josh 10:5](https://ref.ly/Josh10:5); [Judg 1:35](https://ref.ly/Judg1:35); [10:8](https://ref.ly/Judg10:8); [Ezek 16:3](https://ref.ly/Ezek16:3)). • Little is known of the Girgashites, a Canaanite tribe ([Gen 15:21](https://ref.ly/Gen15:21); [Deut 7:1](https://ref.ly/Deut7:1); [Josh 3:10](https://ref.ly/Josh3:10)). • The Hivites were an uncircumcised Canaanite tribe ([Gen 34:2](https://ref.ly/Gen34:2), [13–24](https://ref.ly/Gen34:13-Gen34:24); [Josh 9:1](https://ref.ly/Josh9:1), [7](https://ref.ly/Josh9:7); [11:3](https://ref.ly/Josh11:3); [Judg 3:3](https://ref.ly/Judg3:3); [2 Sam 24:7](https://ref.ly/2Sam24:7)). • The Arkites resided in Tell ’Arqa in Lebanon. • The Sinites formed a city\-state and inhabited Phoenicia. • The Arvadites inhabited Ruad in northern Phoenicia, near the El Kebir River. They were known for shipping (cp. [Ezek 27:8](https://ref.ly/Ezek27:8)). • The Zemarites inhabited Sumur (modern Sumra), north of Arka on the Phoenician coast. • The Hamathites founded what is now Hama on the Orontes River, the northern boundary of Canaan (see [Num 34:8](https://ref.ly/Num34:8); [Josh 13:5](https://ref.ly/Josh13:5); [2 Sam 8:9–10](https://ref.ly/2Sam8:9-2Sam8:10); [1 Kgs 8:65](https://ref.ly/1Kgs8:65); [2 Kgs 14:25–28](https://ref.ly/2Kgs14:25-2Kgs14:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:15–GEN 10:18

## Genesis 10:19 (id: 12248)

The territory of Canaan is specifically marked off because it would be taken from its inhabitants and given to Israel (see [15:18](https://ref.ly/Gen15:18); [Num 34:2–12](https://ref.ly/Num34:2-Num34:12); [Ezek 47:15–20](https://ref.ly/Ezek47:15-Ezek47:20); [48:1–28](https://ref.ly/Ezek48:1-Ezek48:28)). • An ancient north\-south seacoast highway (the Via Maris) extended from Sidon . . . to Gerar, connecting Egypt to Mesopotamia. • Modern Gaza is 11 miles northwest of Gerar. • Sodom and Gomorrah were cities on the border of the land southeast of the Dead Sea. • Admah and Zeboiim are mentioned 15 times in connection with Sodom and Gomorrah ([14:2](https://ref.ly/Gen14:2), [8](https://ref.ly/Gen14:8); [Deut 29:23](https://ref.ly/Deut29:23); see [Hos 11:8](https://ref.ly/Hos11:8)). All four cities were destroyed by God to cleanse the land (see [Gen 19](https://ref.ly/Gen19:1-Gen19:38)). • Lasha was possibly in the northern region of the Dead Sea.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:19

## Genesis 10:21 (id: 12251)

Shem, the older brother of Japheth, was the father of the Semitic peoples. The descendants listed represent countries east of Israel (modern Iraq, Iran, and Syria). The narrator lists these locations within Mesopotamia since Abram, the father of Israel, originated from this area (see [11:27–32](https://ref.ly/Gen11:27-Gen11:32)). • Eber receives special attention because of his connection with Abram (see study note on 10:24).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:21
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 10:24 (ID: `12254@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 10:22 (id: 12252)

The descendants of Elam lived in the region of modern southwestern Iran (see [14:1](https://ref.ly/Gen14:1), [9](https://ref.ly/Gen14:9); [Ezra 4:9](https://ref.ly/Ezra4:9); [Isa 11:11](https://ref.ly/Isa11:11)). • The descendants of Asshur were later Assyrians who lived under Nimrod’s jurisdiction (see [Gen 10:11](https://ref.ly/Gen10:11)). Sumerians descended from Ham were ousted by Mesopotamian Semites. • Arphaxad possibly settled northeast of Nineveh; his descendants are further described in [11:12–26](https://ref.ly/Gen11:12-Gen11:26). • Lud was near the Tigris River; its people were related to the Lydians (see [10:13](https://ref.ly/Gen10:13)). • Aram was a kingdom of tribes that lived in the Mesopotamian plains.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:22

## Genesis 10:23 (id: 12253)

The patriarchs later interacted with the descendants of Aram (see [25:20](https://ref.ly/Gen25:20); [31:20](https://ref.ly/Gen31:20); [Deut 26:5](https://ref.ly/Deut26:5)). • Uz was the chief Aramean tribe, possibly located northeast of the Jordan; it was Job’s home (see [Job 1:1](https://ref.ly/Job1:1); see also [Lam 4:21](https://ref.ly/Lam4:21)). • Hul is unknown. He possibly founded Armenia. • Gether is unknown; he was possibly the founder of the Syrians. • Mash might be associated with Mount Masus in northern Mesopotamia or with a part of the Lebanon Mountains.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:23

## Genesis 10:24 (id: 12254)

Shelah is unknown, but may be short for Methushelah (cp. [38:5](https://ref.ly/Gen38:5), [11](https://ref.ly/Gen38:11)). • Eber was the ancestor of Abram the Hebrew ([11:10–26](https://ref.ly/Gen11:10-Gen11:26)); his name is at the root of the term “Hebrew” (see [14:13](https://ref.ly/Gen14:13); [39:14](https://ref.ly/Gen39:14); [40:15](https://ref.ly/Gen40:15); [41:12](https://ref.ly/Gen41:12); [Exod 2:11](https://ref.ly/Exod2:11); [3:18](https://ref.ly/Exod3:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:24

## Genesis 10:25 (id: 12255)

Peleg means division, anticipating the separation of people into language groups after Babel ([11:1–9](https://ref.ly/Gen11:1-Gen11:9)). Peleg’s line led to Abram (see [11:16–26](https://ref.ly/Gen11:16-Gen11:26)). • Joktan was the ancestor of the southern Arabian tribes. The Ishmaelite tribes were in northern Arabia (see [25:13–16](https://ref.ly/Gen25:13-Gen25:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:25

## Genesis 10:26–29 (id: 12256)

Almodad was an ancestor, region, or tribe in modern Yemen. • Sheleph was a tribe of Yemen. • Hazarmaveth was related to Hadramaut in southern Arabia. • Jerah is unknown, but was possibly associated with Mount Barach. • Hadoram was an Arabian tribe. • Uzal was Sana’a, an old capital of Yemen in pre\-Islamic times. • Diklah was a southern Arabian oasis in Mina. • Obal was between Hodeida and Sana’a in southwest Arabia. • Abimael was a Sabaean. • Sheba was in southern Arabia (see [10:7](https://ref.ly/Gen10:7)). • Ophir was a region of southern Arabia between Sheba and Havilah; it was a source of gold ([Isa 13:12](https://ref.ly/Isa13:12)). • Havilah: See [10:7](https://ref.ly/Gen10:7). • Jobab was possibly Jobebitai in southern Arabia.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:26–GEN 10:29

## Genesis 10:26–32 (id: 12257)

There were fourteen sons of Shem by Eber through Joktan. The placement of the Babel story between the lines of Joktan and Peleg ties Joktan to the judgment of the Babel story ([11:1–9](https://ref.ly/Gen11:1-Gen11:9)) and ties Peleg to Abram ([11:27–12:1](https://ref.ly/Gen11:27-Gen12:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:26–GEN 10:32

## Genesis 10:30 (id: 12259)

Mesha was a region in northern Arabia, south of Hadramaut. • Sephar is identified with Isfar, south of Hadramaut in Yemen.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 10:30

## Genesis 11:1 (id: 12270)

*Now*: The events in [11:1–9](https://ref.ly/Gen11:1-Gen11:9) caused the nations to scatter, as shown in the family histories of [10:2–30](https://ref.ly/Gen10:2-Gen10:30). Although these events happened in a different order than they are written, the author arranged them this way to help explain things about God's actions and plans more clearly (see study note on 10:1–11:9).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:1
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 10:1-11.9 (ID: `12244@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 11:1–9 (id: 12267)

The story of the unfinished tower carries forward themes of language and solidarity from the Table of Nations (ch [10](https://ref.ly/Gen10:1-Gen10:32)). The builders’ desire for autonomy recalls the rebellion in Eden (ch [3](https://ref.ly/Gen3:1-Gen3:24)) and establishes the need for Abram’s redemptive faith in the midst of international disorder (ch [12](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:20)). The scattering of the nations anticipates the warning to Israel that idolatry would result in their being scattered and their cities devastated (see [Lev 26:33](https://ref.ly/Lev26:33); [Num 10:35](https://ref.ly/Num10:35); [Deut 4:27](https://ref.ly/Deut4:27); [28:64](https://ref.ly/Deut28:64); [30:3](https://ref.ly/Deut30:3)). Chronologically, the story is a flashback that explains the rise of the nations during Peleg’s time (see [Gen 10:25](https://ref.ly/Gen10:25)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:1–GEN 11:9

## Genesis 11:2 (id: 12276)

migrated to the east: See study note on 3:24. • Babylonia was located in southern Mesopotamia, the region of Nimrod’s later empire and city\-building campaign (see [10:10](https://ref.ly/Gen10:10); [Isa 11:11](https://ref.ly/Isa11:11); [Dan 1:2](https://ref.ly/Dan1:2); [Zech 5:11](https://ref.ly/Zech5:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:2
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 3:24 (ID: `12605@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 11:3 (id: 12279)

Stone was plentiful in Canaan; in Mesopotamia, stone was scarce and brick technology was developed. • Tar was made from bitumen, a natural, cement\-like, waterproof asphalt (see [6:14](https://ref.ly/Gen6:14); [Exod 2:3](https://ref.ly/Exod2:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:3

## Genesis 11:4 (id: 12280)

Far from the original garden ([2:15](https://ref.ly/Gen2:15)), the first cities of Genesis represent arrogance ([4:17](https://ref.ly/Gen4:17)), tyranny ([10:8–12](https://ref.ly/Gen10:8-Gen10:12)), and wickedness ([18:20–21](https://ref.ly/Gen18:20-Gen18:21)). The city on the Babylonian plain was a magnet for human pride and idolatry. • a tower that reaches into the sky: This was probably a temple\-tower (a ziggurat). Common in ancient Babylonian urban culture, ziggurats were regarded as sacred mountains by which deities descended to earth (Jacob’s dream in [28:12](https://ref.ly/Gen28:12) possibly reflects this idea). • This will make us famous (literally *let us make a name for ourselves*): The tower builders sought fame through idolatrous ambition. God promised to give Abram a famous name because of his humble obedience ([12:2](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:4

## Genesis 11:5 (id: 12281)

came down: The tower was a human attempt to ascend to God’s realm (see [Deut 26:15](https://ref.ly/Deut26:15); [Pss 2:4](https://ref.ly/Ps2:4); [103:19](https://ref.ly/Ps103:19); [115:16](https://ref.ly/Ps115:16)). The folly of that attempt was exposed by God’s “coming down” to see their feeble efforts.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:5

## Genesis 11:6 (id: 12282)

If left unchecked, the tower builders’ solidarity and ambition would allow human wickedness to flourish in unimaginable ways.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:6

## Genesis 11:7 (id: 12283)

Come, let’s go down: God addresses his angelic court (see [1:26](https://ref.ly/Gen1:26); [3:22](https://ref.ly/Gen3:22); and study notes). • won’t be able to understand each other: Their inability to communicate would curtail their unified sinful ambition. The God\-honoring unity of language on the day of Pentecost was a symbolic reversal of the Babel dispersion ([Acts 2:5–13](https://ref.ly/Acts2:5-Acts2:13); see [Zeph 3:9](https://ref.ly/Zeph3:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:7
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 1:26 (ID: `12232@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 11:8 (id: 12284)

the Lord scattered them: Similarly, Adam and Eve’s punishment for grasping at autonomy and Cain’s punishment for murder involved banishment and dispersion ([3:23](https://ref.ly/Gen3:23); [4:12](https://ref.ly/Gen4:12), [14](https://ref.ly/Gen4:14); [9:19](https://ref.ly/Gen9:19); [10:5](https://ref.ly/Gen10:5), [25](https://ref.ly/Gen10:25), [32](https://ref.ly/Gen10:32)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:8

## Genesis 11:9 (id: 12285)

Babel: The Babylonians viewed their city as the residence or gateway of the gods. The pun that concludes this account accurately reveals Babylon’s spiritual confusion. Babylon achieved prominence under Nimrod ([10:10](https://ref.ly/Gen10:10)) and in later biblical history (see [2 Kgs 25:1–30](https://ref.ly/2Kgs25:1-2Kgs25:30)). Its role as an epicenter of arrogance and idolatry make it a fitting image for the anti\-God forces associated with the end of time (e.g., [Rev 14:8](https://ref.ly/Rev14:8); [16:19](https://ref.ly/Rev16:19); [18:2](https://ref.ly/Rev18:2)). • The tower builders had centralized to ascend into God’s realm ([Gen 11:3–4](https://ref.ly/Gen11:3-Gen11:4)). God descended and scattered them all over the world to frustrate their idolatrous ambition.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:9

## Genesis 11:10 (id: 12268)

This account of Shem’s family resumes the line of Shem from [10:21–32](https://ref.ly/Gen10:21-Gen10:32), now with special focus on the line leading to Abram. Only Abram and Israel are heirs to Shem’s God (see [9:26–27](https://ref.ly/Gen9:26-Gen9:27); [Deut 32:8–9](https://ref.ly/Deut32:8-Deut32:9)). The Babel story vividly repudiates the culture that Abram was called to abandon ([Gen 12:1](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1); [24:6–7](https://ref.ly/Gen24:6-Gen24:7)). Together with the account of Terah’s descendants ([11:27–32](https://ref.ly/Gen11:27-Gen11:32)), this second account of Shem’s line forms a bridge from the universal history of chs [1–11](https://ref.ly/Gen1:1-Gen11:32) to the national history of Israel that begins in ch [12](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:20). Abram is the remnant from Babel’s confused world. God called him as an act of grace whereby the fractured world of Babel would be blessed ([12:3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:3)). • the father of: Or *the ancestor of;* Hebrew genealogies do not necessarily list every single generation.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:10

## Genesis 11:18 (id: 12269)

Peleg: See study note on 10:25.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:18
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 10:25 (ID: `12255@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 11:27 (id: 12273)

Nahor was the grandfather of Laban, whose daughters later became Jacob’s wives (chs [29–31](https://ref.ly/Gen29:1-Gen31:55)). • Lot: See “Lot” Profile.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:27
* **Associated Articles:** Lot (ID: `500456@TyndaleStudyNotesProfiles`)

## Genesis 11:27–32 (id: 12272)

This brief section provides a complete summary of Terah’s life and accounts for his other sons and their marriages; it also introduces Lot, Abram’s nephew, who later played a prominent role. The ancestors, including Terah and his family, were idolatrous, worshiping other gods in Mesopotamia ([Josh 24:2](https://ref.ly/Josh24:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:27–GEN 11:32

## Genesis 11:27–25.11 (id: 12271)

This is the account (Hebrew toledoth; see study note on 2:4) of Terah’s family: What follows in [11:28–25:11](https://ref.ly/Gen11:28-Gen25:11) are the particulars about the family descended from Terah, notably about Abraham and God’s covenant with him, and about Isaac, the child of promise, who carried forward the line and the blessing to the next generation.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:27
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 2:4 (ID: `12412@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 11:28 (id: 12274)

The call of Abram occurred in Ur of the Chaldeans ([15:7](https://ref.ly/Gen15:7); [Acts 7:2–4](https://ref.ly/Acts7:2-Acts7:4)), the main city of Sumer in Mesopotamia near the mouth of the Persian Gulf. The family had moved there perhaps generations before the call. Their ancestral home (“native country,” [Gen 12:1](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1)) was apparently near Haran, in the region of the descendants of Shem ([11:10–26](https://ref.ly/Gen11:10-Gen11:26)); thus they settled there when they left Ur ([11:31](https://ref.ly/Gen11:31)) and were later described as “Arameans” ([Deut 26:5](https://ref.ly/Deut26:5)). • land of his birth: The same Hebrew phrase is repeated in [12:1](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1) (“native country”), making Ur, not Haran, the location of Abram’s call (see [15:7](https://ref.ly/Gen15:7); [Neh 9:7](https://ref.ly/Neh9:7); [Acts 7:2](https://ref.ly/Acts7:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:28

## Genesis 11:29 (id: 12275)

Sarai means “princess” in Hebrew. No mention is made of Sarai’s parentage, perhaps to add suspense to the Abimelech story, which reveals that she was Abram’s half sister ([20:9–12](https://ref.ly/Gen20:9-Gen20:12)). Later, the law prohibited such a marriage ([Lev 18:9](https://ref.ly/Lev18:9); [20:17](https://ref.ly/Lev20:17); [Deut 27:22](https://ref.ly/Deut27:22)). • Nahor’s wife was Milcah: Milcah was Haran’s daughter and Nahor’s niece (see [Gen 11:29](https://ref.ly/Gen11:29)). Her son Bethuel was the father of Rebekah, the wife of Abram’s son Isaac ([24:10](https://ref.ly/Gen24:10), [15](https://ref.ly/Gen24:15), [24](https://ref.ly/Gen24:24)). The name Milcah is related to the Hebrew word meaning “queen.” In Akkadian, it is a title of the goddess Ishtar, the moon\-god’s daughter. Terah’s name is related to the word for “moon” in Hebrew; his whole family appears to have worshiped Sin, the moon\-god (see [Josh 24:14](https://ref.ly/Josh24:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:29

## Genesis 11:30 (id: 12277)

Sarai, Rebekah ([25:21](https://ref.ly/Gen25:21)), and Rachel ([29:31](https://ref.ly/Gen29:31)) all suffered infertility. Sarai’s situation in particular highlights the paradox between the apparent reality and God’s promise to give many descendants ([12:2](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2)). The Israelite nation’s origin from barren women fixes its identity in the sovereignty of God, who miraculously gives children to barren women (see also [1 Sam 1:2](https://ref.ly/1Sam1:2); [2:5](https://ref.ly/1Sam2:5); [Ps 113:9](https://ref.ly/Ps113:9); [Isa 54:1](https://ref.ly/Isa54:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:30

## Genesis 11:31 (id: 12278)

Terah took: The text is clear that Abram’s departure from Ur was prompted by God’s calling (see study note on 11:28), but the event is described from Terah’s perspective, in keeping with the patriarchy of ancient Near Eastern culture. This cultural deference to the oldest male is evidently why Abram did not continue on to Canaan by himself at this time (see [Acts 7:2–4](https://ref.ly/Acts7:2-Acts7:4)). • Haran was 550 miles northwest of Ur, near the Syrian\-Turkish border. Despite the similar name, there is no connection with Terah’s son Haran, who had died in Ur ([11:28](https://ref.ly/Gen11:28)). •*Haran* means “caravan.” Ancient commercial routes converged there, making it a key site for trade. •*Haran* was also well known for the moon worship to which Terah’s family was apparently devoted (see study note on 11:29).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 11:31
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 11:28 (ID: `12274@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Genesis 11:29 (ID: `12275@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 12:1 (id: 12296)

Abram knew that he should leave, but he did not know where he was going. Obedience required faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:1

## Genesis 12:1–3 (id: 12286)

These verses are structured around two commands to Abram: Leave and be a blessing (see study note on 12:2). Each directive is followed by three promises conditioned upon obedience.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:1–GEN 12:3
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 12:2 (ID: `12298@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 12:1–9 (id: 12287)

Through Abram’s faith and family, God began restoring the blessing. God called Abram from a pagan world to begin a new nation; his promises to Abram later became a covenant (ch [15](https://ref.ly/Gen15:1-Gen15:21)). • God’s call to Abram later helped convince the Israelites to leave Egypt and go to the land God promised to Abram. It also reminded the Babylonian exiles of their need to return to their own land (e.g., [Isa 51](https://ref.ly/Isa51:1-Isa51:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:1–GEN 12:9

## Genesis 12:2 (id: 12298)

* *make your name great:* Abram gained the fame that the builders of the tower of Babel wanted for themselves (see [11:4](https://ref.ly/Gen11:4) and study note).
* *so that you will be a blessing:* This statement is a command and a promise in Hebrew. It depends on Abram obeying God's command ([12:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:3)): “Go … so that you will be a blessing. I will bless those who bless you and curse those who curse you.”

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:2
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 11:4 (ID: `12280@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 12:3 (id: 12299)

Based on Abram’s obedience to the command to be a blessing ([12:2](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2)), God gave him three more promises. • those who treat you with contempt: People who disregarded Abram and his covenant were rejecting God’s choice and plan. • All the families on earth will be blessed: By faith, they could participate in the covenant God was making with Abram. The blessing spread to the whole world through Abraham, Israel, the covenants, the prophets, Scripture, and the Messiah ([Gal 3:8](https://ref.ly/Gal3:8), [16](https://ref.ly/Gal3:16); cp. [Rom 9:4–5](https://ref.ly/Rom9:4-Rom9:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:3

## Genesis 12:4 (id: 12301)

Abram was middle\-aged, settled, prosperous, aristocratic, and polytheistic (see study note on 11:27–32). When the Lord spoke to him ([12:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:3)), he obediently left his old ways in Ur to follow God’s plan. Since Abram responded in faith, God’s promises ([12:2–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2-Gen12:3)) could be confirmed in a binding covenant ([15:8–21](https://ref.ly/Gen15:8-Gen15:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:4
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 11:27-32 (ID: `12272@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 12:4–9 (id: 12300)

Abram’s obedience to God’s call corresponded to God’s commands (see study note on 12:1–3). He journeyed to Canaan ([12:4–6](https://ref.ly/Gen12:4-Gen12:6)) and became a blessing ([12:5–9](https://ref.ly/Gen12:5-Gen12:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:4–GEN 12:9
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 12:1-3 (ID: `12286@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 12:5 (id: 12302)

The people (Hebrew hannepesh, “the lives”) he had taken into his household were probably converts; Abram first became a blessing by influencing people in his household to join him in following the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:5

## Genesis 12:6–7 (id: 12303)

The oak of Moreh was apparently a Canaanite shrine; fertile groves of trees were sacred to the Canaanites (cp. [Isa 1:29](https://ref.ly/Isa1:29)), and *Moreh* means “teacher.” Abram proclaimed (Luther: “preached”) the Lord’s name beside a pagan place of worship and instruction ([Gen 12:8](https://ref.ly/Gen12:8)). • Abram continued to be a blessing when he built an altar to worship God at Shechem and east of Bethel ([12:8](https://ref.ly/Gen12:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:6–GEN 12:7

## Genesis 12:7 (id: 12304)

* *the LORD appeared to Abram* at Shechem ([12:6](https://ref.ly/Gen12:6)) to confirm that this was the promised land. Israel was meant to live in this land, but they needed faith to share in God's promises (compare [Numbers 14](https://ref.ly/Num14:1-Num14:45); [Joshua 1:6–9](https://ref.ly/Josh1:6-Josh1:9)).
* *to your offspring:* Abram did not own the land yet. He lived there as a temporary settler.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:7

## Genesis 12:8 (id: 12306)

he worshiped the Lord (literally *he made proclamation of the* Lord *by name*): Proclaiming the name (identity and character) of the Lord is central to worship and witness (cp. [4:26](https://ref.ly/Gen4:26); see [Exod 34:5–7](https://ref.ly/Exod34:5-Exod34:7)). Abram had to distinguish his sacrificial worship from that of the pagan Canaanites.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:8

## Genesis 12:8–9 (id: 12305)

Abram had to keep moving camp because the Canaanites had the fertile land.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:8–GEN 12:9

## Genesis 12:10 (id: 12290)

The Nile River provided ample irrigation, so Egypt was often the last region to suffer from famine.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:10

## Genesis 12:10–13 (id: 12288)

Abram’s scheme was rooted in fear that jeopardized his family and God’s promises. Abram was not walking by faith when he went to Egypt. He stopped building altars and his deceptiveness took center stage. Deception would plague his family throughout Genesis ([26:1–11](https://ref.ly/Gen26:1-Gen26:11); [27:1–29](https://ref.ly/Gen27:1-Gen27:29); [29:15–30](https://ref.ly/Gen29:15-Gen29:30); [30:34–36](https://ref.ly/Gen30:34-Gen30:36); [31:6–11](https://ref.ly/Gen31:6-Gen31:11); [37:18–35](https://ref.ly/Gen37:18-Gen37:35); [39:7–20](https://ref.ly/Gen39:7-Gen39:20)). • Abram’s plan was probably based on a social custom whereby a brother arranged the marriage of his sister (cp. [24:29–61](https://ref.ly/Gen24:29-Gen24:61)). Abram may have thought that any potential suitor would have to deal with him, giving him time to leave with Sarai. He did not count on Pharaoh’s acting without negotiation ([12:14–16](https://ref.ly/Gen12:14-Gen12:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:10–GEN 12:13

## Genesis 12:10–20 (id: 12289)

This episode shows that God would not allow Abram to jeopardize his promises. Just after Abram’s obedience to the call, a famine tested his weak faith. God delivered him and his family, even though Abram foolishly used deception rather than trusting in God to preserve him in Egypt. • This story deliberately parallels Israel’s later bondage in Egypt. Because of a famine ([Gen 12:10](https://ref.ly/Gen12:10) *//* [Gen 47:13](https://ref.ly/Gen47:13)), Abram/Israel went to Egypt ([Gen 12:10](https://ref.ly/Gen12:10) *//* [Gen 47:27](https://ref.ly/Gen47:27)); there was an attempt to kill the males and save the females ([Gen 12:12](https://ref.ly/Gen12:12) *//* [Exod 1:22](https://ref.ly/Exod1:22)); God plagued Egypt ([Gen 12:17](https://ref.ly/Gen12:17) *//* [Exod 7:14–11:10](https://ref.ly/Exod7:14-Exod11:10)); Abram/Israel plundered Egypt ([Gen 12:16](https://ref.ly/Gen12:16) *//* [Exod 12:35–36](https://ref.ly/Exod12:35-Exod12:36)); they were expelled (Hebrew shalakh, “send”; [Gen 12:19–20](https://ref.ly/Gen12:19-Gen12:20) *//* [Exod 12:31–33](https://ref.ly/Exod12:31-Exod12:33)) and ascended to the Negev ([Gen 13:1](https://ref.ly/Gen13:1) *//* [Num 13:17](https://ref.ly/Num13:17), [22](https://ref.ly/Num13:22)). Israel was to believe that God would deliver them from bondage in Egypt through the plagues because their ancestor had already been rescued from bondage in Egypt.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:10–GEN 12:20

## Genesis 12:13 (id: 12291)

tell them you are my sister: This request occurs three times in Genesis (see also [20:2](https://ref.ly/Gen20:2); [26:7](https://ref.ly/Gen26:7)). The text explains that this was Abram’s usual strategy ([20:13](https://ref.ly/Gen20:13)), and his son did likewise. This first occasion was outside the land, the second (ch [20](https://ref.ly/Gen20:1-Gen20:18)) within, showing that God protected his promise in both regions.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:13

## Genesis 12:14–15 (id: 12292)

Sarai was 65 years old, but she lived to be 127; she was like a modern childless woman of about 35\. She and Abram came from a noble family (see study note on 11:29), so she was regal in her person and dress. Pharaoh was attracted by her physical appearance and her political assets.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:14–GEN 12:15
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 11:29 (ID: `12275@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 12:14–16 (id: 12293)

Abram was bound by the king’s gift to an unwanted agreement about Sarai that he could not prevent. His scheme had resulted in a terrible bind that endangered him, Sarai, and the promise. • Abram appeared to prosper from his deception, but the new possessions also caused crises. Abram and Lot had to separate (ch [13](https://ref.ly/Gen13:1-Gen13:18)), and Hagar, an Egyptian maiden, became the mother of the Ishmaelites, perennial enemies of Israel (ch [16](https://ref.ly/Gen16:1-Gen16:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:14–GEN 12:16

## Genesis 12:15 (id: 12294)

Pharaoh was a title, not a personal name ([37:36](https://ref.ly/Gen37:36); [Exod 1:15](https://ref.ly/Exod1:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:15

## Genesis 12:17–19 (id: 12295)

God’s intervention rescued Sarai and preserved the marriage to fulfill the covenant promise. Sarai’s restoration to Abram came with a rebuke from Pharaoh on God’s behalf ([12:18–19](https://ref.ly/Gen12:18-Gen12:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:17–GEN 12:19

## Genesis 12:20 (id: 12297)

No answer to Pharaoh’s questions ([12:18–19](https://ref.ly/Gen12:18-Gen12:19)) was needed, because the rebuke was followed by expulsion. Pharaoh’s command paralleled God’s command to Abram ([12:1](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1)), but Pharaoh’s demand brought shame and disgrace. God was faithful in preserving his promise.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 12:20

## Genesis 13:1–7 (id: 12307)

This story is set in conflict amid God’s blessings. In the opening verses, Abram returns to a place where he had built an altar. Previous events are emphasized as Abram’s return to the land is described ([13:3–4](https://ref.ly/Gen13:3-Gen13:4)); Abram renewed his worship and again proclaimed the Lord’s name (cp. [12:8](https://ref.ly/Gen12:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:1–GEN 13:7

## Genesis 13:2 (id: 12314)

Abram already had powerful resources ([12:5](https://ref.ly/Gen12:5)); his Egyptian sojourn augmented his wealth and power ([12:16](https://ref.ly/Gen12:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:2

## Genesis 13:5–7 (id: 12315)

Lot was also wealthy, with flocks and herds. Tents figure prominently in Lot’s story ([13:12](https://ref.ly/Gen13:12)). • The Canaanites and Perizzites (see [34:30](https://ref.ly/Gen34:30); [Deut 7:1](https://ref.ly/Deut7:1); [Judg 1:4](https://ref.ly/Judg1:4); [3:5](https://ref.ly/Judg3:5)) held the well\-watered land; the quarrel between Abram’s and Lot’s herdsmen left Abram more vulnerable to attack.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:5–GEN 13:7

## Genesis 13:8 (id: 12317)

Abram was concerned that there be no conflict (Hebrew meribah) between them, as they were close relatives (literally *brothers*). Moses later reproved Israel over the incident in the wilderness at Meribah ([Exod 17:1–7](https://ref.ly/Exod17:1-Exod17:7); [Num 20:1–13](https://ref.ly/Num20:1-Num20:13)) and instructed them on exercising faith in such situations. Meribah thereafter became a watchword for testing and striving with the Lord in unbelief (see [Ps 95](https://ref.ly/Ps95:1-Ps95:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:8

## Genesis 13:8–13 (id: 12316)

Abram, to whom the land was promised, might have told Lot to find his own place. Abram’s generosity was an act of faith; he knew that even if he gave the whole land away, God would still give it to him and his descendants. Abram did not have to cling to things, whereas Lot’s choices were self\-seeking.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:8–GEN 13:13

## Genesis 13:10 (id: 12308)

What appealed to Lot would be short\-lived. In the garden of the Lord, Adam and Eve succumbed to their craving for what they saw; Israel was later enslaved in Egypt. Sodom and Gomorrah are reminders of putting intense desires ahead of obedience to God ([3:5–6](https://ref.ly/Gen3:5-Gen3:6); see [1 Jn 2:16](https://ref.ly/1John2:16)). • Zoar was a small town in the plain to which Lot and his daughters later fled ([19:18–22](https://ref.ly/Gen19:18-Gen19:22)); it was previously called Bela ([14:2](https://ref.ly/Gen14:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:10

## Genesis 13:11 (id: 12310)

Lot’s choice was totally selfish, without concern for Abram or faith in the Lord. • The region called the whole Jordan Valley (literally *the circle of the Jordan*) is believed to have been near the south end of the Dead Sea, based on descriptions in ancient records that locate cities of the Plain. This area is now very desolate.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:11

## Genesis 13:11–18 (id: 12309)

The narrator makes numerous contrasts between Lot and Abram.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:11–GEN 13:18

## Genesis 13:13 (id: 12311)

The implication is that Lot would not resist Sodom’s influence because he, too, was living for himself.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:13

## Genesis 13:14–17 (id: 12312)

Abram could give Lot the choice land because he believed in God’s promise. Abram waited for God to give him the land; Lot just took what he wanted.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:14–GEN 13:17

## Genesis 13:18 (id: 12313)

Hebron was an Anakite city ([Num 13:22](https://ref.ly/Num13:22)) originally called Kiriath\-arba (“city of Arba”), located in forested highlands just north of the Negev ([Gen 12:9](https://ref.ly/Gen12:9); [Josh 17:15](https://ref.ly/Josh17:15)). Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob all settled there ([Gen 18:1](https://ref.ly/Gen18:1); [35:27](https://ref.ly/Gen35:27); [37:14](https://ref.ly/Gen37:14)), and Sarah, Abraham, Isaac, Rebekah, Jacob, and Leah were buried there ([23:19](https://ref.ly/Gen23:19); [35:27–29](https://ref.ly/Gen35:27-Gen35:29); [49:29–32](https://ref.ly/Gen49:29-Gen49:32); [50:13](https://ref.ly/Gen50:13)). • Mamre was an Amorite ([14:13](https://ref.ly/Gen14:13); [15:16](https://ref.ly/Gen15:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 13:18

## Genesis 14:1 (id: 12327)

Tidal apparently ruled a number of city\-states (Goiim, literally *nations*).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:1

## Genesis 14:1–2 (id: 12319)

Archaeology has not identified these kings, but similar names from antiquity corroborate the report’s accuracy. The Mesopotamian kings were confederates under a suzerain, apparently Amraphel, who is mentioned first.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:1–GEN 14:2

## Genesis 14:1–16 (id: 12318)

In this skirmish typical of ancient politics, powerful kings formed a coalition to subjugate smaller vassal states.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:1–GEN 14:16

## Genesis 14:4 (id: 12331)

King Kedorlaomer apparently defeated the Siddim Valley kings at an earlier time and put them under tribute for asphalt, olive oil, and copper. In the thirteenth year they refused to send it; in the fourteenth year ([14:5](https://ref.ly/Gen14:5)), the invaders returned to subjugate them again.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:4

## Genesis 14:4–5 (id: 12330)

This was Chedorlaomer’s war. Under the feudal system of tribal affiliations, those in covenant with him had to fight. It was also Abram’s battle to rescue Lot, and those under treaty with him had to accompany him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:4–GEN 14:5

## Genesis 14:5–8 (id: 12332)

The invaders came down the King’s Highway on the east side of the Jordan Valley to the Gulf of Aqaba, then circled back to the valley of the Dead Sea.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:5–GEN 14:8

## Genesis 14:8–12 (id: 12333)

The five cities of the plain were close together at the south end of the Dead Sea. The Mesopotamian kings defeated the frail uprising, looted the cities of Sodom and Gomorrah, and carried off Lot with the other captives.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:8–GEN 14:12

## Genesis 14:13 (id: 12320)

The word Hebrew first occurs here in the Bible. It is not equivalent to the later term *Habiru* from Egyptian texts; the *Habiru* were mercenaries that roamed the land in the era of the judges. • Mamre: See study note on 13:18. • relatives: Or *allies;* literally *brothers.*

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:13
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 13:18 (ID: `12313@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 14:14 (id: 12322)

Abram . . . mobilized the 318 trained men who had been born into his household: Abram was a formidable force, an outworking of God’s promise to make him great ([12:2–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2-Gen12:3)). • Dan was a city about 150 miles north of Abram’s home in Hebron, then named Laish or Leshem (see [Josh 19:47](https://ref.ly/Josh19:47) and study note; [Judg 18:29](https://ref.ly/Judg18:29)). Dan, whose descendants migrated north in the days of the judges ([Judg 18:1–29](https://ref.ly/Judg18:1-Judg18:29)), had not yet been born ([Gen 30:6](https://ref.ly/Gen30:6)). An editor apparently updated the text so that later readers could identify this city.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:14
* **Associated Articles:** Joshua 19:47 (ID: `19454@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 14:14–16 (id: 12321)

God could give his people victory over any forces invading the Promised Land. Faithfulness to God was the prerequisite for victory. God promises to bless his people and give them victory over the world. He uses those who respond to his call and can skillfully use weapons of war (cp. [Eph 4:8](https://ref.ly/Eph4:8); [6:10–19](https://ref.ly/Eph6:10-Eph6:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:14–GEN 14:16

## Genesis 14:15 (id: 12323)

Damascus was 40 miles north of Dan. Hobah was about 60 miles north of Damascus.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:15

## Genesis 14:17 (id: 12324)

The valley of Shaveh or King’s Valley was probably the Kidron Valley (see [2 Sam 18:18](https://ref.ly/2Sam18:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:17

## Genesis 14:18 (id: 12325)

Melchizedek means “king of righteousness,” suggesting that he was a righteous servant of God. He was probably a Jebusite priest and king; later authors regarded him as a type of Christ ([Ps 110:4](https://ref.ly/Ps110:4); [Heb 7:1–19](https://ref.ly/Heb7:1-Heb7:19)). • Salem is the ancient name of Jerusalem (cp. [Ps 76:2](https://ref.ly/Ps76:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:18

## Genesis 14:19–20 (id: 12326)

By paying a tithe (a tenth) to Melchizedek, Abram acknowledged Melchizedek as a spiritual superior (see [Heb 7:4](https://ref.ly/Heb7:4)) and affirmed that God had given him victory.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:19–GEN 14:20

## Genesis 14:21–24 (id: 12328)

Abram knew that accepting the offer of the king of Sodom (see study note on 14:1–2) could make him his ally or subject, as Lot had been. This would jeopardize the fulfillment of God’s promises. Faith looks beyond the riches of the world to the greater blessings that God has in store.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:21–GEN 14:24
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 14:1-2 (ID: `12319@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 14:22 (id: 12329)

In the words of this oath, Abram may have been clarifying that his God, the Lord (Yahweh), was the God Most High that Melchizedek invoked. Perhaps Melchizedek had never heard the name Yahweh.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 14:22

## Genesis 15:1 (id: 12342)

Do not be afraid: Abram lacked a son to be his heir. The Lord addressed Abram’s anxiety about the future with comforting words. • I will protect you (literally *I will be your shield*): The Hebrew word for “shield” (magen) is from the same root as Melchizedek’s word *defeated* ([14:20](https://ref.ly/Gen14:20)). The Lord who had defeated Abram’s enemies would continue to protect him. • your reward will be great: The promise of offspring ([12:2–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2-Gen12:3); cp. [Ps 127:3](https://ref.ly/Ps127:3)) was still unfulfilled.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:1

## Genesis 15:1–21 (id: 12334)

God made a covenant (a special agreement) with Abram. In this covenant, God confirmed the promises he had made earlier when he summoned Abram to leave his country ([12:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:3)). Abram's descendants would experience a long period of slavery before God made these promises come true.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:1–GEN 15:21

## Genesis 15:2–3 (id: 12343)

Using a wordplay, Abram expressed his concern that Eliezer of Damascus (Hebrew dammeseq), a man in Abram’s household, would be his heir (Hebrew ben\-mesheq*,* “son of possession”), as was customary when there was no son.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:2–GEN 15:3

## Genesis 15:4–6 (id: 12344)

God affirmed that the promise was for Abram’s own offspring and showed him the stars as a promise of the vast number of descendants that he would have ([22:17](https://ref.ly/Gen22:17); [26:4](https://ref.ly/Gen26:4)). Paul quotes this promise in [Rom 4:18](https://ref.ly/Rom4:18) to underscore the strength of Abram’s faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:4–GEN 15:6

## Genesis 15:6 (id: 12345)

And Abram believed: God made his covenant with a believer; the statement does not indicate when Abram came to faith. The Hebrew text does not link Abram’s belief with the promise of the stars; it just says parenthetically that Abram believed God. Abram already had faith; his departure from Ur was his first great act that demonstrated it (see [Heb 11:8–10](https://ref.ly/Heb11:8-Heb11:10)). • God counted him as righteous because of his faith: This central statement about Abram’s saving faith is quoted three times in the New Testament ([Rom 4:3](https://ref.ly/Rom4:3); [Gal 3:6](https://ref.ly/Gal3:6); [Jas 2:23](https://ref.ly/Jas2:23)) to support the doctrine of righteousness before God by faith (see also [Rom 4:22–23](https://ref.ly/Rom4:22-Rom4:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:6

## Genesis 15:7–21 (id: 12346)

With a solemn ceremony, God made a binding covenant with Abram that guaranteed the fulfillment of God’s promises to him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:7–GEN 15:21

## Genesis 15:10 (id: 12335)

Obeying God’s instructions, Abram gathered three herd animals for the ceremony and cut them in half. Cutting the animals symbolized the oath, indicating that the covenant maker staked his own life on his word ([Jer 34:18](https://ref.ly/Jer34:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:10

## Genesis 15:11 (id: 12336)

Vultures are unclean birds of prey that symbolize those who unjustly attack Abraham’s heirs ([15:13–14](https://ref.ly/Gen15:13-Gen15:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:11

## Genesis 15:13 (id: 12338)

oppressed: The same word is used in [Exod 1:11–12](https://ref.ly/Exod1:11-Exod1:12). Egypt, like predatory birds ([Gen 15:11](https://ref.ly/Gen15:11)), would try to destroy Israel and hinder the covenant’s fulfillment. • Apparently 400 years is a round number (also [Acts 7:6](https://ref.ly/Acts7:6); cp. [Exod 12:40](https://ref.ly/Exod12:40); [Gal 3:17](https://ref.ly/Gal3:17)). Using the chronology in the Hebrew text, the family moved to Egypt around 1876 BC, and the Exodus occurred around 1446 BC (though many scholars date the Exodus later, around 1270 BC; see Exodus Book Introduction, “The Date of the Exodus”).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:13

## Genesis 15:13–16 (id: 12337)

Not even 400 years of bondage could interfere with God’s plan to fulfill the covenant.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:13–GEN 15:16

## Genesis 15:16 (id: 12339)

The reasons for Israel’s bondage included God’s justice. God would tolerate the sins of the Amorites until they fully deserved judgment. • do not yet warrant their destruction (literally *are not yet full*): To give the Promised Land to Israel, the Lord would dispossess the land’s inhabitants in a way that satisfied his justice. The fulfillment of promises to Israel also brought retributive judgment on people of the land (though individuals were saved by faith; see [Josh 2:1–15](https://ref.ly/Josh2:1-Josh2:15); [6:23–25](https://ref.ly/Josh6:23-Josh6:25); [Heb 11:31](https://ref.ly/Heb11:31); [Jas 2:25](https://ref.ly/Jas2:25)). Until then, God would send the family to Egypt where Israel could become a great nation. Seeing all this in advance was terrifying ([Gen 15:12](https://ref.ly/Gen15:12)), but it was comforting to know that nothing could interfere with God’s plan.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:16

## Genesis 15:17–18 (id: 12340)

smoking firepot . . . flaming torch: Fire represented the Lord’s cleansing, consuming zeal and unapproachable holiness (cp. [Isa 6:3–7](https://ref.ly/Isa6:3-Isa6:7)). The holy God made (literally *cut*) a unilateral covenant with Abram; its promises were absolutely sure because they did not depend on what Abram or his descendants might do.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:17–GEN 15:18

## Genesis 15:18–19 (id: 12341)

God specified the boundaries of the Promised Land. His clear message to Abram was that despite prospects of death and suffering (enslavement), he and his descendants would eventually receive the promises, for God had sworn an oath (see [Heb 6:13–14](https://ref.ly/Heb6:13-Heb6:14)). Nothing can separate God’s people from his love or the fulfillment of his plans (see [Rom 8:18–39](https://ref.ly/Rom8:18-Rom8:39); [2 Pet 1:3–4](https://ref.ly/2Pet1:3-2Pet1:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 15:18–GEN 15:19

## Genesis 16:1–3 (id: 12348)

Abram and Sarai faced the tension of her being barren and beyond childbearing years. By custom, a barren woman could give her servant to her husband as a slave\-wife; the child born to that union was considered the wife’s child and could be adopted as the heir. Sarai’s suggestion, unobjectionable by custom, set a problematic human plan in motion. God’s promises would be fulfilled by faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 16:1–GEN 16:3

## Genesis 16:1–16 (id: 12347)

While waiting for their promised son to be born, Abram and Sarai attempted an alternate plan that was not in keeping with faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 16:1–GEN 16:16

## Genesis 16:4–6 (id: 12353)

Perhaps Hagar expected to become the favored wife instead of Sarai (cp. [Prov 30:21–23](https://ref.ly/Prov30:21-Prov30:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 16:4–GEN 16:6

## Genesis 16:7 (id: 12354)

The angel of the Lord was the Lord himself ([16:13](https://ref.ly/Gen16:13); [21:17](https://ref.ly/Gen21:17); [22:11–12](https://ref.ly/Gen22:11-Gen22:12); [31:11–13](https://ref.ly/Gen31:11-Gen31:13); [48:16](https://ref.ly/Gen48:16); [Exod 3:2](https://ref.ly/Exod3:2); [32:34](https://ref.ly/Exod32:34); [Judg 6:11](https://ref.ly/Judg6:11), [16](https://ref.ly/Judg6:16), [22](https://ref.ly/Judg6:22); [13:22–23](https://ref.ly/Judg13:22-Judg13:23); [Zech 3:1–2](https://ref.ly/Zech3:1-Zech3:2)) but was also distinct from the Lord ([Gen 24:7](https://ref.ly/Gen24:7); [2 Sam 24:16](https://ref.ly/2Sam24:16); [Zech 1:12](https://ref.ly/Zech1:12)). The angel of the Lord was probably a *theophany* (a manifestation of God) or a *Christophany* (an appearance of the pre\-incarnate Messiah; see [Gen 18:1–2](https://ref.ly/Gen18:1-Gen18:2); [19:1](https://ref.ly/Gen19:1); [Num 22:22](https://ref.ly/Num22:22); [Judg 2:1–4](https://ref.ly/Judg2:1-Judg2:4); [5:23](https://ref.ly/Judg5:23); [Zech 12:8](https://ref.ly/Zech12:8)), speaking with the authority of the Lord himself.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 16:7

## Genesis 16:8–12 (id: 12355)

The angel’s rhetorical questions encouraged Hagar to pour out her heart to God. When she did, God commanded her to return and submit ([16:9](https://ref.ly/Gen16:9)), promising that her son would have innumerable descendants. The angel of the Lord never referred to Hagar as Abram’s wife, only as Sarai’s servant. She would have Abram’s child, but Ishmael was not central to God’s covenant with Abram.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 16:8–GEN 16:12

## Genesis 16:10–12 (id: 12349)

Hagar’s son would become the father of a great but wild and hostile nation living in the Arabian Desert as perennial enemies of Israel (cp. [25:18](https://ref.ly/Gen25:18)). God blessed Ishmael as Abram’s descendant, but not as the line chosen to carry on the covenant. That blessing was reserved for Abram’s chosen heir.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 16:10–GEN 16:12

## Genesis 16:11 (id: 12350)

Names in Genesis often capture the message of a passage and aid the remembrance of the events and their significance in the history of the faith. The name Ishmael, which means “God hears,” commemorates that the Lord . . . heard Hagar’s cry of distress (see also study note on 16:14–15). This name would have greatly comforted Hagar; God listened to her prayers and acknowledged her complaint.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 16:11
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 16:14-15 (ID: `12352@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 16:13 (id: 12351)

Hagar responded to God’s messages by faith, in her words and in her obedience. • the God who sees me: God knew Hagar’s plight and watched over her.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 16:13

## Genesis 16:14–15 (id: 12352)

The names Beer\-lahai\-roi, which means “well of the Living One who sees me,” and Ishmael (see [16:11](https://ref.ly/Gen16:11)) were a message and a rebuke for Abram and Sarai. God sees affliction and hears the cries of those in need. Sarai and Abram should have prayed rather than taking the fulfillment of the promise into their own hands by following social custom (cp. [25:21](https://ref.ly/Gen25:21)). Giving children to the barren woman is God’s work ([Ps 113:9](https://ref.ly/Ps113:9); cp. [1 Sam 1:1–28](https://ref.ly/1Sam1:1-1Sam1:28); [Luke 1:1–25](https://ref.ly/Luke1:1-Luke1:25)); impossible difficulties cannot be resolved by human intervention. The Lord hears the afflicted, sees them in their need, and will miraculously provide for them.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 16:14–GEN 16:15

## Genesis 17:1 (id: 12361)

El\-Shaddai: This name for God emphasizes his power (see also [28:3](https://ref.ly/Gen28:3); [35:11](https://ref.ly/Gen35:11); [43:14](https://ref.ly/Gen43:14); [48:3](https://ref.ly/Gen48:3); [49:25](https://ref.ly/Gen49:25)). • Serve me faithfully and live a blameless life: Being a blessing to the nations required obedience from Abram; his conduct would be guided by Almighty God.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:1

## Genesis 17:1–27 (id: 12356)

God now gave the family signs that the promises would be fulfilled. He changed Abram’s name to Abraham ([17:1–8](https://ref.ly/Gen17:1-Gen17:8)), instituted the rite of circumcision as the sign of the covenant ([17:9–14](https://ref.ly/Gen17:9-Gen17:14), [23–27](https://ref.ly/Gen17:23-Gen17:27)), and changed Sarai’s name to Sarah ([17:15–22](https://ref.ly/Gen17:15-Gen17:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:1–GEN 17:27

## Genesis 17:4–5 (id: 12364)

God guaranteed his promise by changing Abram’s name. Abram referred to his noble lineage, as Terah was the “exalted father” ([11:27](https://ref.ly/Gen11:27)). His new name, Abraham (Hebrew ’ab hamon) was a wordplay on the promise of his own progeny (see also [John 8:31–59](https://ref.ly/John8:31-John8:59); [Rom 4:16–17](https://ref.ly/Rom4:16-Rom4:17); [Gal 3:7](https://ref.ly/Gal3:7), [15–19](https://ref.ly/Gal3:15-Gal3:19), [29](https://ref.ly/Gal3:29)). Whenever the new name was used, he and his household would remember that a multitude of nations would issue from him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:4–GEN 17:5

## Genesis 17:6 (id: 12365)

kings will be among them! This is the first indication that Israel would become a monarchy (see also [35:11](https://ref.ly/Gen35:11); [36:31](https://ref.ly/Gen36:31); [Num 24:7](https://ref.ly/Num24:7); [Deut 17:14–18](https://ref.ly/Deut17:14-Deut17:18); [28:36](https://ref.ly/Deut28:36)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:6

## Genesis 17:7–8 (id: 12366)

The land of Canaan was to be an everlasting possession for the descendants of Abraham; the Lord would be their God forever (see [Jer 31:31–40](https://ref.ly/Jer31:31-Jer31:40); [Zech 8:8](https://ref.ly/Zech8:8); [Luke 1:68–79](https://ref.ly/Luke1:68-Luke1:79); [Rev 21:1–4](https://ref.ly/Rev21:1-Rev21:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:7–GEN 17:8

## Genesis 17:9–14 (id: 12367)

God gave circumcision as a confirming sign that reminded all households of loyalty to the covenant.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:9–GEN 17:14

## Genesis 17:14 (id: 12357)

will be cut off: This punishment seems to have several applications. A person could be exiled from society or put to death by the community; most often it warned that a person might die prematurely as God cut him off from the land of the living (see [Exod 31:14](https://ref.ly/Exod31:14); [Lev 7:20–27](https://ref.ly/Lev7:20-Lev7:27); [17:3–4](https://ref.ly/Lev17:3-Lev17:4); [20:17–18](https://ref.ly/Lev20:17-Lev20:18); [23:28–29](https://ref.ly/Lev23:28-Lev23:29); [Num 15:30–31](https://ref.ly/Num15:30-Num15:31); see also [Ps 31:22](https://ref.ly/Ps31:22); [Ezek 21:4](https://ref.ly/Ezek21:4); [Rom 9:3](https://ref.ly/Rom9:3); [11:22](https://ref.ly/Rom11:22)). Failure to be circumcised was a serious violation (see [Exod 4:24–26](https://ref.ly/Exod4:24-Exod4:26); cp. [Gal 5:2–4](https://ref.ly/Gal5:2-Gal5:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:14

## Genesis 17:15–16 (id: 12358)

Sarah: The new name, fitting for one who would be the mother of kings, was a milestone in Sarah’s calling and brought attention to the promise.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:15–GEN 17:16

## Genesis 17:17–18 (id: 12359)

Abraham laughed (Hebrew yitskhaq) because the promise seemed unbelievable; he had begun to believe that his line would come through Ishmael. But Abraham and Sarah would have a son of their own.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:17–GEN 17:18

## Genesis 17:19 (id: 12360)

The name Isaac (Hebrew yitskhaq, “he laughs”) would constantly recall Abraham’s disbelieving laughter when he heard the promise. It was also a reminder of God’s favor and his pleasure in the birth (cp. [21:6](https://ref.ly/Gen21:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:19

## Genesis 17:20–21 (id: 12362)

Ishmael would not be abandoned; his family would prosper (see [25:13–16](https://ref.ly/Gen25:13-Gen25:16)), but the covenant promises were for Isaac.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:20–GEN 17:21

## Genesis 17:23–27 (id: 12363)

Having received God’s word about Isaac, Abraham immediately complied with God’s instructions. He implemented the rite of circumcision as an act of faith; it signified their participation in the covenant (cp. [Rom 4:11–12](https://ref.ly/Rom4:11-Rom4:12); [Gal 5:2–6](https://ref.ly/Gal5:2-Gal5:6), [11](https://ref.ly/Gal5:11); [6:15](https://ref.ly/Gal6:15); [Phil 3:2–3](https://ref.ly/Phil3:2-Phil3:3); [Col 2:11–12](https://ref.ly/Col2:11-Col2:12); [1 Pet 3:21](https://ref.ly/1Pet3:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 17:23–GEN 17:27

## Genesis 18:1–15 (id: 12368)

The Lord’s visit to Abraham set the time for Isaac’s birth. The three visitors were probably the Lord and two angels (see study note on 16:7). Abraham’s peaceful and generous reception of the visitors contrasts sharply with the chaos and corruption of Sodom (ch [19](https://ref.ly/Gen19:1-Gen19:38)). Eating together was important in making or confirming covenants; when God was ready to fulfill the covenant promise, he came in person to share a meal with Abraham. Fellowship with God has always been signified by a communal meal (see [Exod 24:9–11](https://ref.ly/Exod24:9-Exod24:11); [Matt 26:17–30](https://ref.ly/Matt26:17-Matt26:30) *//* [Luke 22:7–38](https://ref.ly/Luke22:7-Luke22:38); [Acts 2:42](https://ref.ly/Acts2:42); [1 Cor 11:20–34](https://ref.ly/1Cor11:20-1Cor11:34)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:1–GEN 18:15
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 16:7 (ID: `12354@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 18:2–8 (id: 12374)

Abraham received his visitors as very important guests, perhaps realizing that they were messengers from God.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:2–GEN 18:8

## Genesis 18:3 (id: 12378)

*My lord:* The Hebrew text uses *’adonay*, which always refers to God. In Hebrew tradition, people said this word when they saw the letters of God's holy name ("Yahweh") in the text. The text here might use *’adonay* instead of the more common *’adoni* to show this was the angel of the Lord, meaning the Lord himself (see study note on 16:7). We do not know if Abraham knew who his visitors were at first. But by the end of the story, he knew he had been talking with God.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:3
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 16:7 (ID: `12354@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 18:9 (id: 12379)

The visitors asked about Sarah by name, even though no one had told them who she was.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:9

## Genesis 18:10 (id: 12369)

*I will surely return:* The Hebrew phrase is emphatic. The statement announced a certain promise and dramatic change.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:10

## Genesis 18:13–15 (id: 12370)

Sarah thought her disbelieving laughter was hidden, but God knows human hearts (see [Ps 69:5](https://ref.ly/Ps69:5); [Prov 20:27](https://ref.ly/Prov20:27); [Mark 4:22](https://ref.ly/Mark4:22); [Luke 8:43–48](https://ref.ly/Luke8:43-Luke8:48); [Heb 4:13](https://ref.ly/Heb4:13)), whether they stagger at the promises or step out in faith (see [Heb 11:11–12](https://ref.ly/Heb11:11-Heb11:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:13–GEN 18:15

## Genesis 18:14 (id: 12371)

Is anything too hard for the Lord? The question is rhetorical. God is able to do marvelous things. Nothing is incredible to those in covenant fellowship with the Lord, because nothing is too difficult for him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:14

## Genesis 18:16–33 (id: 12372)

God took Abraham into his confidence as his prophet ([18:16–21](https://ref.ly/Gen18:16-Gen18:21); see [20:7](https://ref.ly/Gen20:7)); Abraham, in turn, interceded for Sodom ([18:23–32](https://ref.ly/Gen18:23-Gen18:32); see [Heb 7:23–26](https://ref.ly/Heb7:23-Heb7:26)). God is able to do whatever he chooses to do; this passage affirms that it will be just and right.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:16–GEN 18:33

## Genesis 18:17–19 (id: 12373)

Abraham was responsible for teaching his descendants righteousness and justice so that they might enjoy God’s blessings. It was important for Abraham to know how God’s righteousness was at work in judgment.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:17–GEN 18:19

## Genesis 18:20 (id: 12376)

a great outcry: See [Ezek 16:49–50](https://ref.ly/Ezek16:49-Ezek16:50).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:20

## Genesis 18:20–21 (id: 12375)

The omniscient God was cautious in his judgment: He knew the sins of Sodom and Gomorrah, but this close scrutiny communicated God’s justice in human terms—he would not destroy the people of the plain unless he was absolutely sure they were wicked.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:20–GEN 18:21

## Genesis 18:22–33 (id: 12377)

Abraham probably thought there were more righteous people in Sodom and Gomorrah than there were (see study note on 19:14). In his concern for them, he approached the Lord with a legal appeal based on God’s justice. His prayer seems too bold at times, as though he were bargaining with God, but he approached God with genuine humility and reverence. He did not try to talk God into doing something against his will, but prayed for the well\-being of others (contrast Lot’s prayer, [19:18–23](https://ref.ly/Gen19:18-Gen19:23)). God is a righteous judge; righteousness exalts a nation ([Prov 14:34](https://ref.ly/Prov14:34)), and righteous people help to preserve society (cp. [Matt 5:13](https://ref.ly/Matt5:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 18:22–GEN 18:33
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 19:14 (ID: `12382@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 19:1 (id: 12385)

The two angels also first appeared to Abraham as men ([18:2](https://ref.ly/Gen18:2)). • Lot was no longer living in tents next to Sodom ([13:12](https://ref.ly/Gen13:12))—he had become a citizen and leader in Sodom, sitting there at the entrance of the city. Community leaders (elders) usually congregated in the gates, where legal and business transactions were publicly finalized (cp. [23:18](https://ref.ly/Gen23:18); [Job 29:7](https://ref.ly/Job29:7), [12–17](https://ref.ly/Job29:12-Job29:17)). As a righteous man ([2 Pet 2:7–8](https://ref.ly/2Pet2:7-2Pet2:8)), Lot tried to modify the townspeople’s wickedness by giving advice on good living (cp. [Gen 19:9](https://ref.ly/Gen19:9)). Although he denounced gross evil, Lot preferred Sodom’s sumptuous lifestyle to life in the hills (cp. [13:10–11](https://ref.ly/Gen13:10-Gen13:11)), where there was clean living but no “good life.” As long as the Lord left Lot and his family alone in Sodom, he lived comfortably there and kept his personal belief in God; but finally, he could not hold to both. Sodom would have destroyed Lot if the Lord had not destroyed Sodom. • This account showed Israel that God is the righteous judge of the whole earth ([18:25](https://ref.ly/Gen18:25)) who will judge evildoers with justice and equity. In wicked societies, moral and ethical failures lead to social injustice.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:1

## Genesis 19:1–14 (id: 12380)

The two angels who were with the Lord at Mamre (cp. [18:2](https://ref.ly/Gen18:2), [22](https://ref.ly/Gen18:22)) visited Sodom reluctantly, knowing what kind of people lived there. Despite Lot’s hospitality, they preferred lodging in the square to entering Lot’s house.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:1–GEN 19:14

## Genesis 19:1–38 (id: 12381)

The Canaanites were an evil, corrupting people. God judged their morally bankrupt civilization and warned others against becoming like them. It was difficult to get Lot and his family out of Sodom; it was more difficult to get Sodom out of Lot and his family. This chapter helped later Israelites to understand the moral and spiritual threat of the peoples living in and around the Promised Land, such as the Canaanites and Lot’s descendants, the Moabites and the Ammonites (see [Num 22–25](https://ref.ly/Num22:1-Num25:18); [Deut 23:3–6](https://ref.ly/Deut23:3-Deut23:6); [Josh 24:9](https://ref.ly/Josh24:9); [Judg 10:7–9](https://ref.ly/Judg10:7-Judg10:9); [11:4–5](https://ref.ly/Judg11:4-Judg11:5); [1 Sam 10:27](https://ref.ly/1Sam10:27); [1 Kgs 11:1–3](https://ref.ly/1Kgs11:1-1Kgs11:3); [2 Kgs 24:2](https://ref.ly/2Kgs24:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:1–GEN 19:38

## Genesis 19:4–5 (id: 12392)

The townsmen’s vileness was matched by Lot’s hypocrisy ([19:6–9](https://ref.ly/Gen19:6-Gen19:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:4–GEN 19:5

## Genesis 19:6–9 (id: 12393)

Lot opposed homosexuality and rape and rebuked their wicked plans, but he was hypocritically willing to sacrifice his daughters to fend off the townsmen’s vice. Lot had originally pitched his tent next to Sodom; now Sodom controlled his life.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:6–GEN 19:9

## Genesis 19:9 (id: 12394)

The men of the city were enraged by Lot’s attempts to curtail their wickedness. Lot had apparently not condemned them before, since they were amazed that he now judged them.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:9

## Genesis 19:14 (id: 12382)

Lot’s warning words were not taken seriously because of his hypocrisy. It seemed that there would not be even ten righteous people in the city.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:14

## Genesis 19:15–23 (id: 12383)

* Lot escaped God's judgment because of God's grace, but Lot was still hesitant to leave Sodom. Israel would forever remember Lot as someone who hesitated and needed angels to pull him to safety. The Lord was merciful and spared Lot because of Abraham ([18:23](https://ref.ly/Gen18:23); [19:29](https://ref.ly/Gen19:29)).
* Today, people who follow God often feel pressure to copy the wrong ways that many people live. God asks his followers to live differently from people who do not follow him. This means Christians should choose actions and behaviors that show they belong to God ([1 John 2:15–17](https://ref.ly/1John2:15-1John2:17)). God will judge the corrupt systems of the world. This judgment will be even more severe than the destruction of Sodom and Gomorrah ([Matthew 11:23–24](https://ref.ly/Matt11:23-Matt11:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:15–GEN 19:23

## Genesis 19:18–22 (id: 12384)

Lot asked the angels for permission to live in the small town of Zoar (which means "little place") even after they saved him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:18–GEN 19:22

## Genesis 19:23–25 (id: 12386)

Cp. [Luke 17:29](https://ref.ly/Luke17:29). The eruption of Vesuvius and the destruction of Pompeii in AD 79, as well as recent natural disasters, show how quickly a thorough catastrophe like this could happen.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:23–GEN 19:25

## Genesis 19:26 (id: 12387)

looked back: The verb indicates prolonged, intense gazing toward the world she loved, not a curious glance ([15:5](https://ref.ly/Gen15:5); [Exod 33:8](https://ref.ly/Exod33:8); [Num 21:9](https://ref.ly/Num21:9); [1 Sam 2:32](https://ref.ly/1Sam2:32); cp. [Exod 3:6](https://ref.ly/Exod3:6)). Lot’s wife was too attached to Sodom to follow God’s call of grace, so she was included in the judgment as she lingered on the valley slopes. Christ’s return to judge the world will be as sudden and devastating as the destruction of Sodom ([Luke 17:32–37](https://ref.ly/Luke17:32-Luke17:37)). Those who crave the life of this wicked world will lose this world and the next.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:26

## Genesis 19:29 (id: 12388)

God listened to Abraham's plea (compare [18:23–32](https://ref.ly/Gen18:23-Gen18:32)). But Lot lost everything during the destruction of Sodom. His choice to live in the fertile land of Sodom eventually led him into a dangerous situation ([13:10–13](https://ref.ly/Gen13:10-Gen13:13)). After this, he could no longer remain in the land he had chosen.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:29

## Genesis 19:30–35 (id: 12389)

The character of Lot’s daughters was formed by Sodom’s culture more than by their father’s heritage, so they had no qualms about having children by their drunk father (cp. [9:21–22](https://ref.ly/Gen9:21-Gen9:22)). They saw no other way to carry on their line.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:30–GEN 19:35

## Genesis 19:30–38 (id: 12390)

The poverty of the cave contrasts with the wealth Lot shared with Abram and the good life he lived in Sodom. Abraham would father a righteous nation ([17:1](https://ref.ly/Gen17:1)), but Lot and his daughters gave birth to a new Sodom.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:30–GEN 19:38

## Genesis 19:36–38 (id: 12391)

The daughters’ plan worked, and they each became pregnant by their own father. From these two incest\-born sons came two perennial enemies of Israel, the nations of Moab and Ammon. Their grotesque wickedness was due in part to their origin. • Both daughters chose ambiguous names that hinted at their actions without raising the suspicions of those who did not know the stories.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 19:36–GEN 19:38

## Genesis 20:1 (id: 12423)

Gerar was near the coast in Philistine land, about twelve miles south of Gaza and fifty miles southwest of Hebron.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:1

## Genesis 20:1–18 (id: 12419)

This second “sister story” in Genesis (cp. [12:10–20](https://ref.ly/Gen12:10-Gen12:20)) occurred shortly before Sarah became pregnant with Isaac (ch [21](https://ref.ly/Gen21:1-Gen21:34)). On both occasions, God protected Abraham and Sarah’s marriage in purity for the sake of the covenant promises. Participation in God’s plan requires separation from worldly corruption. • This story took place in the Promised Land; it showed Israel how God intervened in people’s lives to fulfill his plan, how God continued to protect them against threats from other tribes, and how God used his chosen people to mediate his relationship with the nations. • God’s preventing the destruction of Abraham’s marriage by adultery reminded the Israelites to keep their marriages morally and racially pure ([Ezra 9:1–4](https://ref.ly/Ezra9:1-Ezra9:4); [Neh 13:23–27](https://ref.ly/Neh13:23-Neh13:27); [Mal 2:10–17](https://ref.ly/Mal2:10-Mal2:17)); they should not allow any opportunity for temptation ([Exod 20:14](https://ref.ly/Exod20:14), [17](https://ref.ly/Exod20:17); [Lev 20:10](https://ref.ly/Lev20:10); [21:13–15](https://ref.ly/Lev21:13-Lev21:15)). Adultery would eventually destroy the covenant and the covenant people.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:1–GEN 20:18

## Genesis 20:2 (id: 12424)

Abraham told the same lie to Abimelech that he had told to Pharaoh ([12:13](https://ref.ly/Gen12:13)); Isaac would later do the same ([26:1–11](https://ref.ly/Gen26:1-Gen26:11)), probably having learned this tactic from his father. • Abimelech (literally *my father the king*) was probably a title like “Pharaoh” ([37:36](https://ref.ly/Gen37:36); [Exod 1:15](https://ref.ly/Exod1:15)), not a proper name (see study note on Gen 26:1).
