* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:2
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 26:1 (ID: `12533@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 20:3 (id: 12426)

that night God came . . . in a dream: God urgently intervened to stop Abimelech from violating Sarah’s purity shortly before God’s promise was fulfilled ([18:10](https://ref.ly/Gen18:10); [21:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen21:1-Gen21:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:3

## Genesis 20:3–7 (id: 12425)

God gave Abimelech a stern warning against committing adultery; it was a capital offense (cp. [Exod 20:14](https://ref.ly/Exod20:14)), viewed throughout the ancient Near East as a “great sin” ([Gen 20:9](https://ref.ly/Gen20:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:3–GEN 20:7

## Genesis 20:4–5 (id: 12427)

Abimelech was innocent ([20:6](https://ref.ly/Gen20:6)); his conscience was clear. Nonetheless, he was about to commit adultery, and ignorance does not excuse guilt ([Lev 4:13–14](https://ref.ly/Lev4:13-Lev4:14)). • will you destroy an innocent nation? Abimelech’s appeal to God echoes Abraham’s earlier words (cp. [18:23–32](https://ref.ly/Gen18:23-Gen18:32)) and rebukes Abraham’s lack of faith on this occasion.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:4–GEN 20:5

## Genesis 20:6 (id: 12428)

Because Abimelech acted with a clear conscience, God kept him from sinning. God will graciously help those who try to do what is right. When people act with reverence toward God, God gives them more revelation and draws them into more specific faith (see [Acts 10](https://ref.ly/Acts10:1-Acts10:48)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:6

## Genesis 20:7 (id: 12429)

Abraham's prayer saved the king's life and restored his family ([20:17–18](https://ref.ly/Gen20:17-Gen20:18)). Abimelech learned that Abraham's God was sovereign (meaning God has complete power and authority over everything). Abraham, as God's prophet, received God's messages and would pray for others (see [Numbers 12:13](https://ref.ly/Num12:13); [Deuteronomy 9:20](https://ref.ly/Deut9:20)). This was true even if he did not always act like a prophet.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:7

## Genesis 20:8–10 (id: 12430)

Abraham had earned rebukes from Abimelech and from God (cp. [12:17–19](https://ref.ly/Gen12:17-Gen12:19)). Abimelech was angry that Abraham’s deception had made him guilty of this great sin (see study note on 20:3–7). He knew that taking a married woman into his harem was wrong.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:8–GEN 20:10
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 20:3-7 (ID: `12425@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 20:11–13 (id: 12420)

Abraham’s duplicity was not a momentary loss of faith. Despite the rebuke he received in Egypt, he practiced this strategy wherever he went (cp. [12:12–13](https://ref.ly/Gen12:12-Gen12:13)). Living by faith requires perseverance.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:11–GEN 20:13

## Genesis 20:14–16 (id: 12421)

Abimelech showed he was an honorable person by making things right (see study note on 20:4–5). He made things right by letting Abraham live in the area. He also gave him slaves, livestock (compare [21:27](https://ref.ly/Gen21:27)), and 1,000 pieces of silver to make up for any wrong done to Sarah.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:14–GEN 20:16
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 20:4-5 (ID: `12427@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 20:17–18 (id: 12422)

The infertility suggests that some time had passed. God controls births; he opens and closes wombs ([25:21](https://ref.ly/Gen25:21); [29:31](https://ref.ly/Gen29:31); [30:2](https://ref.ly/Gen30:2), [17](https://ref.ly/Gen30:17), [22–23](https://ref.ly/Gen30:22-Gen30:23); [1 Sam 1:19–20](https://ref.ly/1Sam1:19-1Sam1:20); [Pss 113:9](https://ref.ly/Ps113:9); [127:3](https://ref.ly/Ps127:3); [Luke 1:13](https://ref.ly/Luke1:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 20:17–GEN 20:18

## Genesis 21:1–2 (id: 12431)

See [Genesis 18:10](https://ref.ly/Gen18:10).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:1–GEN 21:2

## Genesis 21:3–4 (id: 12440)

Abraham responded in faith by naming his son Isaac and circumcising him according to the terms of the covenant (see [17:9–14](https://ref.ly/Gen17:9-Gen17:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:3–GEN 21:4

## Genesis 21:5 (id: 12443)

Isaac was born twenty\-five years after the promise was first given (cp. [12:4](https://ref.ly/Gen12:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:5

## Genesis 21:6 (id: 12445)

Sarah’s wordplay on the name *Isaac* (Hebrew yitskhaq, “he laughs”) shows that the laughter of unbelief when the promise was given ([18:12](https://ref.ly/Gen18:12)) had changed to the laughter of joy at its fulfillment. Isaac’s name could refer to the pleasure of God and of his parents at his birth. Sarah knew that everyone who heard about this would laugh with her and rejoice at the news.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:6

## Genesis 21:6–7 (id: 12444)

Sarah was filled with joy and praise for this amazing event—only God could enable her to have a child.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:6–GEN 21:7

## Genesis 21:8–9 (id: 12447)

The feast for Isaac’s weaning probably occurred when he was three and Ishmael was about seventeen years old ([16:16](https://ref.ly/Gen16:16)). Sarah saw Ishmael making fun of her son, Isaac: The verb metsakheq (“making fun of”) is related to the word for “laughter”; this theme ([21:6](https://ref.ly/Gen21:6)) is given a sour twist by Ishmael’s mockery.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:8–GEN 21:9

## Genesis 21:8–21 (id: 12446)

God used the incident of Ishmael’s mocking Isaac to separate Ishmael and Hagar from the family and the child of promise. They would constantly threaten the promised descendant if they remained with the family.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:8–GEN 21:21

## Genesis 21:10 (id: 12432)

Earlier, Sarah mistreated Hagar and pressured her to flee ([16:6](https://ref.ly/Gen16:6)); when Hagar’s son mistreated Isaac, Sarah demanded that that slave woman and her son leave.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:10

## Genesis 21:11–13 (id: 12433)

Abraham was upset by Sarah’s demand to oust Hagar and Ishmael. God told him to comply, assuring Abraham that Ishmael would also have a future as Abraham’s offspring.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:11–GEN 21:13

## Genesis 21:14–21 (id: 12434)

God again rescued Hagar in the wilderness and guaranteed her future (cp. [16:7–14](https://ref.ly/Gen16:7-Gen16:14)). This passage is similar to ch [16](https://ref.ly/Gen16:1-Gen16:16), but the differences are great. Here, Hagar and Ishmael are rescued, but there is no commemorative naming. God’s earlier promise to Hagar is reiterated, but this time Hagar is not told to return to Sarah. The repeated motifs on the two occasions confirm God’s sovereign plan for Hagar and Ishmael. As Joseph later told Pharaoh, a twofold event demonstrated God’s confirmation ([41:32](https://ref.ly/Gen41:32)). God did not abandon Hagar and Ishmael but met them in their despair (cp. [16:7](https://ref.ly/Gen16:7)), provided sustenance for them, and promised again that Ishmael would found a great nation ([21:13](https://ref.ly/Gen21:13); cp. [16:11–12](https://ref.ly/Gen16:11-Gen16:12)). Paul uses this event in his letter to the Galatians to illustrate how God’s people must relinquish all that threatens the fulfillment of God’s promise ([Gal 4:21–31](https://ref.ly/Gal4:21-Gal4:31)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:14–GEN 21:21

## Genesis 21:16 (id: 12435)

a hundred yards (literally *a bowshot*): This description connects with Ishmael’s vocation ([21:20](https://ref.ly/Gen21:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:16

## Genesis 21:22–23 (id: 12436)

Abimelech pressed for the treaty so that Abraham would not cheat or deceive him. Abimelech knew that God was blessing Abraham even though Abraham was not entirely trustworthy ([20:9–10](https://ref.ly/Gen20:9-Gen20:10)). This sad contradiction made the treaty necessary. By contrast, God’s faithful people are exhorted to speak the truth ([Eph 4:15](https://ref.ly/Eph4:15), [25](https://ref.ly/Eph4:25)), and Jesus warned against manipulating truth by the clever use of oaths ([Matt 5:37](https://ref.ly/Matt5:37); [Jas 5:12](https://ref.ly/Jas5:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:22–GEN 21:23

## Genesis 21:22–34 (id: 12437)

This passage, at its climax, explains the name of Beersheba, Abraham’s home ([21:31–34](https://ref.ly/Gen21:31-Gen21:34)). Beersheba reflected the covenant Abraham made with the residents of the land, which enabled him to dwell there in peace and prosperity. God’s promise was coming to fruition ([12:7](https://ref.ly/Gen12:7); [13:14–17](https://ref.ly/Gen13:14-Gen13:17); [15:7](https://ref.ly/Gen15:7), [18–21](https://ref.ly/Gen15:18-Gen15:21); [17:8](https://ref.ly/Gen17:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:22–GEN 21:34

## Genesis 21:25 (id: 12438)

The motif of the well appears again (cp. [16:14](https://ref.ly/Gen16:14); [21:19](https://ref.ly/Gen21:19)). God provided water (a symbol of blessing) in the barren wilderness and later even brought water out of a rock for Israel ([Exod 15:22–27](https://ref.ly/Exod15:22-Exod15:27); [17:1–7](https://ref.ly/Exod17:1-Exod17:7); [Num 20:1–13](https://ref.ly/Num20:1-Num20:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:25

## Genesis 21:27–31 (id: 12439)

Abraham’s gifts to Abimelech (cp. [20:14](https://ref.ly/Gen20:14)) secured his legal right to dwell peaceably in the land and to claim ownership of the well. Beersheba marked one more step toward the fulfillment of God’s promise.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:27–GEN 21:31

## Genesis 21:32 (id: 12441)

The Philistines in Genesis are different from the Philistines of Judges through Kings. The earlier Philistines had Semitic names (e.g., Abimelech) and Canaanite culture. The later Philistines were apparently of Greek origin, with Greek customs and culture. They seem to have arrived in Canaan by sea from the Aegean area around 1200 BC, during the time of the judges. Probably the name of the later Philistines was used here simply to describe the region’s earlier inhabitants.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:32

## Genesis 21:33–34 (id: 12442)

A tamarisk tree requires a lot of water; this act indicated Abraham’s security in his land rights and his faith that God would provide water in this desert area. He settled as a foreigner in the land, but dwelling under his tree was a sign of peaceful security (cp. [Zech 3:10](https://ref.ly/Zech3:10)). • there he worshiped the Lord: See study note on 12:8.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 21:33–GEN 21:34
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 12:8 (ID: `12306@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 22:1 (id: 12457)

Some time later: Abraham had sent Ishmael away and settled in the land. Now God tested Abraham’s faith by telling him to give up Isaac. This pushed the limits of logic and of Abraham’s knowledge of God. Would he still obey when God seemed to be working against him and against the covenant? Would he cling to the boy or surrender him to God (see [Exod 13:11–13](https://ref.ly/Exod13:11-Exod13:13))? Did he believe that God would still keep his word and bless the world through Abraham’s offspring?

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:1

## Genesis 22:1–2 (id: 12448)

The greatest test in Abraham’s life came after he had received the promised child following a long wait. He had grown to love Isaac and had enjoyed his presence for a number of years.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:1–GEN 22:2

## Genesis 22:2 (id: 12462)

Obedience to God’s earlier call ([12:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:3)) was rewarded with great blessing; now Abraham had the opportunity to show even greater obedience. • Take your son . . . Isaac, whom you love so much: By this detailed description of Isaac, God reminded Abraham that the young man was his beloved son, and intensified his awareness of the cost of the sacrifice. • The name Moriah is explained by the Chronicler ([2 Chr 3:1](https://ref.ly/2Chr3:1)) as the place of the later Temple Mount in Jerusalem. • Go: By wording the command in this way, God helped Abraham to obey by recalling his former call (cp. [12:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:2

## Genesis 22:3 (id: 12463)

Abraham’s immediate, unquestioning obedience is almost as astounding as the test.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:3

## Genesis 22:5 (id: 12464)

We will worship there, and then we will come (or *We will worship there so that we may come back*): Abraham’s amazing statement makes us wonder what he was thinking. Abraham knew that God had planned the future of the covenant around Isaac and that God wanted him to sacrifice Isaac. He could not reconcile these things in his mind; he could only do what God commanded him to do, and leave the future to God (cp. [Heb 11:17–19](https://ref.ly/Heb11:17-Heb11:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:5

## Genesis 22:7–8 (id: 12465)

In response to Isaac’s question, Abraham again showed his faith in the Lord, saying God will provide*,* although he was not sure how. This theme is central to the entire narrative.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:7–GEN 22:8

## Genesis 22:9–19 (id: 12466)

God’s intervention was dramatic and instructive, confirming that he never intended for Isaac to be sacrificed. God later made it clear that child sacrifice was an abomination to him (see [Lev 18:21](https://ref.ly/Lev18:21); [20:1–5](https://ref.ly/Lev20:1-Lev20:5); [Deut 18:10](https://ref.ly/Deut18:10); [2 Kgs 16:2–3](https://ref.ly/2Kgs16:2-2Kgs16:3); [Isa 57:5](https://ref.ly/Isa57:5); [Jer 32:35](https://ref.ly/Jer32:35)). God wanted Abraham to sacrifice his own will and surrender it to God, and when he did, God intervened. This passage sets a pattern for all sacrificial worshipers. Like Abraham, true worshipers of God know that everything belongs to God—it all came from God and must therefore be acknowledged as God’s possession. A true worshiper holds nothing back but obediently gives God what he asks, trusting that God will provide for all needs, and then discovering through experience that God always does so.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:9–GEN 22:19

## Genesis 22:11 (id: 12449)

The angel of the Lord stopped Abraham just as he was ready to plunge the knife into his son.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:11

## Genesis 22:12 (id: 12450)

Now God knew that Abraham would hold nothing back from him, that he did truly fear God. To fear the Lord means to reverence him as sovereign, trust him implicitly, and obey him without protest. The sacrifice that pleases God is a heart broken of self\-will, surrendered to God ([Pss 40:6–8](https://ref.ly/Ps40:6-Ps40:8); [51:17](https://ref.ly/Ps51:17)) and offering its best to God. • You have not withheld: Cp. [Rom 8:32](https://ref.ly/Rom8:32), which uses the same verb (“spare”) as the Greek Old Testament uses here. If God gave us his dearest possession, he will surely provide all things for us.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:12

## Genesis 22:13 (id: 12451)

God provided a ram caught by its horns in a thicket for the sacrifice. God graciously allowed Abraham to substitute an animal sacrifice in place of Isaac. Later, all Israel would offer animals to the Lord, knowing that God’s grace had provided this substitution ([Exod 29:10](https://ref.ly/Exod29:10); [Lev 4:15](https://ref.ly/Lev4:15); [16:20–22](https://ref.ly/Lev16:20-Lev16:22)). In the New Testament, God substituted his only son for all humanity; the perfect sacrifice was made once and for all ([Isa 53:6](https://ref.ly/Isa53:6), [10](https://ref.ly/Isa53:10); [John 1:29](https://ref.ly/John1:29); [Heb 7:27](https://ref.ly/Heb7:27); [10:1–14](https://ref.ly/Heb10:1-Heb10:14); [1 Pet 3:18](https://ref.ly/1Pet3:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:13

## Genesis 22:14 (id: 12452)

As with many patriarchal narratives, the heart of the matter is retained through commemorative naming. Yahweh\-Yireh . . . means “the Lord will provide” (see [22:8](https://ref.ly/Gen22:8)). That Abraham used the holy name in this act shows that the patriarchs knew the name Yahweh (cp. [Exod 6:2–3](https://ref.ly/Exod6:2-Exod6:3)) but not its full meaning (see study note on Exod 6:2–3). • To this day: Later Israelites understood this passage as a lesson about their own worship in Jerusalem. Abraham’s sacrifice took place on the mountain of the Lord, later the location of the Temple in Jerusalem (see study note on 22:2). Three times a year, the people of Israel brought their best to God as a sacrifice, trusting that he would continue to provide for their needs.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:14
* **Associated Articles:** Exodus 6:2-3 (ID: `11409@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Genesis 22:2 (ID: `12462@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 22:15–19 (id: 12453)

After the event, God again confirmed his covenant with Abraham (cp. [15:5](https://ref.ly/Gen15:5), [18–21](https://ref.ly/Gen15:18-Gen15:21); [17:3–8](https://ref.ly/Gen17:3-Gen17:8)). His descendants would be numerous like the stars in the sky (cp. [26:4](https://ref.ly/Gen26:4)), like the sand on the seashore (cp. [32:12](https://ref.ly/Gen32:12)), and like the dust of the earth (cp. [13:16](https://ref.ly/Gen13:16); [28:14](https://ref.ly/Gen28:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:15–GEN 22:19

## Genesis 22:16 (id: 12454)

by my own name: There is no higher name by which God can swear ([Heb 6:13–17](https://ref.ly/Heb6:13-Heb6:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:16

## Genesis 22:17 (id: 12455)

Joshua and Old Testament Israel partially fulfilled the promise that Abraham’s descendants would conquer the cities (literally *take possession of the gates*) of God’s enemies; this will be fulfilled fully by the church (cp. [Matt 16:18](https://ref.ly/Matt16:18) and study note there).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:17

## Genesis 22:18–25.11 (id: 12456)

Abraham passed the test of faith; from this point, his task was to pass the covenant blessings to Isaac. He purchased a burial plot (ch [23](https://ref.ly/Gen23:1-Gen23:20)), acquired a wife for Isaac (ch [24](https://ref.ly/Gen24:1-Gen24:67)), and distributed his property ([25:1–11](https://ref.ly/Gen25:1-Gen25:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:18

## Genesis 22:20 (id: 12459)

Milcah, Nahor’s wife, was also his niece (see [11:29](https://ref.ly/Gen11:29)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:20

## Genesis 22:20–24 (id: 12458)

A report came from the east that Abraham’s brother Nahor (see [11:27–29](https://ref.ly/Gen11:27-Gen11:29)) was flourishing. The actors in the following narrative are introduced here.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:20–GEN 22:24

## Genesis 22:21 (id: 12460)

Uz might have been Job’s forefather ([Job 1:1](https://ref.ly/Job1:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:21

## Genesis 22:22–23 (id: 12461)

Bethuel was the youngest of Nahor’s eight sons by Milcah; he was the father of Rebekah, Isaac’s future wife (see [24:15](https://ref.ly/Gen24:15), [67](https://ref.ly/Gen24:67)). Rebekah would follow Sarah as matriarch of the clan ([23:1–2](https://ref.ly/Gen23:1-Gen23:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 22:22–GEN 22:23

## Genesis 23:1–2 (id: 12468)

Sarah was 127 years old: Isaac was 37 at this time (cp. [17:17](https://ref.ly/Gen17:17)). • Hebron: See study note on 13:18.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:1–GEN 23:2
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 13:18 (ID: `12313@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 23:1–20 (id: 12467)

When Sarah . . . died, Abraham acquired a parcel of land for a burial place. This transaction was the first sign that a permanent transition had taken place, as people were normally buried in their ancestral homeland (cp. [49:29–50:13](https://ref.ly/Gen49:29-Gen50:13)). In burying Sarah, Abraham detached from his just\-mentioned ancestral home (where his relatives still lived, [22:20–24](https://ref.ly/Gen22:20-Gen22:24)); his future would be in Canaan, where his descendants would realize the promise.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:1–GEN 23:20

## Genesis 23:3–4 (id: 12473)

Abraham bargained with local Hittite elders for a piece of land for a burial site. These Hittites had apparently migrated south to Canaan from the great Hittite empire in eastern Asia Minor (modern Turkey; cp. [10:15](https://ref.ly/Gen10:15)). • Abraham was a stranger and a foreigner among these people; his hope was in God’s promise that he would eventually possess the land.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:3–GEN 23:4

## Genesis 23:5–6 (id: 12474)

my lord, you are an honored prince: Either Abraham was highly regarded by these people, or they were politely appealing to his generosity. • Choose the finest. . . . No one here will refuse: They were willing to accommodate his request, especially if they could legally obligate him to themselves ([23:11](https://ref.ly/Gen23:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:5–GEN 23:6

## Genesis 23:7–16 (id: 12475)

Abraham wanted to buy Ephron’s cave at Machpelah, but Ephron wanted him to buy the entire field.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:7–GEN 23:16

## Genesis 23:9 (id: 12476)

This would be a permanent burial place for Abraham’s family. The site was near Mamre ([23:19](https://ref.ly/Gen23:19)), where Abraham lived (see [13:18](https://ref.ly/Gen13:18); [14:13](https://ref.ly/Gen14:13); [18:1](https://ref.ly/Gen18:1)). Abraham and Sarah, Isaac and Rebekah, and Jacob and Leah would all be buried in this cave ([23:19](https://ref.ly/Gen23:19); [25:9](https://ref.ly/Gen25:9); [35:27–29](https://ref.ly/Gen35:27-Gen35:29); [49:29–31](https://ref.ly/Gen49:29-Gen49:31); [50:13](https://ref.ly/Gen50:13)), their permanent place in the Promised Land.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:9

## Genesis 23:11 (id: 12469)

Ephron did not intend to give the cave to Abraham; Abraham was expected to “give” the full price in return. Ephron wanted to sell as much as he could to avoid responsibility for caring for the cave and to receive as high a price as possible.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:11

## Genesis 23:12–13 (id: 12470)

Abraham didn’t want the whole field, but he was willing to take it to get the cave.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:12–GEN 23:13

## Genesis 23:15 (id: 12471)

400 pieces of silver was a very high price. Ephron’s politeness was typical of the bargaining process.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:15

## Genesis 23:16–20 (id: 12472)

Abraham paid the amount and finalized the transaction, avoiding indebtedness by accepting no gifts from the people (cp. [14:21–24](https://ref.ly/Gen14:21-Gen14:24)). The Hittite elders witnessed the transaction, ensuring that no one could challenge Abraham’s full ownership of the land. The transaction took place at the city gate, where public legal and business dealings were conducted (cp. [19:1](https://ref.ly/Gen19:1)). The land became Abraham’s permanent possession, a down payment on God’s promise to give him the land. Abraham knew that God’s promise was not fulfilled ([12:7](https://ref.ly/Gen12:7)) by this acquisition, so he planned for the future. By buying land for his dead, he declared that God’s promises do not end with this life. This is the hope of all who die in faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 23:16–GEN 23:20

## Genesis 24:1–9 (id: 12478)

Confident in the Lord’s promise, Abraham had his chief servant (probably Eliezer, [15:2](https://ref.ly/Gen15:2)) swear to find a wife among Abraham’s relatives in his homeland, some 450 miles away. Abraham was faithful to the covenant by preparing for Isaac’s future.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:1–GEN 24:9

## Genesis 24:1–67 (id: 12477)

Isaac’s marriage to Rebekah ensured that God’s plan would continue into the next generation. God showed covenant faithfulness by working through his faithful people ([24:12](https://ref.ly/Gen24:12), [27](https://ref.ly/Gen24:27), [49](https://ref.ly/Gen24:49)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:1–GEN 24:67

## Genesis 24:2 (id: 12490)

Putting his hand under Abraham’s thigh (cp. [47:29](https://ref.ly/Gen47:29)), the servant took a very solemn oath, assuming the burden of completing this mission.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:2

## Genesis 24:3 (id: 12492)

Isaac knew how wicked and threatening the local Canaanite people were, so he maintained separation from them (cp. [26:34–35](https://ref.ly/Gen26:34-Gen26:35); [27:46](https://ref.ly/Gen27:46); [28:8–9](https://ref.ly/Gen28:8-Gen28:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:3

## Genesis 24:6–8 (id: 12498)

Under no circumstances was the servant to take Isaac from the Promised Land to seek a wife. Abraham ensured Isaac’s safety and secured God’s promises in the land of blessing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:6–GEN 24:8

## Genesis 24:10 (id: 12481)

Aram\-naharaim (“Aram of the two rivers”) was also called *Paddan\-aram* (“the field of Aram,” cp. [25:20](https://ref.ly/Gen25:20)). It was a two\-week journey in each direction, so the servant had ten . . . camels for provisions and gifts ([24:22](https://ref.ly/Gen24:22), [53](https://ref.ly/Gen24:53)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:10

## Genesis 24:10–27 (id: 12479)

The servant obeyed his master’s instructions and trusted God to lead him to the right woman.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:10–GEN 24:27

## Genesis 24:10–60 (id: 12480)

The servant faithfully carried out the mission, and he glorified God in displaying faithful covenant love for Abraham’s family by bringing all the details together. God sovereignly worked behind the scenes to accomplish his will through the circumstances of those acting responsibly in faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:10–GEN 24:60

## Genesis 24:14 (id: 12482)

Abraham’s future daughter\-in\-law manifested hospitality and industry like Abraham’s (see [18:1–8](https://ref.ly/Gen18:1-Gen18:8)). Ten thirsty camels could drink 250 gallons of water, so a woman who would work that hard for a stranger was certainly not lazy, but generous and hospitable.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:14

## Genesis 24:15–22 (id: 12483)

The servant received a precise, immediate answer to his prayer for guidance.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:15–GEN 24:22

## Genesis 24:22 (id: 12484)

The servant showed his gratitude by giving the girl expensive jewelry.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:22

## Genesis 24:23–24 (id: 12485)

God led the servant to Abraham’s family.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:23–GEN 24:24

## Genesis 24:25 (id: 12486)

Rebekah again showed kindness by offering lodging for the servant and food for his camels.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:25

## Genesis 24:26 (id: 12487)

The servant bowed to the ground and worshiped the Lord for his loyal love and faithfulness in guiding him to the exact family he sought.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:26

## Genesis 24:27 (id: 12488)

The Lord has shown unfailing love and faithfulness to my master: These words express this chapter’s message (cp. [24:48](https://ref.ly/Gen24:48)). Believers can trust the Lord’s leading because he is sovereign in all things. He never leaves his people to their own resources in carrying out his covenant work.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:27

## Genesis 24:29–31 (id: 12489)

Laban . . . ran: He was not going to miss the chance to marry his sister off and receive more gifts. Laban’s response to the servant’s wealth foreshadows his avaricious character (see [24:54–56](https://ref.ly/Gen24:54-Gen24:56) and study note; [29:21–27](https://ref.ly/Gen29:21-Gen29:27); [30:27–36](https://ref.ly/Gen30:27-Gen30:36); [31:1–13](https://ref.ly/Gen31:1-Gen31:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:29–GEN 24:31
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 24:54-56 (ID: `12496@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 24:33–48 (id: 12491)

The servant was not diverted from his mission; he insisted on telling his story before he would eat. He recounted his mission and acknowledged God’s providence in directing him to Rebekah before attending to his personal needs. The servant wanted everyone to know that this was God’s work, not a chance or humanly arranged meeting.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:33–GEN 24:48

## Genesis 24:48 (id: 12493)

The way that God directed this event from behind the scenes is different than in most of Genesis, but it is true to how the life of faith normally works. Faith, expressed in personal prayer and obedience, looks for evidence of God’s working. Believers usually have to make wise choices and remain faithful to the covenant, trusting that God will guide them through the circumstances of life to accomplish his will.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:48

## Genesis 24:50–51 (id: 12494)

Following his testimony about God’s guidance, the servant secured the family’s blessing and permission to take Rebekah to his master’s son Isaac.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:50–GEN 24:51

## Genesis 24:53 (id: 12495)

The servant gave expensive gifts to Rebekah, her mother, and her brother to conclude the arrangements.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:53

## Genesis 24:54–56 (id: 12496)

It was hard for Rebekah’s family to let her go so suddenly, and Laban may have hoped to gain more wealth. However, the servant had sworn an oath and would not rest until it was completed. There was no reason for him to stay.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:54–GEN 24:56

## Genesis 24:57–58 (id: 12497)

Rebekah’s decision to leave immediately to be with her new husband settled the impasse. Rebekah submitted to the Lord’s obvious leading. Young women were normally eager to marry (not to marry was a catastrophe), and later accounts of Laban suggest why Rebekah preferred to leave (cp. [31:14–15](https://ref.ly/Gen31:14-Gen31:15)). Rebekah displayed faithful love to the servant, Abraham’s family, and Isaac by going to be Isaac’s wife.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:57–GEN 24:58

## Genesis 24:60 (id: 12499)

At Rebekah’s departure, her family invoked the blessing that she would be a mother of many millions and that her descendants would conquer . . . their enemies. Rebekah’s marriage to Isaac was part of God’s plan to bless all humankind ([12:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:60

## Genesis 24:62 (id: 12500)

Isaac lived in the Negev, in the southern part of Canaan.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:62

## Genesis 24:67 (id: 12501)

Isaac was 40 years old when he married Rebekah ([25:20](https://ref.ly/Gen25:20)), so Abraham was 140 ([21:5](https://ref.ly/Gen21:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 24:67

## Genesis 25:1 (id: 12510)

Exactly when Abraham married . . . Keturah is unknown. It was probably, but not necessarily, after Sarah’s death.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:1

## Genesis 25:1–11 (id: 12502)

Though Abraham had sons by another wife, he safeguarded Isaac’s inheritance and blessing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:1–GEN 25:11

## Genesis 25:2–4 (id: 12511)

The birth of these nations from Abraham partially fulfilled God’s promise to him ([12:2](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2); [17:4](https://ref.ly/Gen17:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:2–GEN 25:4

## Genesis 25:3 (id: 12525)

Sheba and Dedan: Cp. [10:7](https://ref.ly/Gen10:7). Abraham’s descendants probably settled in these regions and became identified by their names, along with people of other lineage.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:3

## Genesis 25:5–6 (id: 12526)

Abraham loved all his sons, so before he died, he gave them gifts and sent them away as he had sent Ishmael ([21:8–14](https://ref.ly/Gen21:8-Gen21:14)). In this way, he preserved Isaac’s position as his heir.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:5–GEN 25:6

## Genesis 25:7–8 (id: 12527)

Abraham’s death is recorded before the births of Jacob and Esau, but he lived until they were fifteen years old ([25:19–26](https://ref.ly/Gen25:19-Gen25:26); cp. [21:5](https://ref.ly/Gen21:5); [25:26](https://ref.ly/Gen25:26)). This literary arrangement closes Abraham’s story before focusing on Isaac’s family.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:7–GEN 25:8

## Genesis 25:11 (id: 12503)

God’s blessing transferred to Isaac; Abraham’s other sons had been sent away. Isaac lived near Beer\-lahai\-roi—a special place where God had answered prayer ([16:14](https://ref.ly/Gen16:14)) and where Isaac waited on the Lord ([24:62](https://ref.ly/Gen24:62)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:11

## Genesis 25:12–18 (id: 12504)

This record lists Ishmael’s descendants before tracing Isaac’s ([25:19–35:29](https://ref.ly/Gen25:19-Gen35:29)), which is in keeping with the literary arrangement of Genesis (see Genesis Book Introduction, “Summary”).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:12–GEN 25:18

## Genesis 25:16 (id: 12505)

Ishmael’s twelve sons fulfilled God’s promise of blessing ([17:20](https://ref.ly/Gen17:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:16

## Genesis 25:18 (id: 12506)

Havilah was a region in north\-central Arabia. • Shur was a region between Beersheba and Egypt. • in open hostility toward all their relatives: The wording is close to that of [16:12](https://ref.ly/Gen16:12).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:18

## Genesis 25:19–20 (id: 12507)

Isaac . . . married Rebekah: Isaac’s marriage tied him and his family even more closely to Abraham’s ancestors. Had he married a Canaanite, the covenant faith would have been imperiled by this corrupt, syncretistic people.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:19–GEN 25:20

## Genesis 25:19–26 (id: 12508)

Jacob’s struggle for supremacy began before the twins were born (see [Hos 12:3](https://ref.ly/Hos12:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:19–GEN 25:26

## Genesis 25:19–35.29 (id: 12509)

This account of the family of Isaac, the chosen son of Abraham, mostly recounts Jacob’s struggle for the blessing ([25:27–34](https://ref.ly/Gen25:27-Gen25:34); chs [27–33](https://ref.ly/Gen27:1-Gen33:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:19

## Genesis 25:21 (id: 12512)

For twenty years, Rebekah was barren (cp. [25:20](https://ref.ly/Gen25:20), [26](https://ref.ly/Gen25:26)), like Sarah (see [16:1](https://ref.ly/Gen16:1)). This condition tested their faith (see study note on 16:1–3). How could they be childless when God promised that nations would issue from them? • Isaac pleaded with the Lord and the Lord answered Isaac’s prayer. Isaac apparently learned from his father’s mistake and responded in faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:21
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 16:1-3 (ID: `12348@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 25:22 (id: 12513)

When the pregnancy was difficult, Rebekah went to ask the Lord about it, probably by visiting a prophet—perhaps Abraham ([20:7](https://ref.ly/Gen20:7); see study note on 25:7–8).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:22
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 25:7-8 (ID: `12527@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 25:23 (id: 12514)

rivals: Jacob and Esau fought in the womb, and their descendants (Israel and Edom) fought continuously throughout their history. In their many conflicts, Israel achieved supremacy over Edom. • your older son will serve your younger son: God’s choice of the younger son over the elder ran against natural order (cp. [48:12–14](https://ref.ly/Gen48:12-Gen48:14); see [Mal 1:1–3](https://ref.ly/Mal1:1-Mal1:3); [Rom 9:11–16](https://ref.ly/Rom9:11-Rom9:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:23

## Genesis 25:24–26 (id: 12515)

When the twins were born, the unusual circumstances inspired each boy’s name and hinted at what would happen to them in the future.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:24–GEN 25:26

## Genesis 25:25 (id: 12516)

Two wordplays anticipate Esau’s later life. Esau sounds like Hebrew se‘ar, which means “hair”; Esau’s later homeland, Edom, was known as *Seir* (“hairy”) because it was wooded (as though covered with hair). • red (Hebrew ’admoni) sounds like Esau’s other name, Edom ([25:27–34](https://ref.ly/Gen25:27-Gen25:34)); Edom had red soil. • Esau’s \>hair was like the fur coat of an animal, foreshadowing his unspiritual character ([25:34](https://ref.ly/Gen25:34); [Heb 12:16](https://ref.ly/Heb12:16); cp. [Lev 26:22](https://ref.ly/Lev26:22); [Deut 7:22](https://ref.ly/Deut7:22); [1 Cor 15:32](https://ref.ly/1Cor15:32)). The description of the child uses words that highlight the Edomites’ nature.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:25

## Genesis 25:26 (id: 12517)

The name Jacob (Hebrew ya‘aqob, from Hebrew ‘aqeb, “heel, footprint”) was originally positive, meaning “protect” (like a rear guard), but it took on the negative meaning of “heel grabber” or “deceiver” in the context of Jacob’s deceptive, grasping, usurping character (see [27:36](https://ref.ly/Gen27:36)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:26

## Genesis 25:27 (id: 12519)

Esau was a skillful hunter and an outdoorsman—a wild man who loved wild country. Jacob had a quiet (or *even*) temperament and preferred to stay at home (literally *dwelling in tents*)—i.e., he was civilized.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:27

## Genesis 25:27–34 (id: 12518)

Jacob and Esau each developed in accord with his initial characteristics ([25:24–26](https://ref.ly/Gen25:24-Gen25:26)). Esau, the reddish, hairy man, cared about physical things rather than spiritual things (see [Heb 12:16](https://ref.ly/Heb12:16)); he was finally overcome by physical appetites and sold his birthright. Jacob, the heel grabber, knew the birthright’s value and drove a ruthless bargain to gain it presumptuously from his brother.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:27–GEN 25:34

## Genesis 25:28 (id: 12520)

The parents each practiced favoritism. • because: Isaac’s love for Esau was conditioned upon his son’s performance. Rebekah’s love for Jacob was constant and unconditional.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:28

## Genesis 25:29 (id: 12521)

Ironically, Jacob proved the more cunning hunter. The word cooking (Hebrew wayyazed, “boiling”) sounds like the word for “hunter” (Hebrew tsayid). While boiling stew, Jacob was laying a trap for the hairy red animal. He may have waited a long time for this opportunity. This word for “boil” was also used for presumptuous action (like water boiling over the rim of the pot). Jacob overstepped his boundaries when he seized the promise for himself. By contrast, Abraham knew the promise was his and was secure in giving the land away (see study note on 13:8–13). • Esau was exhausted and hungry, but his life was not in danger ([25:32](https://ref.ly/Gen25:32); see study note on 25:33–34).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:29
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 13:8-13 (ID: `12316@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Genesis 25:33-34 (ID: `12524@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 25:30 (id: 12522)

Esau was preoccupied with his appetite. Being driven by one’s appetites leaves no place for spiritual values. The text emphasizes this by using a Hebrew word (translated give) that was normally used for feeding animals. • Edom, which means “red”: See study note on 25:25.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:30
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 25:25 (ID: `12516@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 25:31–33 (id: 12523)

Jacob was the better hunter on this occasion, but great danger lay in exercising such strong ambition. God’s people should desire the things of God, but they must not seek them in the flesh (see [Zech 4:6](https://ref.ly/Zech4:6); [Gal 5:16–17](https://ref.ly/Gal5:16-Gal5:17); [Eph 6:10–12](https://ref.ly/Eph6:10-Eph6:12)). The Lord dealt severely with Jacob to purge him of carnal methods. He later received the promise not as crafty Jacob the usurper, but as Israel (meaning, “God fights”), with God fighting on his behalf ([Gen 32:28](https://ref.ly/Gen32:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:31–GEN 25:33

## Genesis 25:33–34 (id: 12524)

Esau eagerly took Jacob’s bait and fell into the trap. He ate and left too quickly to have been near death ([25:32](https://ref.ly/Gen25:32)). The final comment on the passage explains that Esau showed contempt for his birthright, considering it worthless ([Heb 12:16](https://ref.ly/Heb12:16)). It is foolish to sacrifice spiritual blessings to satisfy physical appetites (cp. [Gen 3:6](https://ref.ly/Gen3:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 25:33–GEN 25:34

## Genesis 26:1 (id: 12533)

This Abimelech is probably not the man in ch [20](https://ref.ly/Gen20:1-Gen20:18), for these events could have been 90 years apart. Possibly Abimelech was a dynastic name or title (a later King Achish, [1 Sam 21:10](https://ref.ly/1Sam21:10), was also called Abimelech, [Ps 34:title](https://ref.ly/Ps34:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:1

## Genesis 26:1–35 (id: 12528)

In this digression from Jacob’s story, Isaac’s prosperity (ch [26](https://ref.ly/Gen26:1-Gen26:35)) shows that the blessing had passed to him (cp. [25:11](https://ref.ly/Gen25:11)) despite his failures of faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:1–GEN 26:35

## Genesis 26:2–5 (id: 12534)

The Lord assured Isaac that the covenant promises (cp. [12:2–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2-Gen12:3); [15:5–8](https://ref.ly/Gen15:5-Gen15:8); [17:3–8](https://ref.ly/Gen17:3-Gen17:8); [22:15–18](https://ref.ly/Gen22:15-Gen22:18); [28:13–14](https://ref.ly/Gen28:13-Gen28:14)) would pass to him because Abraham faithfully listened to God and obeyed all his requirements, commands, decrees, and instructions. These terms were later used in Deuteronomy to describe God’s full legal covenant with Israel. An Israelite reader would immediately think of the complete Torah when hearing these words and be prompted to obey God’s law as Abraham did, though Abraham had only a few commands from the Lord. Through these words, the text emphasizes that Abraham would have obeyed the later commands if he had had them, because he was an obedient servant of the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:2–GEN 26:5

## Genesis 26:6–11 (id: 12539)

While staying in Gerar, Isaac, like his father, deceived people into believing that his wife was his sister. Some suppose that this story duplicates the stories of Abraham’s deception ([12:10–20](https://ref.ly/Gen12:10-Gen12:20); [20:1–18](https://ref.ly/Gen20:1-Gen20:18)), but the differences are greater than the similarities, and the son’s repetition of his father’s lie is natural. Through numerous parallels with Abraham, ch [26](https://ref.ly/Gen26:1-Gen26:35) shows how God’s plan continued with Isaac. Even when Isaac jeopardized the covenant as his father had, God prevented disaster and preserved the marriage. Abraham’s descendants would be blessed because of Abraham, but they had to exercise their own faith to enjoy the blessings. Genuine faith in God’s promises engenders a fearless walk with him; cowering in fear endangers the blessing and mocks the faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:6–GEN 26:11

## Genesis 26:8 (id: 12540)

Abimelech . . . saw Isaac caressing Rebekah: The word for “caressing” (Hebrew metsakheq) is the same as the word used for Ishmael’s “making fun of” Isaac ([21:9](https://ref.ly/Gen21:9)); the word is related to the name “Isaac” (Hebrew yitskhaq). It is as though Isaac’s lapse of faith made fun of Abimelech and made a mockery of Rebekah and the great promise embodied in Isaac’s name.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:8

## Genesis 26:10–11 (id: 12529)

Isaac, like his father, was rebuked by Abimelech (see study note on 20:1–18). This legal wording would remind Israel of how important it was to preserve marital purity. Abimelech recognized the danger to his own people. Though his decree preserved his own society, it was also a word from God that preserved the sanctity of Isaac’s and Israel’s marriages. If Isaac’s marriage had ended here, there would have been no Israelite society.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:10–GEN 26:11
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 20:1-18 (ID: `12419@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 26:12–13 (id: 12530)

Isaac lived in the land as a temporary settler, enjoying abundant prosperity because of God’s blessing; his crops flourished and he became very rich.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:12–GEN 26:13

## Genesis 26:14–16 (id: 12531)

The Philistines envied Isaac’s prosperity and filled his wells with dirt. The king then ordered Isaac to leave that region because he was too powerful for them (cp. [21:22–23](https://ref.ly/Gen21:22-Gen21:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:14–GEN 26:16

## Genesis 26:17–22 (id: 12532)

Isaac moved . . . to the Gerar Valley (away from the city of Gerar itself, [26:6](https://ref.ly/Gen26:6), but probably still within ten miles) and reopened his father’s wells. Isaac was also opposed there, but chose not to fight back; he relinquished one well after another until God’s blessing outdid the opposition. Whenever Isaac reopened a well, and regardless of how often enemies caused them to cave in, he found water. God was blessing Isaac and that blessing could not be hindered. Finally, the Philistines left Isaac alone.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:17–GEN 26:22

## Genesis 26:23–25 (id: 12535)

At Beersheba, . . . the Lord appeared to Isaac to confirm his covenant (cp. [21:31–33](https://ref.ly/Gen21:31-Gen21:33)). Isaac responded in faith as his father had done by building an altar to the Lord and proclaiming the Lord’s identity and nature (see study note on 12:8; [21:33](https://ref.ly/Gen21:33)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:23–GEN 26:25
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 12:8 (ID: `12306@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 26:26–33 (id: 12536)

This treaty is similar to the one an earlier king had made with Abraham (cp. [21:22–31](https://ref.ly/Gen21:22-Gen21:31)). This king acknowledged that God was blessing Isaac and realized that a treaty with Isaac would benefit him. No opposition can hinder God’s blessing—it will flourish, and other nations will see it and seek peace with God’s people to share in the blessing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:26–GEN 26:33

## Genesis 26:33 (id: 12537)

Since the earlier treaty was renewed with Isaac, the name of the well was also renewed by the oath.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:33

## Genesis 26:34–35 (id: 12538)

Esau’s marriages illustrate how unfit he was to lead the covenant people into God’s blessings, and how foolish was Isaac’s later attempt to bless Esau ([27:1–40](https://ref.ly/Gen27:1-Gen27:40)). Esau later married a third wife in a vain attempt to do the right thing ([28:6–9](https://ref.ly/Gen28:6-Gen28:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 26:34–GEN 26:35

## Genesis 27:1–4 (id: 12542)

The first scene sets up the chapter’s crisis. Isaac knew of God’s oracle ([25:22–23](https://ref.ly/Gen25:22-Gen25:23)), yet he thwarted or ignored it by trying to bless Esau. • Isaac was old and turning blind: He was losing his senses, both physically and spiritually.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:1–GEN 27:4

## Genesis 27:1–40 (id: 12541)

Jacob got his father Isaac’s blessing through deception. In this story, an entire family tries to carry out their responsibilities by physical means rather than by faith. Faith would have provided Rebekah and Jacob a more honorable solution to the crisis.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:1–GEN 27:40

## Genesis 27:3–4 (id: 12547)

Like Esau, Isaac allowed his palate to govern his heart (cp. [25:28–34](https://ref.ly/Gen25:28-Gen25:34)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:3–GEN 27:4

## Genesis 27:5 (id: 12558)

Esau agreed to Isaac’s plan, thus breaking the oath he had sworn to Jacob ([25:33](https://ref.ly/Gen25:33)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:5

## Genesis 27:5–17 (id: 12557)

The blessing seemed to be in jeopardy. In scene two, Rebekah and Jacob sought to achieve God’s blessing by deception, without faith or love. Rebekah planned to deceive the old man into thinking that he was blessing Esau when he was actually blessing Jacob.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:5–GEN 27:17

## Genesis 27:11–12 (id: 12543)

Jacob had no qualms about this deception; he only feared that it might not work and that he would be cursed for trying.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:11–GEN 27:12

## Genesis 27:18–20 (id: 12544)

Jacob lied about his identity, and then came close to blasphemy by lying about God.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:18–GEN 27:20

## Genesis 27:18–29 (id: 12545)

In scene three, Jacob deceives his father and receives the blessing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:18–GEN 27:29

## Genesis 27:20–27 (id: 12546)

Isaac voiced his suspicion three times but was finally deceived by his senses, which were not functioning well (see study note on 27:1–4).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:20–GEN 27:27
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 27:1-4 (ID: `12542@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 27:30–40 (id: 12548)

In scene four, everything is discovered, and the family becomes even more divided.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:30–GEN 27:40

## Genesis 27:33 (id: 12549)

When he realized what had happened, Isaac began to tremble uncontrollably; he had been tampering with God’s plan, and God had overruled him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:33

## Genesis 27:34–35 (id: 12550)

Esau was very bitter, and angry enough to kill ([27:41](https://ref.ly/Gen27:41)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:34–GEN 27:35

## Genesis 27:36 (id: 12551)

Esau began to realize Jacob’s true nature, saying he has cheated me (or *tripped me up,* or *deceived me)* twice, by taking the birthright (the right of inheritance, Hebrew bekorah; [25:27–34](https://ref.ly/Gen25:27-Gen25:34)) and by deceiving their father to receive the blessing (the spoken pronouncement of the inheritance, Hebrew berakah). • Esau’s assessment of Jacob was correct, but he failed to see his own ungodliness in these transactions (see study note on 27:5; [Heb 12:16](https://ref.ly/Heb12:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:36
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 27:5 (ID: `12558@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 27:37 (id: 12552)

There was no going back. Isaac had declared an oracle from God, who had made Jacob to be Esau’s master (see [Rom 9:11–13](https://ref.ly/Rom9:11-Rom9:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:37

## Genesis 27:39–40 (id: 12553)

All that remained for Esau was a promise of hardship and struggle (cp. [3:17–19](https://ref.ly/Gen3:17-Gen3:19); [16:11–12](https://ref.ly/Gen16:11-Gen16:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:39–GEN 27:40

## Genesis 27:41–45 (id: 12554)

Rebekah and Jacob got the blessing but reaped hatred from Esau and separation from one another; there is no indication that Rebekah and Jacob ever saw each other again. They gained nothing that God was not already going to give them, and their methods were costly. Jacob fled from home to escape Esau’s vengeance.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:41–GEN 27:45

## Genesis 27:42–33.17 (id: 12555)

Jacob met the Lord on a road that Abraham had traveled 125 years earlier. The story in [27:42–33:17](https://ref.ly/Gen27:42-Gen33:17) follows a pattern (called a "chiasm") focusing on Jacob’s exile: 

* A: Jacob runs away from Esau ([27:42–28:9](https://ref.ly/Gen27:42-Gen28:9))

    + B: Angels of God meet Jacob at Bethel ([28:10–22](https://ref.ly/Gen28:10-Gen28:22))

    - C: Jacob lives in exile in Paddan\-aram ([29:1–31:55](https://ref.ly/Gen29:1-Gen31:55))
        + B’: Angels of God meet Jacob at Mahanaim ([32:1–2](https://ref.ly/Gen32:1-Gen32:2))
* A’: Jacob reconciles with Esau ([32:3–33:17](https://ref.ly/Gen32:3-Gen33:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:42–GEN 27:45

## Genesis 27:46 (id: 12556)

Rebekah convinced Isaac to send Jacob away by carefully planning her words and actions. Like Isaac, Jacob married a woman from his relatives who lived in the east.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 27:46

## Genesis 28:1–2 (id: 12559)

Isaac remained in the land, but Jacob had to leave it. God would deal with Jacob under the hand of Laban, his uncle (see study note on 29:1–31:55). • Believers in any age must remain spiritually pure by marrying other believers ([2 Cor 6:14–18](https://ref.ly/2Cor6:14-2Cor6:18)). The Canaanite people incorporated dozens of groups and clans into their society and religion by wars, treaties, and marriages (see [Gen 34:20–23](https://ref.ly/Gen34:20-Gen34:23)). Abraham’s family was to resist such mixing (cp. [24:3](https://ref.ly/Gen24:3); ch [34](https://ref.ly/Gen34:1-Gen34:31)); they were to marry within their clan to maintain the purity of the line and of the faith that identified them as the chosen seed. The surest way to lose their distinctiveness was to intermarry with people of other tribal backgrounds and beliefs (see [Ezra 9–10](https://ref.ly/Ezra9:1-Ezra10:44); [Neh 13:23–29](https://ref.ly/Neh13:23-Neh13:29)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:1–GEN 28:2
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 29:1-31.55 (ID: `12573@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 28:3–5 (id: 12571)

Before Jacob departed, Isaac gave him a pure, legitimate blessing. He did not hold back, because he now knew what God wanted him to do. Isaac clearly passed on the blessing God Almighty (Hebrew El\-Shaddai; see [17:1](https://ref.ly/Gen17:1)) had given to Abraham and to him regarding prosperity and the land (cp. [15:5](https://ref.ly/Gen15:5), [18–20](https://ref.ly/Gen15:18-Gen15:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:3–GEN 28:5

## Genesis 28:6–9 (id: 12572)

Esau, the unchosen son still trying to please his father, married a woman from the unchosen line of Ishmael, which he thought would be more acceptable. He did not understand the uniqueness of the covenant family.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:6–GEN 28:9

## Genesis 28:10–22 (id: 12560)

Despite Jacob’s previous means of securing the blessing, God assured him of protection and provision. The God of Abraham and Isaac was also the God of Jacob. The revelation dramatically changed Jacob’s outlook and brought faith into clearer focus.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:10–GEN 28:22

## Genesis 28:11 (id: 12561)

The good place where Jacob set up camp was apparently a protected area at the foot of a hill. The stone to rest his head against was probably large, more for protection than for a pillow.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:11

## Genesis 28:12–13 (id: 12562)

stairway (traditionally *ladder*): This word occurs nowhere else in Scripture. The imagery probably reminded readers of a staircase or ramp up the front of a ziggurat that signified communication between heaven and earth (see study note on 11:4). God initiated a divine communication between heaven and earth to guide and protect Jacob, the steward of his covenant ([28:13–15](https://ref.ly/Gen28:13-Gen28:15); see [Ps 91:11–15](https://ref.ly/Ps91:11-Ps91:15)). • Jesus said that he himself is the stairway between heaven and earth ([John 1:51](https://ref.ly/John1:51)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:12–GEN 28:13
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 11:4 (ID: `12280@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 28:12–15 (id: 12563)

The point of the vision was that God and his angels were with Jacob on his journey. God reiterated to Jacob the covenant promises made to Abraham and Isaac, promising him land, descendants numerous as the dust (cp. [13:16](https://ref.ly/Gen13:16); [22:17](https://ref.ly/Gen22:17)), and universal blessing through him (cp. [12:2–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2-Gen12:3); [15:5](https://ref.ly/Gen15:5), [18](https://ref.ly/Gen15:18); [17:3–8](https://ref.ly/Gen17:3-Gen17:8); [22:15–18](https://ref.ly/Gen22:15-Gen22:18); [35:11–12](https://ref.ly/Gen35:11-Gen35:12)). God also promised to be with Jacob and watch over him until he returned.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:12–GEN 28:15

## Genesis 28:14 (id: 12564)

Jacob inherited Abraham’s entire covenant (see [22:17](https://ref.ly/Gen22:17)), which confirmed Isaac’s blessing ([28:3–4](https://ref.ly/Gen28:3-Gen28:4)) and stipulated a temporary exile ([28:15](https://ref.ly/Gen28:15); see [15:12–16](https://ref.ly/Gen15:12-Gen15:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:14

## Genesis 28:15 (id: 12565)

I am with you: The promise of God’s presence meant that God would protect and provide for Jacob in a special way. God’s promise to be with his people is repeated throughout Scripture (see also [26:24](https://ref.ly/Gen26:24)), prompting a response of worship and confidence in those who have faith ([28:16–22](https://ref.ly/Gen28:16-Gen28:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:15

## Genesis 28:16–22 (id: 12566)

The second half of the passage gives Jacob’s response to the revelation. He felt reverential fear and awe in the Lord’s presence, and his acts of devotion became archetypes of Israel’s worship. When God graciously visits his people and promises to be with them and make them a blessing to the world, his people respond in faith with reverential fear, worship, offerings, and vows. They preserve their faith in memory for future worshipers.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:16–GEN 28:22

## Genesis 28:18 (id: 12567)

Anointing with oil became a way of setting something apart for divine use in Israel’s worship ([Exod 29:1–7](https://ref.ly/Exod29:1-Exod29:7); [40:9](https://ref.ly/Exod40:9); [Lev 2:1](https://ref.ly/Lev2:1); [1 Sam 10:1](https://ref.ly/1Sam10:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:18

## Genesis 28:19 (id: 12568)

Bethel later became a holy site for Israel (see [Judg 20:18–27](https://ref.ly/Judg20:18-Judg20:27); [1 Sam 7:16](https://ref.ly/1Sam7:16); [10:3](https://ref.ly/1Sam10:3); [1 Kgs 12:26–13:10](https://ref.ly/1Kgs12:26-1Kgs13:10); [2 Kgs 2:2–3](https://ref.ly/2Kgs2:2-2Kgs2:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:19

## Genesis 28:20–22 (id: 12569)

In view of what God would do for him, Jacob vowed to do certain things for God. He believed the Lord’s words and responded in gratitude. Jacob’s vow influenced Israel’s way of making commitments to God in worship.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:20–GEN 28:22

## Genesis 28:22 (id: 12570)

I will present to God a tenth: By paying a tithe (*a tenth*) as an act of worship, a person acknowledges that everything is a gift from God and belongs to God (see [14:19–20](https://ref.ly/Gen14:19-Gen14:20); [Num 18:21–32](https://ref.ly/Num18:21-Num18:32); [Deut 14:22–29](https://ref.ly/Deut14:22-Deut14:29); [2 Chr 31:5–6](https://ref.ly/2Chr31:5-2Chr31:6); [Mal 3:7–12](https://ref.ly/Mal3:7-Mal3:12); [Matt 23:23](https://ref.ly/Matt23:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 28:22

## Genesis 29:1 (id: 12579)

Jacob hurried on: The Hebrew text says that he “picked up his feet” as if he felt the wind at his back; he continued his journey with fresh enthusiasm. His changed outlook was the direct result of the vision he received at Bethel, a marvelous revelation that God was going to protect and bless him. He now sought the fulfillment of God’s promises to him, not just an escape from Esau. Jacob’s attitude had become positive and magnanimous to the point of being naive and vulnerable.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:1

## Genesis 29:1–31.55 (id: 12573)

These chapters tell how God kept his promise by abundantly blessing Jacob with family and possessions. God also disciplined Jacob, leaving him to struggle with Laban for many years. Laban was Jacob’s match in deception, and thus a means of correction. • The story of Jacob and Laban parallels Israel’s later sojourn in Egypt. Jacob struggled while serving his uncle but finally emerged with a large family (the founders of the twelve tribes) and great wealth. In Egypt, the Israelites suffered under their oppressors, but they also flourished, becoming a great nation of twelve large tribes and escaping with great riches.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:1–GEN 31:55

## Genesis 29:2–12 (id: 12580)

Jacob’s meeting Rachel at the well was providentially timed by the sovereign God who was leading Jacob to fulfillment of the promises (cp. [24:12–20](https://ref.ly/Gen24:12-Gen24:20)). The well was a reminder of God’s blessing (cp. [16:13–14](https://ref.ly/Gen16:13-Gen16:14); [21:19](https://ref.ly/Gen21:19); [26:19–25](https://ref.ly/Gen26:19-Gen26:25), [33](https://ref.ly/Gen26:33)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:2–GEN 29:12

## Genesis 29:10 (id: 12574)

In contrast to the lazy, unhelpful shepherds ([29:7–8](https://ref.ly/Gen29:7-Gen29:8)), Jacob is portrayed as generous, industrious, and energetic. • Jacob . . . watered his uncle’s flock: Laban’s flocks would flourish under Jacob’s care.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:10

## Genesis 29:11 (id: 12575)

Jacob kissed Rachel: Kissing relatives was a proper greeting ([29:13](https://ref.ly/Gen29:13); cp. [Song 8:1](https://ref.ly/Song8:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:11

## Genesis 29:14 (id: 12577)

You really are my own flesh and blood! Laban welcomed Jacob into his house and treated him much like a son.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:14

## Genesis 29:14–30 (id: 12576)

Jacob’s joyful prospect of marriage to the lovely Rachel became an occasion for Laban’s shrewdness and Jacob’s discipline. Jacob and his mother had deceived his father and brother to gain the blessing; now his mother’s brother deceived him. Jacob received a dose of his own duplicity through twenty years of labor, affliction, and deception in Laban’s service ([31:38](https://ref.ly/Gen31:38)). In God’s justice, people harvest what they plant ([Gal 6:7](https://ref.ly/Gal6:7)). Laban’s deception was perfectly designed to make Jacob aware of his own craftiness. God often brings people into the lives of believers to discipline them. But Jacob was tenacious, and God blessed him abundantly with a large family and many possessions ([30:25–43](https://ref.ly/Gen30:25-Gen30:43)) during this time of service.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:14–GEN 29:30

## Genesis 29:18 (id: 12578)

Seven years of service was a high bride\-price in the ancient world, but Rachel was beautiful (like Sarah and Rebekah), and Jacob was in love with her.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:18

## Genesis 29:23–26 (id: 12581)

Like Isaac, Jacob was plied with food and wine (cp. [27:25](https://ref.ly/Gen27:25)), deprived of sight in the darkness (cp. [27:1](https://ref.ly/Gen27:1)), baffled by clothing (cp. [27:15](https://ref.ly/Gen27:15)), and misled by touch (cp. [27:23](https://ref.ly/Gen27:23)). The marriage had been consummated ([29:23](https://ref.ly/Gen29:23); see [2:24](https://ref.ly/Gen2:24)), so Jacob was bound to Leah. • It’s not our custom here to marry off a younger daughter ahead of the firstborn: Laban’s words are a reminder of what Jacob did when he, the younger son, pretended to be his older brother to gain the blessing (ch [27](https://ref.ly/Gen27:1-Gen27:46)). Now Leah, the older sister, pretended to be the younger sister to get a husband. God gave the deceiver a dose of his own deception as a discipline in his life.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:23–GEN 29:26

## Genesis 29:28–30 (id: 12582)

After the bridal week ended ([29:27](https://ref.ly/Gen29:27)), Jacob also received Rachel as his wife, though he then would have to work an additional seven years (cp. [31:41](https://ref.ly/Gen31:41)). Laban seemed to have gained the upper hand.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:28–GEN 29:30

## Genesis 29:30 (id: 12583)

Jacob loved Rachel much more than Leah: Favoritism was an ongoing cause of dysfunction in Jacob’s family (cp. [25:28](https://ref.ly/Gen25:28); [37:3](https://ref.ly/Gen37:3)). Jacob’s favoritism had lasting effects: his family was never together, and their descendants, the tribes of Israel, were rarely unified.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:30

## Genesis 29:31–35 (id: 12585)

Leah’s first four sons were born in rapid succession, but Rachel could not conceive. She was barren, like Sarah and Rebekah (cp. [16:1](https://ref.ly/Gen16:1); [25:21](https://ref.ly/Gen25:21); see also [1 Sam 1:1–28](https://ref.ly/1Sam1:1-1Sam1:28); [Luke 1:5–25](https://ref.ly/Luke1:5-Luke1:25)). • Each name is a memorable wordplay on Leah’s experience and hopes.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:31–GEN 29:35

## Genesis 29:31–30.24 (id: 12584)

The rivalry between these sisters explains much of the later rivalry among their sons, and then among the tribes, just as the rivalry between Jacob and Laban foreshadowed conflict between Israel and the Arameans of Damascus ([2 Sam 8:5–6](https://ref.ly/2Sam8:5-2Sam8:6); [10:8–19](https://ref.ly/2Sam10:8-2Sam10:19); [1 Kgs 20:1–34](https://ref.ly/1Kgs20:1-1Kgs20:34); [2 Kgs 5:1–8:29](https://ref.ly/2Kgs5:1-2Kgs8:29); [13:1–25](https://ref.ly/2Kgs13:1-2Kgs13:25); [Isa 7:1–9](https://ref.ly/Isa7:1-Isa7:9)). • God champions the cause of the poor and oppressed; he exalted Leah, the despised first wife, as the first to become a mother. Judah’s kingly tribe and Levi’s priestly line came through her despite Jacob’s favoritism for Rachel and her children. Despite the tension and jealousy resulting from Laban’s treachery and Jacob’s favoritism, God still built Jacob’s family and brought about the births of the tribal ancestors.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:31–GEN 30:24

## Genesis 29:32 (id: 12586)

Reuben (Hebrew re’uben) sounds like the Hebrew for “He has seen my misery” (ra‘ah be‘onyi). His birth gave Leah consolation from God and hope for Jacob’s love. Jacob seems not to have seen her misery, but God did (cp. [16:14](https://ref.ly/Gen16:14); [24:62](https://ref.ly/Gen24:62); [25:11](https://ref.ly/Gen25:11)). The name was a reminder of God’s intervention.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:32

## Genesis 29:33 (id: 12587)

Leah named her second son Simeon. The name suggests that she had cried out to the Lord and was heard (cp. [16:11](https://ref.ly/Gen16:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:33

## Genesis 29:34 (id: 12588)

Leah named her third son Levi, hoping that her husband would become “attached” to her since she had given him three sons. This hope was not fulfilled.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:34

## Genesis 29:35 (id: 12589)

Leah reconciled herself to the reality that nothing would turn Jacob’s affections toward her. She named her fourth son Judah with the sentiment, “I will praise the Lord.” She seems to have given up on Jacob, taking her consolation from the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 29:35

## Genesis 30:1–2 (id: 12611)

In that culture, it was like death for a woman not to have children (cp. [1 Sam 1](https://ref.ly/1Sam1:1-1Sam1:28)); only God could open Rachel’s womb.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:1–GEN 30:2

## Genesis 30:1–8 (id: 12612)

Rachel’s naming of sons through Bilhah does not reflect faith as Leah’s namings had. Rachel felt wronged over the marriage and her barrenness. The names of Bilhah’s sons reflect Rachel’s bitter struggle with her sister and her feeling of some victory.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:1–GEN 30:8

## Genesis 30:3–4 (id: 12621)

Rachel’s decision to have children through her servant, and Jacob’s compliance, recall Sarai’s use of Hagar ([16:1–4](https://ref.ly/Gen16:1-Gen16:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:3–GEN 30:4

## Genesis 30:5–6 (id: 12629)

Dan means “he judged” or “he vindicated.” Rachel felt vindicated (Hebrew dananni) by Dan’s birth.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:5–GEN 30:6

## Genesis 30:7–8 (id: 12630)

The name Naphtali is related to the clause I have struggled hard (Hebrew naptule ’elohim niptalti, “I have struggled the struggles of God”).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:7–GEN 30:8

## Genesis 30:9 (id: 12631)

When Leah saw that she had stopped bearing children, she countered Rachel’s effort by giving her servant . . . to Jacob as a wife even though she already had four sons.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:9

## Genesis 30:10–13 (id: 12613)

*Gad* (meaning "fortune") was the name of a god associated with fortune. *Asher* (meaning "happy") was the name of a god associated with luck. These names show Leah's pagan background, but there is no indication she believed in these gods.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:10–GEN 30:13

## Genesis 30:14–17 (id: 12614)

People believed *mandrakes* could increase sexual desire and help women become pregnant (see [Song 7:13](https://ref.ly/Song7:13)). Rachel hoped they would help her become pregnant, so she made a deal with Leah. Rachel would let Leah spend one night with Jacob in exchange for mandrakes. Yet, Leah became pregnant, not Rachel.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:14–GEN 30:17

## Genesis 30:18 (id: 12615)

The name Issachar captures the sense of Jacob’s being hired ([30:16](https://ref.ly/Gen30:16)) and of the Lord’s rewarding Leah (Hebrew sekari, “my hire”).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:18

## Genesis 30:19–20 (id: 12616)

Zebulun means “honor” or “gift,” as in a dowry or tribute. Leah thought that God gave her Zebulun so that her husband would honor her. This hope never fully left her.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:19–GEN 30:20

## Genesis 30:21 (id: 12617)

Dinah was Jacob’s only daughter. See ch [34](https://ref.ly/Gen34:1-Gen34:31).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:21

## Genesis 30:22–24 (id: 12618)

Rachel finally gave birth to her own son, Joseph. His birth was brought about by God’s intervention, not by superstitious practices ([30:14–16](https://ref.ly/Gen30:14-Gen30:16)) or the social custom of giving servants as wives. • Removed (Hebrew ’asap, “take away”) sounds similar to Joseph (Hebrew yosep, “may he add”). Rachel rejoiced over Joseph’s birth, yet she prayed that the Lord would add yet another son to her family.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:22–GEN 30:24

## Genesis 30:25–34 (id: 12619)

After his fourteen years of service, Jacob asked Laban for permission to go home. The two bedouin leaders negotiated politely but remained cautiously on guard. Laban wanted to get more out of Jacob. Jacob wanted to gain his wages by selective breeding.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:25–GEN 30:34

## Genesis 30:27 (id: 12620)

I have become wealthy: God had prospered Laban through Jacob’s presence (see [22:18](https://ref.ly/Gen22:18)). Laban may have looked for omens, or simply have perceived what was happening. Since dark\-colored sheep ([30:32](https://ref.ly/Gen30:32)) were rare, a large number of them was considered an omen of God’s blessing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:27

## Genesis 30:30–33 (id: 12622)

Jacob agreed that God had blessed Laban through him, so he made a plan to gain something for himself. He proposed for his wages the rare black and multicolored goats and the speckled and spotted sheep that were born.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:30–GEN 30:33

## Genesis 30:32 (id: 12623)

As Abraham had done with Lot ([13:9](https://ref.ly/Gen13:9)), Jacob gave Laban what he valued most. White sheep were more common and more valuable than dark or multicolored sheep; as a man of faith, Jacob was willing to take the rejects (cp. [1 Cor 1:26](https://ref.ly/1Cor1:26)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:32

## Genesis 30:34–36 (id: 12624)

Laban verbally agreed with Jacob’s plan, but he tried to prevent Jacob from accruing wealth by removing animals from the flock that would fulfill the agreement. • Laban’s deception with his goats reminds us of Jacob’s deception of Esau (cp. [27:9](https://ref.ly/Gen27:9); see study note on 29:14–30).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:34–GEN 30:36
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 29:14-30 (ID: `12576@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 30:37 (id: 12626)

*exposing the white inner wood:* This clever wordplay explains the entire section. When Jacob *exposed the white* (Hebrew *laban*) streaks of wood underneath, he played the "white" game (the *Laban* game) and won. By outsmarting Laban ("Whitey"), Jacob's flocks increased and he became successful.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:37

## Genesis 30:37–43 (id: 12625)

God blessed Jacob despite Laban’s duplicity. Not to be outwitted, Jacob used selective breeding to acquire a flock, following the traditional belief that peeled sticks influenced the kind of animal that would be born. The peeled branches seemingly made his animals produce streaked and spotted young; Jacob later acknowledged that God had prospered him ([31:7–12](https://ref.ly/Gen31:7-Gen31:12)). Jacob gained stronger animals for himself and weaker ones for Laban ([30:41–42](https://ref.ly/Gen30:41-Gen30:42)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:37–GEN 30:43

## Genesis 30:42 (id: 12627)

Laban now received due recompense for his treatment of Jacob. Laban’s attempt to defraud Jacob resulted in Jacob’s coming out ahead, because God was at work in his life.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:42

## Genesis 30:43 (id: 12628)

Jacob became very wealthy, in fulfillment of God’s promises to him ([27:28](https://ref.ly/Gen27:28); [28:13–15](https://ref.ly/Gen28:13-Gen28:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 30:43

## Genesis 31:1–2 (id: 12633)

The animosity of Laban’s sons against Jacob grew because his flocks were multiplying faster than Laban’s. They were jealous of God’s blessing on Jacob and afraid that he would completely overrun them.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:1–GEN 31:2

## Genesis 31:1–21 (id: 12632)

Jacob’s return journey precipitated a confrontation with Laban that set a permanent boundary between Israel (Jacob) and Aram (Laban). God kept his word to Jacob by prospering him in Paddan\-aram and protecting him on his journey home.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:1–GEN 31:21

## Genesis 31:3 (id: 12644)

* *the land of your fathers* was Canaan, to which God had called Abraham ([12:1–7](https://ref.ly/Gen12:1-Gen12:7); [17:8](https://ref.ly/Gen17:8)).
* *Go back … I will be with you:* See study notes on 26:2–5; 28:12–15. God protected Jacob (also called “Israel,” [32:28](https://ref.ly/Gen32:28)) as he led his family back to the land God promised them. Later, God brought Israel back to Canaan after many years of slavery in Egypt.

    This return had many similarities to this passage:

    + God defeated foreign gods and beliefs.
        + He used dreams for rescue and protection.
        + He gave victory over threats.
        + He set boundaries between nations and tribes (see [Deuteronomy 32:8](https://ref.ly/Deut32:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:3
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 26:2-5 (ID: `12534@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Genesis 28:12-15 (ID: `12563@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 31:4–13 (id: 12645)

Jacob explained to his wives how God had blessed him despite Laban’s opposition. He was not sure they would want to leave Laban and go to Canaan. He wanted to take a willing family, so he had to make an effective appeal. He rehearsed God’s leading and provision over the years and then told them that he had to keep the vow he had made at Bethel ([28:20–22](https://ref.ly/Gen28:20-Gen28:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:4–GEN 31:13

## Genesis 31:14–16 (id: 12634)

The women responded immediately that they would go with Jacob because God had blessed him. They were very willing to leave Laban, who had squandered their wealth (the property that would have provided for them). They knew that what God had given to Jacob would also be theirs.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:14–GEN 31:16

## Genesis 31:17–21 (id: 12635)

Jacob left Laban secretly out of fear of reprisal ([31:31](https://ref.ly/Gen31:31)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:17–GEN 31:21

## Genesis 31:19–20 (id: 12636)

Rachel stole her father’s household idols: Rachel probably wanted to regain some of the assets Laban had squandered; possibly she also worshiped idols (cp. [35:2–4](https://ref.ly/Gen35:2-Gen35:4)). To have the idols may have signified claiming the family inheritance, as customs in subsequent periods indicate. Laban apparently felt vulnerable without them. Whatever her reasons, Rachel’s theft almost brought disaster on the fleeing family when Laban caught up with them. • A wordplay shows that Rachel and Jacob were very much alike—Rachel stole (Hebrew wattignob) Laban’s household gods, and Jacob outwitted (Hebrew wayyignob, “stole the heart of, deceived”) Laban.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:19–GEN 31:20

## Genesis 31:21 (id: 12637)

The journey took the family from Haran southwest to the land of Gilead, just east of the Jordan River in the north of today’s kingdom of Jordan.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:21

## Genesis 31:22–23 (id: 12638)

The theft of the idols ([31:19](https://ref.ly/Gen31:19)) was probably the main reason that Laban and his men chased Jacob. It was one thing for Jacob to take his family and flocks—Laban probably still believed they were all his—but another matter entirely to take his household gods. Laban may have feared that Jacob would return someday to claim all of Laban’s estate. When he failed to find the gods, he asked for a treaty to keep Jacob away ([31:43–53](https://ref.ly/Gen31:43-Gen31:53)). • It took Laban seven days to catch up with Jacob.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:22–GEN 31:23

## Genesis 31:24 (id: 12639)

leave Jacob alone! (literally *Do not speak to Jacob either good or evil*): God commanded Laban not to take justice into his own hands. When we try to enact our own sense of good and evil apart from God’s command, we always do evil (see study note on 2:9).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:24
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 2:9 (ID: `12418@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 31:25–30 (id: 12640)

The dispute between the two men used the language of legal controversies and lawsuits (see also [31:36](https://ref.ly/Gen31:36)). In his first argument, Laban presented himself as a wounded party that Jacob had robbed.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:25–GEN 31:30

## Genesis 31:32 (id: 12641)

Jacob, so convinced that he didn’t have the gods, used an oath that unwittingly put Rachel under a death sentence.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:32

## Genesis 31:33–35 (id: 12642)

Laban searched for the idols but found nothing. Laban never dreamed that a woman having her monthly period would desecrate the idols by sitting on them (cp. [Lev 15:19–24](https://ref.ly/Lev15:19-Lev15:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:33–GEN 31:35

## Genesis 31:36–42 (id: 12643)

Jacob retaliated by accusing Laban of false charges and humiliation. Laban now became the defendant, for his charges were demeaning and apparently groundless.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:36–GEN 31:42

## Genesis 31:40 (id: 12646)

Jacob, who preferred domestic life ([25:27](https://ref.ly/Gen25:27)), had for twenty years endured the rigors of the outdoors that Esau had loved.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:40

## Genesis 31:42 (id: 12647)

The God that Isaac feared (see textual note) was with Jacob ([31:3](https://ref.ly/Gen31:3)), had seen his hard work and faithfulness despite Laban’s abuse, and had rewarded Jacob. Laban’s dream only proved to Jacob that he was in the right.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:42

## Genesis 31:43–44 (id: 12648)

Laban pushed for a treaty to settle the dispute—he felt vulnerable, so he wanted to secure the borders. Jacob did not need a treaty, since God had provided for him and protected him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:43–GEN 31:44

## Genesis 31:45–48 (id: 12649)

The stone and the heap of stones were a monument to the border treaty between the two men, as a witness to future generations. Each man named the monument witness pile in his native language. It remained the perpetual border between Israel and the kingdom of Aram (Syria), two nations often at war.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:45–GEN 31:48

## Genesis 31:49 (id: 12650)

The witness pile was also called watchtower. God would watch over Jacob and Laban and keep them apart, for they could not trust each other.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:49

## Genesis 31:50–53 (id: 12651)

Laban added some face\-saving stipulations to the treaty, using many words to cover up his own untrustworthiness and portray Jacob as the unethical party. He even took credit for the monument Jacob had erected (this monument I have set, [31:51](https://ref.ly/Gen31:51)). The women and children would be much safer and better cared for with Jacob than they ever were with Laban.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 31:50–GEN 31:53

## Genesis 32:1 (id: 12653)
