God assured Jacob of his protection at a time when Jacob most needed such consolation. His journey was both a physical return to his homeland and a spiritual return to the land of God’s promised blessing. God protects his people and fulfills his plan.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:1

## Genesis 32:2 (id: 12662)

This is God’s camp! Jacob must have seen the angels that revealed God’s presence as a sign of protection, as with the earlier vision when he was departing the land ([28:10–22](https://ref.ly/Gen28:10-Gen28:22)). • Mahanaim (“two camps”): Jacob’s company and the company of angels were together in one place.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:2

## Genesis 32:3–5 (id: 12663)

Apparently inspired by the vision of angels ([32:1](https://ref.ly/Gen32:1), Hebrew mal’akim), Jacob sent messengers (mal’akim) into Edom to meet Esau.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:3–GEN 32:5

## Genesis 32:7–8 (id: 12667)

Jacob divided his company into two groups or camps (Hebrew makhanoth, related to “Mahanaim” in [32:2](https://ref.ly/Gen32:2)) because he was afraid, remembering Esau’s character and his threat to kill Jacob (see [25:25](https://ref.ly/Gen25:25); [27:41](https://ref.ly/Gen27:41)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:7–GEN 32:8

## Genesis 32:9–12 (id: 12668)

Jacob’s prayer is a wonderful example of how to address God. He based his appeal on God’s will, reminding God of his relationship with him, his command for him to return to the land, and his promise ([32:9](https://ref.ly/Gen32:9)). He had a correct attitude of genuine humility and total dependence on God ([32:10](https://ref.ly/Gen32:10)). Finally, he asked that God rescue him from his brother, and he repeated God’s promises (see [22:17](https://ref.ly/Gen22:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:9–GEN 32:12

## Genesis 32:13–21 (id: 12652)

Taking a large portion of the wealth God had blessed him with (some 550 animals), Jacob prepared a gift to appease Esau’s anger and gain his favor.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:13–GEN 32:21

## Genesis 32:22–24 (id: 12654)

The Jabbok River flows westward to the Jordan Valley, dividing the region of Bashan on the north from Gilead on the south. Wordplays on Jacob’s name and character preserve the memory of this encounter. Jacob (Hebrew ya‘aqob), while at Jabbok (Hebrew yabboq), wrestled (Hebrew wayye’abeq). Through his fight with an adversary to receive the blessing, Jacob’s name would be changed, and his deceptive striving would partially give way to faith as his way of life.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:22–GEN 32:24

## Genesis 32:22–32 (id: 12655)

Before Jacob returned to the land God had promised him, God met him, crippled him, and blessed him, changing his name to Israel. This episode was a significant turning point for him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:22–GEN 32:32

## Genesis 32:24 (id: 12656)

a man came: The narrative unfolds as the event did for Jacob. No details are given about the assailant, who later refused to identify himself ([32:29](https://ref.ly/Gen32:29)). • until the dawn: The darkness fit Jacob’s situation and increased the fear and uncertainty that seized him. In the darkness he had no idea who it was—it might have been one of Esau’s men, or Laban’s.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:24

## Genesis 32:25 (id: 12657)

he touched Jacob’s hip and wrenched it out of its socket: Jacob, the deceitful fighter, could fight no more. When his assailant fought him as man to man, Jacob could hold his own. But like so many of his own rivals, he had now more than met his match.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:25

## Genesis 32:26 (id: 12658)

At daybreak, the significance of this fight began to dawn on Jacob. He realized who his assailant was, and since it was futile to fight, he held on to obtain God’s blessing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:26

## Genesis 32:27 (id: 12659)

What is your name? The Lord’s question was really about Jacob’s character, not his identity (cp. [3:9](https://ref.ly/Gen3:9); [4:9](https://ref.ly/Gen4:9)). By giving his name, Jacob confessed his nature, his way of doing things as “Heel\-grabber, Deceiver, Usurper.” Before God would bless him, he had to acknowledge who he was, and then God would change his identity.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:27

## Genesis 32:28 (id: 12660)

Jacob: See study note on 25:26. • Israel (“God fights”): God first had to fight *with* him, but now God would fight *for* him. Jacob’s name was thus full of promise for Jacob and his descendants. • you have fought with God and with men: Through his entire life, Jacob had been seizing God’s blessing by his own abilities and by any means possible. Jacob knew the importance of the blessing, but he was too self\-sufficient and proud to let the blessing be given to him. He had been fighting God long before this encounter. • and have won: He had prevailed in his struggles with Esau and with Laban; now he prevailed in obtaining God’s blessing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:28
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 25:26 (ID: `12517@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 32:29 (id: 12661)

Jacob knew who was with him ([32:30](https://ref.ly/Gen32:30)); the request was his attempt to regain some control. God would not reveal his name, which cannot be had on demand.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:29

## Genesis 32:30 (id: 12664)

Peniel (which means “face of God”): The name shows that Jacob recognized the man as a manifestation of God (a *theophany*). • yet my life has been spared (or *and I have been rescued*): The saying probably meant that Jacob realized that his prayer to be rescued from Esau ([32:11](https://ref.ly/Gen32:11)) had been answered, for if he could meet God like this and walk away, he had nothing to fear from Esau. The saying may also reflect an ancient understanding that no one could see God and live (see [Exod 33:20](https://ref.ly/Exod33:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:30

## Genesis 32:31 (id: 12665)

God injured Jacob’s hip, thus curtailing his proud self\-sufficiency. Since the Lord had restricted his natural strength, Jacob would have to rely on the Lord with greater faith. He had thought that returning to his land would be a matter of outwitting his brother once again ([32:3–21](https://ref.ly/Gen32:3-Gen32:21)), but here at the land’s threshold he met its true proprietor. He would get the land, but only if God fought for him. Self\-sufficiency—trying to achieve the blessing by our own strength or by the ways of the world—will not suffice. If we persist, God may have to cripple our self\-sufficiency to make us trust him more.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:31

## Genesis 32:32 (id: 12666)

The story includes a dietary restriction for Israel that became a custom but was not put into law. This custom helped preserve the memory of the story. Observant Jews still refuse to eat the tendons of an animal’s hindquarters.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 32:32

## Genesis 33:1–2 (id: 12670)

Jacob’s identity had been changed ([32:27–28](https://ref.ly/Gen32:27-Gen32:28)), but he had not yet learned to live up to the new name; he still showed the favoritism that divides families. He lined up his family and his possessions in the order of their importance to him, with the slave wives and their children in front (to face danger first), Leah’s group behind them, and Rachel and Joseph in the back, where it was safest.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:1–GEN 33:2

## Genesis 33:1–17 (id: 12669)

Jacob’s long\-anticipated meeting with his brother Esau turned out far better than he had feared. Esau’s changed heart is an example of how “God fights” (See [32:28](https://ref.ly/Gen32:28)). Earlier, he had cared little about the birthright ([25:32–34](https://ref.ly/Gen25:32-Gen25:34)); now he cared little for old grudges. Jacob recognized that God had intervened.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:1–GEN 33:17

## Genesis 33:3–13 (id: 12677)

Even though Jacob had nothing to fear, he was afraid and tried to appease his brother. He assumed the role of a servant before royalty by bowing ([33:3](https://ref.ly/Gen33:3)), using an honorific title ([33:8](https://ref.ly/Gen33:8), [13](https://ref.ly/Gen33:13)), making introductions ([33:6–7](https://ref.ly/Gen33:6-Gen33:7)), and presenting gifts ([33:8](https://ref.ly/Gen33:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:3–GEN 33:13

## Genesis 33:4 (id: 12678)

Esau’s friendly greeting was an answer to prayer ([32:11](https://ref.ly/Gen32:11)). God had rescued Jacob from Esau’s revenge.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:4

## Genesis 33:5 (id: 12679)

your servant: In talking with his brother, Jacob continued to refer to himself as Esau’s servant (also [33:14](https://ref.ly/Gen33:14)) and to Esau as his lord ([33:8](https://ref.ly/Gen33:8), [13–15](https://ref.ly/Gen33:13-Gen33:15)); Esau called Jacob “my brother” ([33:9](https://ref.ly/Gen33:9)). Jacob was cautiously warding off any possible retaliation by reversing the words of the oracle ([25:23](https://ref.ly/Gen25:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:5

## Genesis 33:7 (id: 12680)

Among Jacob’s sons, only Joseph is named; he was Jacob’s favorite son and the recipient of the blessing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:7

## Genesis 33:10 (id: 12671)

Jacob knew that Esau’s friendly greeting was God’s work, secured at Peniel when he saw God face to face.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:10

## Genesis 33:11 (id: 12672)

this gift I have brought you (literally *my blessing*): Jacob perceived Esau as a threat and tried to appease him with a gift (cp. [2 Kgs 17:3–4](https://ref.ly/2Kgs17:3-2Kgs17:4); [18:7](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:7), [14](https://ref.ly/2Kgs18:14); [2 Chr 28:21](https://ref.ly/2Chr28:21)), perhaps in a guilty attempt to undo the past. Jacob would not take no for an answer.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:11

## Genesis 33:12–15 (id: 12673)

Despite Esau’s apparent magnanimity, Jacob was wary and cleverly avoided traveling with his brother. • I will meet you at Seir: Jacob’s lie manifests his old character, living by deception rather than by faith.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:12–GEN 33:15

## Genesis 33:16–17 (id: 12674)

Instead of following Esau south to Seir as promised, Jacob again deceived his brother, then headed in the opposite direction to Succoth, east of the Jordan River and north of the Jabbok.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:16–GEN 33:17

## Genesis 33:18–20 (id: 12675)

These verses form an epilogue to Jacob’s adventures outside the land. He returned in peace with a large family and many possessions. • Jacob, like Abraham, built an altar at Shechem (see [12:6–8](https://ref.ly/Gen12:6-Gen12:8)) and purchased land from the family of Hamor.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:18–GEN 33:20

## Genesis 33:20 (id: 12676)

El\-Elohe\-Israel: The name of the altar (“God, the God of Israel”) commemorated Jacob’s relationship with God. Jacob publicly proclaimed that God was his God, and that God had led him back to the land he would inherit.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 33:20

## Genesis 34:1–2 (id: 12681)

As far as we know, Dinah was Jacob’s only daughter ([30:21](https://ref.ly/Gen30:21)). • Shechem . . . seized her and raped her: Shechem violated and debased Dinah, so she had no chance for a proper marriage.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 34:1–GEN 34:2

## Genesis 34:1–31 (id: 12682)

Once Jacob and his family settled in the land, the Canaanite presence became a threat. This account is a stern warning to the Israelites about the possibility of their being defiled by the Canaanites. The nation of Israel was later commanded not to intermarry or make treaties with them, for they were a corrupt and corrupting people. This chapter implicitly warns against becoming familiar with the way they lived ([34:1–2](https://ref.ly/Gen34:1-Gen34:2)). It also taught Israel that in dealing with the Canaanites, they were to keep their integrity and not use the holy things of the covenant for deception and slaughter ([34:13](https://ref.ly/Gen34:13)); Israel’s reputation was at stake in the land ([34:30](https://ref.ly/Gen34:30)). For their ruthless violence, Simeon and Levi were passed over in the birthright blessing ([49:5–7](https://ref.ly/Gen49:5-Gen49:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 34:1–GEN 34:31

## Genesis 34:3–4 (id: 12686)

While Shechem’s intense feelings for Dinah made him willing to undergo significant hardship ([34:11–12](https://ref.ly/Gen34:11-Gen34:12), [17–18](https://ref.ly/Gen34:17-Gen34:18), [24](https://ref.ly/Gen34:24)), the way he showed his “love” for her was selfish, impetuous, and in violation of customary decency (cp. [Judg 14:2](https://ref.ly/Judg14:2); see [1 Cor 13:4–7](https://ref.ly/1Cor13:4-1Cor13:7); [1 Jn 4:10](https://ref.ly/1John4:10)). His character illustrated why Israel needed to remain separate from the Canaanites.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 34:3–GEN 34:4

## Genesis 34:5–7 (id: 12688)

Jacob’s response to this crime is surprising. When he heard that Dinah had been defiled, he said nothing about it until his sons came home. Because Jacob did not act, his sons did, though without the wisdom and integrity necessary for justice. Dinah’s full brothers were shocked and furious that Shechem had done such a disgraceful thing to Jacob’s family (literally *in Israel;* this is the first use of the name *Israel* to describe the family). This sexual crime was an outrage against the community of God’s people and deserved punishment, but the leader of the clan did nothing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 34:5–GEN 34:7

## Genesis 34:8–10 (id: 12689)

Hamor proposed an alliance of intermarriage with the prospect of mutual economic benefit, but God had already promised Jacob everything, including the land ([34:10](https://ref.ly/Gen34:10); see [14:21–24](https://ref.ly/Gen14:21-Gen14:24)). Hamor wanted to gain control of Israel’s wealth ([34:23](https://ref.ly/Gen34:23)); no good could come of trusting the defiling Canaanites. For some of these reasons, intermarriage with Canaanites was not allowed under the law (see [Exod 23:27–33](https://ref.ly/Exod23:27-Exod23:33); [Deut 7:1–5](https://ref.ly/Deut7:1-Deut7:5))—unless, of course, they came to faith (see [Josh 2:1–15](https://ref.ly/Josh2:1-Josh2:15); [6:23–25](https://ref.ly/Josh6:23-Josh6:25); [Matt 1:5](https://ref.ly/Matt1:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 34:8–GEN 34:10

## Genesis 34:13–17 (id: 12683)

Dinah’s brothers responded to the proposal without waiting for Jacob to respond, and they acted deceitfully. They may not have thought that Shechem and his people would ever agree to the rite of circumcision, but they knew what they would do if the Canaanites accepted ([34:25](https://ref.ly/Gen34:25)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 34:13–GEN 34:17

## Genesis 34:18–24 (id: 12684)

The Canaanites accepted the stipulation and every male in the town was circumcised. This was not just to allow Shechem to marry Dinah, but to give them the opportunity to acquire everything that Jacob possessed ([34:23](https://ref.ly/Gen34:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 34:18–GEN 34:24

## Genesis 34:25–29 (id: 12685)

When Simeon and Levi used circumcision to deceive and slaughter the Canaanites, they showed disdain for the sign of the covenant. Their slaughter of all the males and their plunder of the city was not justice, but brutal and excessive revenge. In their moral outrage and desire to right the wrong, they should have demanded compensation (see [Exod 22:16–17](https://ref.ly/Exod22:16-Exod22:17); [Deut 22:28–29](https://ref.ly/Deut22:28-Deut22:29)). Instead, their passionate act of rage cost them their birthright blessing ([Gen 49:5–7](https://ref.ly/Gen49:5-Gen49:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 34:25–GEN 34:29

## Genesis 34:30 (id: 12687)

Jacob responded again out of fear of what would happen to him, but God caused the people of this land to fear him instead ([35:5](https://ref.ly/Gen35:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 34:30

## Genesis 35:1–7 (id: 12691)

Jacob returned to Bethel, about fifteen miles south of Shechem, to complete the vows he had made there ([28:20–22](https://ref.ly/Gen28:20-Gen28:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:1–GEN 35:7

## Genesis 35:1–29 (id: 12690)

This chapter highlights God’s promises, Jacob’s vow, and the transition to Jacob’s sons’ carrying on the covenant. Deborah, Rachel, and Isaac all died, marking the end of an era and of the account of Isaac’s family ([25:19–35:29](https://ref.ly/Gen25:19-Gen35:29)). • Idols were removed ([35:1–4](https://ref.ly/Gen35:1-Gen35:4)) and pure worship was established ([35:6–7](https://ref.ly/Gen35:6-Gen35:7)). During this transition, the faith had to be revitalized so that the covenant could be carried forward by Jacob’s sons.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:1–GEN 35:29

## Genesis 35:2–4 (id: 12697)

Jacob had vowed wholehearted devotion to the Lord ([28:20–22](https://ref.ly/Gen28:20-Gen28:22)); establishing this required that his family remove all pagan idols and cease their devotion to other gods. God permits no rivals; only the Lord was to be their God (cp. [Josh 5:1–9](https://ref.ly/Josh5:1-Josh5:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:2–GEN 35:4

## Genesis 35:3 (id: 12702)

He has been with me wherever I have gone: God had fulfilled his promises ([28:15](https://ref.ly/Gen28:15); [31:3](https://ref.ly/Gen31:3)), so Jacob must fulfill his vow.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:3

## Genesis 35:5 (id: 12703)

a terror from God: People had heard about the massacre of Shechem ([34:25–30](https://ref.ly/Gen34:25-Gen34:30)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:5

## Genesis 35:6–7 (id: 12704)

Jacob built an altar at Bethel as God had instructed ([35:1](https://ref.ly/Gen35:1); cp. [12:8](https://ref.ly/Gen12:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:6–GEN 35:7

## Genesis 35:9–15 (id: 12705)

At Bethel, God confirmed the promise he had made there earlier; he reiterated Jacob’s change of name from Jacob to Israel as proof that the blessing had been given.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:9–GEN 35:15

## Genesis 35:11–12 (id: 12692)

God’s reference to himself as God Almighty assured Jacob that his promise could and would be fulfilled. At Bethel, God had promised that Jacob would have descendants in the land (cp. [28:13–14](https://ref.ly/Gen28:13-Gen28:14)); here he added that his descendants would include kings (see [17:6](https://ref.ly/Gen17:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:11–GEN 35:12

## Genesis 35:14–15 (id: 12693)

In fulfilling his vow ([28:20–22](https://ref.ly/Gen28:20-Gen28:22)), Jacob’s actions were almost identical to his actions in the earlier experience at Bethel (cp. [28:16–19](https://ref.ly/Gen28:16-Gen28:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:14–GEN 35:15

## Genesis 35:16–20 (id: 12694)

Benjamin’s birth completed the family, but it was a sorrowful event because Rachel died in childbirth (see study note on 31:32).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:16–GEN 35:20
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 31:32 (ID: `12641@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 35:18 (id: 12695)

Rachel found the name son of my sorrow appropriate to the situation, but Jacob did not want such a sad name for his son, so he changed it to son of my right hand. Jacob thus turned the day of sorrow into a day of hope that gave his son the prospect of success.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:18

## Genesis 35:19 (id: 12696)

Jacob did not carry Rachel’s body to the family tomb at Machpelah ([23:1–20](https://ref.ly/Gen23:1-Gen23:20); [25:9](https://ref.ly/Gen25:9); [49:30](https://ref.ly/Gen49:30); [50:13](https://ref.ly/Gen50:13)) but buried her in the territory that would be Benjamin’s (see [Josh 18:21–28](https://ref.ly/Josh18:21-Josh18:28); [1 Sam 10:2](https://ref.ly/1Sam10:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:19

## Genesis 35:20 (id: 12698)

it can be seen there to this day: This seems to be a later editorial comment by someone who was living in the land after the conquest and giving directions to the tomb (see Genesis Book Introduction, “Composition”).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:20

## Genesis 35:22 (id: 12699)

Reuben had intercourse with Bilhah, thus defiling his father’s marriage bed. Perhaps Reuben, as the oldest son, was trying to replace his father as head of the clan by a pagan procedure (cp. [2 Sam 16:15–22](https://ref.ly/2Sam16:15-2Sam16:22)), but by this action he lost his birthright (see [Gen 49:3–4](https://ref.ly/Gen49:3-Gen49:4)). • Jacob soon heard about it, but he again delayed his response (see [49:3–4](https://ref.ly/Gen49:3-Gen49:4); cp. [34:5](https://ref.ly/Gen34:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:22

## Genesis 35:23–26 (id: 12700)

The twelve sons became leaders of the twelve tribes of Israel. Their names are the firstfruits of the nation.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:23–GEN 35:26

## Genesis 35:27–29 (id: 12701)

Esau and Jacob (listed here in ordinary birth order) came together—probably for the first time since they had reconciled ([33:16–17](https://ref.ly/Gen33:16-Gen33:17))—to bury Isaac in Hebron (see [13:18](https://ref.ly/Gen13:18); [23:1–2](https://ref.ly/Gen23:1-Gen23:2), [17–19](https://ref.ly/Gen23:17-Gen23:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 35:27–GEN 35:29

## Genesis 36:1–8 (id: 12707)

The account of Esau stresses two points. First, Esau’s sons were born . . . in the land of Canaan ([36:5](https://ref.ly/Gen36:5)) before he moved to Seir ([36:8](https://ref.ly/Gen36:8)). Jacob’s children, by contrast, were almost all born outside the land but then moved into it. God was giving the land to Jacob and his descendants and so made room for them by providing for Esau in a different place. Second, Esau’s other name was Edom. Israel often struggled with the Edomites (see [1 Sam 21:7](https://ref.ly/1Sam21:7); [22:9–22](https://ref.ly/1Sam22:9-1Sam22:22); [Obad 1](https://ref.ly/Obad1:1-Obad1:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:1–GEN 36:8

## Genesis 36:1–43 (id: 12706)

The book turns to the accounts of Isaac’s sons, concluding the unchosen line of Esau (ch [36](https://ref.ly/Gen36:1-Gen36:43)) before proceeding with the chosen line of Jacob (ch [37](https://ref.ly/Gen37:1-Gen37:36)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:1–GEN 36:43

## Genesis 36:2 (id: 12711)

Oholibamah was a great\-granddaughter of Seir the Horite, whose descendants lived in Edom when Esau went to live there ([36:20](https://ref.ly/Gen36:20), [25](https://ref.ly/Gen36:25)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:2

## Genesis 36:2–3 (id: 12709)

Esau’s three wives—Adah, Oholibamah, and Basemath—are listed. Two of these wives’ names are different from those listed earlier ([26:34](https://ref.ly/Gen26:34); [28:9](https://ref.ly/Gen28:9)). Perhaps the others died, or Esau favored these three among a total of six, or these were just different names for the same three. There is not enough information to decide.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:2–GEN 36:3

## Genesis 36:7–8 (id: 12715)

Esau, like Lot, left for the eastern land (cp. [13:5–6](https://ref.ly/Gen13:5-Gen13:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:7–GEN 36:8

## Genesis 36:9–14 (id: 12716)

The descendants of Esau’s five sons are named.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:9–GEN 36:14

## Genesis 36:9–43 (id: 12717)

This passage begins a second account of Esau’s descendants ([36:1](https://ref.ly/Gen36:1)); it traces the family to subsequent generations and alliances.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:9–GEN 36:43

## Genesis 36:15–19 (id: 12708)

Thirteen of Esau’s descendants had positions as leaders of various clans. A picture begins to emerge of Esau as a grand overlord of tribes (cp.[36:40–43](https://ref.ly/Gen36:40-Gen36:43)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:15–GEN 36:19

## Genesis 36:20–30 (id: 12710)

Seir the Horite was an early inhabitant of the land; his descendants populated the region until Esau moved in and displaced them ([Deut 2:12](https://ref.ly/Deut2:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:20–GEN 36:30

## Genesis 36:31 (id: 12713)

before any king ruled over the Israelites: This editorial note was probably inserted into the text during Israel’s monarchy (see study note on 35:20).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:31
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 35:20 (ID: `12698@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 36:31–39 (id: 12712)

It is not clear how these kings of Edom were related to Esau. The clans in Edom followed the same pattern of organization as the later tribes of Israel. They eventually chose a king from one of their tribes and carried on a line of succession from him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:31–GEN 36:39

## Genesis 36:40–43 (id: 12714)

These chiefs descended from Esau, who was a great and powerful overlord. As father of the Edomites, he ruled over clans and regions, with eleven chiefs descended from him. By separating from Jacob, Esau was beginning to shake Jacob’s yoke from his neck ([27:39–40](https://ref.ly/Gen27:39-Gen27:40)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 36:40–GEN 36:43

## Genesis 37:1 (id: 12723)

In ch [36](https://ref.ly/Gen36:1-Gen36:43), Esau was well on his way to power and prosperity; by contrast, Jacob, still waiting for the promise, settled in the land as a foreigner, like his father. He was still a temporary resident with a single family. Worldly greatness often comes more swiftly than spiritual greatness. Waiting for the promised spiritual blessing while others prosper is a test of patience, faith, and perseverance.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:1

## Genesis 37:2 (id: 12731)

The account of Jacob and his family tells of Joseph and his brothers. Jacob is still prominent, but the focus is on Joseph, who is introduced as an obedient seventeen\-year\-old son. • Joseph reported . . . the bad things his brothers were doing: Bringing a bad report has never been popular, but it was the right thing to do and shows that Joseph was faithful from the beginning. As the story progresses, we see more of his brothers’ wickedness displayed until, like Cain, they tried to eliminate the brother who pleased God.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:2

## Genesis 37:2–50.26 (id: 12724)

The story of Joseph and his brothers comprises a separate unit in Genesis, distinct in tone and emphasis from the preceding material. It traces one continuous series of episodes with Joseph at their center. • Cycles of repeated motifs structure the entire Joseph account. The themes are closely related to those found in wisdom books such as Proverbs, Ecclesiastes, and Daniel. Wisdom literature assures the faithful that God brings good out of evil and joy out of pain—if not in this life, then certainly in the life to come. Though the wicked may prosper for a time, the righteous should hold fast to their integrity because a higher, more enduring principle of life is realized through obedience to God. Everyone who aspires to leadership in God’s plan should observe how wisdom led to Joseph’s success. Christ Jesus embodied the life of wisdom portrayed here as no one else could, for he is the wisdom of God. • Judah is also an important character in the story. He began as irresponsible and mean\-spirited as his brothers; but he truly repented, put his life on the line to ransom a child for his father, and received a very important inheritance.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:2

## Genesis 37:3 (id: 12737)

Jacob: Hebrew *Israel;* also in [37:13](https://ref.ly/Gen37:13). • Jacob loved Joseph more because Joseph had been born to him in his old age and because he was the first son of his favorite wife, Rachel. • Jacob gave Joseph a beautiful robe to demonstrate that he intended to grant him the largest portion of the inheritance.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:3

## Genesis 37:4 (id: 12738)

Jacob’s favoritism toward Joseph inflamed his other sons’ hatred of their brother. Just as Isaac’s and Rebekah’s favoritism had separated their family, Jacob’s favoritism would separate him from his son Joseph.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:4

## Genesis 37:5–11 (id: 12739)

God confirmed his choice of this faithful son as the eventual leader of the whole family through two symbolic dreams. This is the first of three dream sequences in chs [37–50](https://ref.ly/Gen37:1-Gen50:26) (see [40:1–41:36](https://ref.ly/Gen40:1-Gen41:36); cp. [15:13](https://ref.ly/Gen15:13); [20:3](https://ref.ly/Gen20:3); [28:12–15](https://ref.ly/Gen28:12-Gen28:15); [31:24](https://ref.ly/Gen31:24)). Dreams carried weight as a form of divine communication, especially if the dream revelation was given twice.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:5–GEN 37:11

## Genesis 37:7 (id: 12740)

The bundles of grain hint at how Joseph’s authority over his family would be achieved (see [42:1–3](https://ref.ly/Gen42:1-Gen42:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:7

## Genesis 37:8 (id: 12741)

The brothers’ angry response to the revelation, in contrast to Joseph’s honesty and faithfulness, clearly demonstrates why they were not chosen for leadership: Leaders in God’s plan cannot be consumed with jealousy and hatred. In their anger, they missed an important part of the revelation—they too would be rulers (stars, [37:9](https://ref.ly/Gen37:9)) who would productively bind their sheaves ([37:7](https://ref.ly/Gen37:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:8

## Genesis 37:9 (id: 12742)

The sun, moon, and eleven stars: Astrological symbols often represent rulers. The dream predicted Joseph’s elevation to a position of authority over the whole clan of Israel.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:9

## Genesis 37:10 (id: 12718)

your mother and I: Joseph’s birth mother, Rachel, was dead ([35:19](https://ref.ly/Gen35:19)). Leah was now the matriarch of the clan.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:10

## Genesis 37:11 (id: 12719)

His brothers hated Joseph because they were jealous of him. Rather than recognize the hand of God, the brothers tried to prevent the dream from being fulfilled ([37:18–36](https://ref.ly/Gen37:18-Gen37:36)). These actions show that they were not fit to lead the household of faith. God’s sovereign choice of a leader, especially if the one chosen is young or appears unqualified, often brings out the true colors of those who have to submit to that leader’s authority. The brothers represent people throughout history who have been driven by envy and malice because they were not committed to doing the Lord’s will. • Jacob wondered what the dreams meant because he knew that God would choose the next leader, that God could choose the younger son to rule over the older sons, and that God could reveal all this in dreams.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:11

## Genesis 37:12–13 (id: 12720)

It was foolish for Jacob to send Joseph on such a mission, knowing how the brothers felt about him. Joseph obeyed his father by going to find his brothers despite their hatred for him.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:12–GEN 37:13

## Genesis 37:14–17 (id: 12721)

The brothers ranged far and wide. Shechem was about fifty miles from Hebron, and Dothan another fifteen miles beyond Shechem.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:14–GEN 37:17

## Genesis 37:18–20 (id: 12722)

When Joseph’s brothers saw him coming, they devised a plot to kill . . . the dreamer and end his dreams. Earlier, they had unjustly killed the men of Shechem to avenge their sister (ch [34](https://ref.ly/Gen34:1-Gen34:31)); in the region of Shechem, they now plotted to unjustly kill their own brother out of envy.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:18–GEN 37:20

## Genesis 37:21–24 (id: 12725)

Reuben was perhaps trying to get back into his father’s good graces ([35:22](https://ref.ly/Gen35:22)) by exercising the leadership of the firstborn (cp. [42:22](https://ref.ly/Gen42:22)). Reuben succeeded in saving Joseph’s life, but he failed to earn his father’s favor ([37:29–30](https://ref.ly/Gen37:29-Gen37:30)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:21–GEN 37:24

## Genesis 37:23 (id: 12726)

The recurring motif of changed clothes signifies changes in status, position, and authority (see [37:3](https://ref.ly/Gen37:3), [23](https://ref.ly/Gen37:23); [38:14](https://ref.ly/Gen38:14), [19](https://ref.ly/Gen38:19); [39:15–18](https://ref.ly/Gen39:15-Gen39:18); [41:14](https://ref.ly/Gen41:14), [42](https://ref.ly/Gen41:42)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:23

## Genesis 37:25–28 (id: 12727)

The Ishmaelite traders were descendants of the slave child who was cast out for mocking Isaac. Now they would enslave Joseph. When he was sold for twenty pieces of silver and carried to Egypt, he was at least preserved alive.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:25–GEN 37:28

## Genesis 37:26 (id: 12728)

Judah began to exercise leadership that he would continue to develop as events unfolded (see [43:8–10](https://ref.ly/Gen43:8-Gen43:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:26

## Genesis 37:28 (id: 12729)

the Ishmaelites, who were Midianite traders (literally *the Midianite traders*): Ishmaelites were descendants of Abraham through Hagar ([16:5](https://ref.ly/Gen16:5)), while Midianites were descendants of Abraham through Keturah ([25:1–2](https://ref.ly/Gen25:1-Gen25:2)). The term *Ishmaelite* may have described bedouin tribes generally. The Midianites might also have been traveling with a separate caravan of *Ishmaelite traders* ([37:27](https://ref.ly/Gen37:27)). • Kidnapping (see [40:15](https://ref.ly/Gen40:15)) would become a capital offense (see [Exod 21:16](https://ref.ly/Exod21:16)) in Jewish law.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:28

## Genesis 37:29–30 (id: 12730)

When Reuben returned, he tore his clothes in grief. His attempt to restore his relationship with his father by saving Joseph had gone awry.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:29–GEN 37:30

## Genesis 37:31–35 (id: 12732)

The old family propensity for deception seized the brothers’ imagination. Jacob had killed a young goat to deceive Isaac ([27:5–17](https://ref.ly/Gen27:5-Gen27:17)); now Jacob’s sons deceived him with a goat (see study note on 30:34–36).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:31–GEN 37:35
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 30:34-36 (ID: `12624@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 37:32 (id: 12733)

your son: In their cold hatred, the brothers did not refer to Joseph by name or acknowledge him as their brother (see [21:10](https://ref.ly/Gen21:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:32

## Genesis 37:33 (id: 12734)

recognized: Cp. [27:23](https://ref.ly/Gen27:23).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:33

## Genesis 37:34–35 (id: 12735)

Jacob tore his clothes and dressed himself in burlap: These were signs of great distress and mourning (see [44:13](https://ref.ly/Gen44:13); [Job 1:20](https://ref.ly/Job1:20); [16:15](https://ref.ly/Job16:15)). Jacob was devastated and refused to be comforted. The treachery thus affected everyone in his family.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:34–GEN 37:35

## Genesis 37:36 (id: 12736)

sold Joseph to Potiphar: Joseph found himself in a place of service that seemed congruent with his rise to authority, yet he still faced more testing.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 37:36

## Genesis 38:1–30 (id: 12743)

The story of Judah and Tamar is a carefully placed interlude; it reports what was happening in the family of Judah, who would later rise to prominence, and it shows the beginnings of assimilation with the people of the land to help explain why God sent the family to Egypt (chs [39–47](https://ref.ly/Gen39:1-Gen47:31)). The Egyptians were strict separatists ([43:32](https://ref.ly/Gen43:32)); the Israelites would retain their unique identity better in Egypt than in Canaan.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:1–GEN 38:30

## Genesis 38:7 (id: 12755)

That Er was a wicked man is not surprising, since his mother was a Canaanite and his father a wayward Israelite.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:7

## Genesis 38:7–10 (id: 12754)

In this story, the Lord is mentioned as the sovereign judge who took the lives of Judah’s evil sons.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:7–GEN 38:10

## Genesis 38:8 (id: 12756)

as our law requires. . . . You must produce an heir for your brother: The custom that informs this episode is the law for levirate marriage (Latin levir, “husband’s brother”). By this custom, which was later incorporated into God’s law for Israel ([Deut 25:5–10](https://ref.ly/Deut25:5-Deut25:10)), if a man died childless, his brother or nearest relative would marry his widow to produce a child who would carry on the family name of the deceased and inherit his property. Apparently, the near kinsman had a right to refuse, but he would be disgraced in the family for refusing to perpetuate his brother’s name.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:8

## Genesis 38:9–10 (id: 12757)

Onan married Tamar, but was not willing to provide an heir for his brother. He would have sex with Tamar but not fulfill his responsibility to his dead brother ([38:8](https://ref.ly/Gen38:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:9–GEN 38:10

## Genesis 38:11 (id: 12744)

Judah is now presented as the model for his sons’ behavior—he, too, was unfaithful to his levirate responsibility to Tamar as next kinsman (see study note on 38:8). Judah and his sons were far too Canaanite in their ways (see study note on 38:27–30; contrast Boaz, [Ruth 3–4](https://ref.ly/Ruth3:1-Ruth4:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:11
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 38:27-30 (ID: `12752@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Genesis 38:8 (ID: `12756@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 38:12–13 (id: 12745)

Without a marriage, the family’s future was in jeopardy. • Judah’s wife died: This made Judah available to fulfill the responsibility of providing an heir.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:12–GEN 38:13

## Genesis 38:14–19 (id: 12746)

Tamar realized that she would have to take matters into her own hands if the family were to have a future. Tamar acted in keeping with the levirate custom (see study note on 38:8) out of loyalty to her deceased husband. She had a legal right to an heir by Judah’s son or by Judah, so she lured her father\-in\-law into having sex with her. Jacob’s family was deceived again, this time by a Canaanite daughter\-in\-law.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:14–GEN 38:19
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 38:8 (ID: `12756@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 38:17 (id: 12747)

It would be normal for Tamar to ask for a pledge if the man did not have the money to pay. A woman in such a position would not trust anyone to send the money.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:17

## Genesis 38:18 (id: 12748)

identification seal: A stone or metal cylinder was engraved with distinctive designs and was usually worn around the neck on a cord; when rolled onto clay or wax, it left a distinct impression.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:18

## Genesis 38:20–23 (id: 12749)

Judah had gone in to Tamar as a regular prostitute (Hebrew zonah, [38:15](https://ref.ly/Gen38:15)), whereas Hirah was mistakenly looking for a shrine prostitute (Hebrew qedeshah), of which there were none.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:20–GEN 38:23

## Genesis 38:24–26 (id: 12750)

Judah played the hypocrite when he condemned Tamar to death for adultery. When she produced the seal and cord and walking stick that identified him as the father, he withdrew the condemnation.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:24–GEN 38:26

## Genesis 38:26 (id: 12751)

She is more righteous than I am: Judah acknowledged that he had shirked his responsibility to provide an heir. It was sinful for Judah to go to a prostitute, but Tamar had a legal right to be the mother of Judah’s child and had acted on that right. In the book of Ruth, the elders analogously blessed the marriage of Boaz and Ruth, praying that God would make Ruth like Tamar ([Ruth 4:12](https://ref.ly/Ruth4:12); cp. [Matt 1:3](https://ref.ly/Matt1:3), [5](https://ref.ly/Matt1:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:26

## Genesis 38:27–30 (id: 12752)

Judah’s line continued because of Tamar. The twins replaced Judah’s two slain sons ([38:7](https://ref.ly/Gen38:7), [10](https://ref.ly/Gen38:10)); their birth was similar to the birth of Jacob and Esau ([25:21–26](https://ref.ly/Gen25:21-Gen25:26)) in that the “red” one was born first, but the other son pushed past him in later life. Jacob’s gaining the right to rule over his older brother ([27:29](https://ref.ly/Gen27:29)) seemed to be relived in Judah’s line. The line was carried on through Perez and not through the elder son Shelah, whom he had gone to such lengths to protect ([38:11](https://ref.ly/Gen38:11); see [1 Chr 4:21](https://ref.ly/1Chr4:21)), nor through the elder twin Zerah (see [Ruth 4:13–22](https://ref.ly/Ruth4:13-Ruth4:22); [Matt 1:3](https://ref.ly/Matt1:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:27–GEN 38:30

## Genesis 38:29–30 (id: 12753)

Perez pushed past his brother, just as Joseph would soon do in relation to his brothers (chs [39–47](https://ref.ly/Gen39:1-Gen47:31)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 38:29–GEN 38:30

## Genesis 39:1–23 (id: 12758)

Joseph’s integrity in Potiphar’s service contrasts with Judah’s moral failure (ch [38](https://ref.ly/Gen38:1-Gen38:30)). God was with Joseph ([39:2–3](https://ref.ly/Gen39:2-Gen39:3), [21](https://ref.ly/Gen39:21), [23](https://ref.ly/Gen39:23)) and enabled him to prosper and be a blessing (see study notes on 12:1–9; 28:16–22) despite his slavery and imprisonment.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 39:1–GEN 39:23
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 12:1-9 (ID: `12287@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Genesis 28:16-22 (ID: `12566@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 39:1–47.31 (id: 12759)

Joseph began as a slave, alienated from his brothers and separated from his father; he ended as Pharaoh’s viceroy. Through the trips to Egypt, the covenant family went from the brink of apostasy, divided by jealousy and deception, to being reconciled and united by Judah’s intercession and Joseph’s forgiveness.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 39:1–GEN 39:23

## Genesis 39:2 (id: 12763)

God is mentioned for the first time (apart from [38:7–10](https://ref.ly/Gen38:7-Gen38:10)) since Jacob built his altar in Bethel ([35:1–15](https://ref.ly/Gen35:1-Gen35:15)); his covenant name, “the Lord,” is used for the first time since Jacob left Laban ([31:49](https://ref.ly/Gen31:49)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 39:2

## Genesis 39:5 (id: 12764)

God began to bless Egypt through Joseph (see [22:18](https://ref.ly/Gen22:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 39:5

## Genesis 39:6–10 (id: 12765)

One of the major motifs of wisdom literature (see study note on 37:2–50:26) is to warn young people that immorality will lead them to disaster (cp. [Prov 5–7](https://ref.ly/Prov5:1-Prov7:24)). Joseph was able to resist temptation because he had godly wisdom—he was guided by the fear of the Lord ([Prov 1:7](https://ref.ly/Prov1:7); [9:10](https://ref.ly/Prov9:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 39:6–GEN 39:10
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 37:2-50.26 (ID: `12724@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 39:14 (id: 12760)

Though Potiphar’s wife was addressing slaves, she appealed to them as fellow Egyptians (us) to enlist them as witnesses against the despised Hebrew (see [43:32](https://ref.ly/Gen43:32)) who had won Potiphar’s trust.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 39:14

## Genesis 39:19–20 (id: 12761)

This was the second time that Joseph, while faithfully doing the right thing, was thrown into bondage with his clothing used deceptively as evidence (cp. [37:23–24](https://ref.ly/Gen37:23-Gen37:24), [31–33](https://ref.ly/Gen37:31-Gen37:33)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 39:19–GEN 39:20

## Genesis 39:21–23 (id: 12762)

Joseph thrived in prison because God was with him. Each time Joseph prospered, he was put in charge of something.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 39:21–GEN 39:23

## Genesis 40:1–4 (id: 12788)

Joseph was so faithful and trustworthy that the captain of the guard trusted him with the care of two of Pharaoh’s chief . . . officials.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 40:1–GEN 40:4

## Genesis 40:1–23 (id: 12787)

Joseph did not lose faith in God’s promises, as evidenced by his readiness to interpret the dreams of two prisoners. He was still convinced that God’s revelation in his own two dreams ([37:5–11](https://ref.ly/Gen37:5-Gen37:11)) was true, and he had not abandoned hope that they would be fulfilled. When the fellow prisoners’ dreams were fulfilled exactly as Joseph said, this confirmed that his previous dreams were from God.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 40:1–GEN 40:23

## Genesis 40:5–8 (id: 12792)

Pharaoh’s cup\-bearer and baker looked worried because they knew that their futures were somehow bound up in these ominous and disturbing dreams that they could not understand. • Joseph still had faith that interpreting dreams is God’s business and that he would understand them with God’s help. He knew he had not misinterpreted his own dreams.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 40:5–GEN 40:8

## Genesis 40:9–19 (id: 12793)

Joseph listened to the dreams and offered their interpretations. These dreams were not trivial; they were ominous warnings from God about what everyone was going to face. These two dreams prepared for Pharaoh’s two dreams, which revealed the periods of life and death that the nation would soon experience.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 40:9–GEN 40:19

## Genesis 40:14–15 (id: 12789)

Because he knew that the chief cup\-bearer was going back into Pharaoh’s personal service, Joseph saw an opportunity to seek his own release from prison.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 40:14–GEN 40:15

## Genesis 40:20–22 (id: 12790)

The interpretations Joseph gave the prisoners proved true. The death of the chief baker speaks of the harsh realities of life in ancient Egypt, with a king whose word was his land’s highest law.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 40:20–GEN 40:22

## Genesis 40:23 (id: 12791)

The cup\-bearer . . . forgot all about Joseph, but God did not forget him. Joseph’s faith was about to be rewarded (ch [41](https://ref.ly/Gen41:1-Gen41:57)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 40:23

## Genesis 41:1–4 (id: 12795)

Pharaoh’s first dream was about cows. Cows liked to stand half\-submerged among the reeds in the Nile River to take refuge from the heat and flies. They would come out of the water to find pasture. The second group of cows disturbed Pharaoh because they were scrawny yet able to swallow the fat cows.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:1–GEN 41:4

## Genesis 41:1–46 (id: 12794)

God had used two dreams to identify Joseph as a leader among his brothers ([37:5–11](https://ref.ly/Gen37:5-Gen37:11)). He used two dreams to test Joseph’s faith in prison ([40:5–14](https://ref.ly/Gen40:5-Gen40:14)). Now he would use two dreams to elevate Joseph from prison to preeminence. Joseph had repeatedly proven faithful in small matters; now he would be put in charge of great things.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:1–GEN 41:46

## Genesis 41:5–7 (id: 12809)

Pharaoh’s second dream carried a similar message. Seven plump … heads of grain on a single stalk were swallowed up by seven shriveled and withered heads that sprouted after them.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:5–GEN 41:7

## Genesis 41:8 (id: 12812)

The magicians and wise men belonged to a guild of supposed experts in spiritual matters, including dreams and visions (cp. [Exod 8:18–19](https://ref.ly/Exod8:18-Exod8:19); [Dan 2:10–11](https://ref.ly/Dan2:10-Dan2:11)), but they could not interpret these dreams. God used an Israelite slave to confound the wisdom of the world (cp. [Dan 2](https://ref.ly/Dan2:1-Dan2:49)). However powerful a nation becomes, it is still under God’s sovereign control ([Dan 2:20–23](https://ref.ly/Dan2:20-Dan2:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:8

## Genesis 41:9–13 (id: 12813)

The chief cup\-bearer finally remembered Joseph and testified that his interpretations were true.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:9–GEN 41:13

## Genesis 41:14–15 (id: 12796)

Pharaoh immediately summoned Joseph from prison to interpret his dreams. • He shaved, as was the Egyptian custom.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:14–GEN 41:15

## Genesis 41:16 (id: 12797)

Joseph knew that only God could tell what Pharaoh’s dreams meant (cp.[40:8](https://ref.ly/Gen40:8)), and he was confident that God would do so, because he had given the dreams for a purpose ([41:25](https://ref.ly/Gen41:25), [28](https://ref.ly/Gen41:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:16

## Genesis 41:17–24 (id: 12798)

Pharaoh recounted his dreams and testified that no human wisdom could interpret them.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:17–GEN 41:24

## Genesis 41:25–32 (id: 12799)

Both dreams predicted that seven years of abundant crops would be followed by seven years of severe famine.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:25–GEN 41:32

## Genesis 41:32 (id: 12800)

The two similar dreams confirmed that the message was decreed by God and would soon . . . happen, just as the dreams of the two prisoners were quickly fulfilled ([40:5–23](https://ref.ly/Gen40:5-Gen40:23)). Joseph’s own two dreams ([37:5–11](https://ref.ly/Gen37:5-Gen37:11)) were about to come true as well ([41:37–46](https://ref.ly/Gen41:37-Gen41:46); [42:6–9](https://ref.ly/Gen42:6-Gen42:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:32

## Genesis 41:33–36 (id: 12801)

God’s revelation demanded a response—it was not given just to satisfy curiosity about the future. Joseph’s advice about planning and preparing showed that he was the kind of intelligent and wise man that Pharaoh needed ([41:37–40](https://ref.ly/Gen41:37-Gen41:40)). • Joseph instituted central planning and control with a supervisor, local managers, a 20 percent tax on grain, and a rationing system. Later wisdom literature (see study note on 37:2–50:26) teaches the principle of planning ahead rather than living just for the moment (see [Prov 6:6–8](https://ref.ly/Prov6:6-Prov6:8); [27:12](https://ref.ly/Prov27:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:33–GEN 41:36
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 37:2-50.26 (ID: `12724@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 41:37–40 (id: 12802)

Pharaoh recognized that Joseph was the man for the job; he had the spirit of God and was intelligent and wise. God showed his sovereign rule in Egypt; Israelites who later read the account could be confident that God would save them as he had promised.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:37–GEN 41:40

## Genesis 41:41–46 (id: 12803)

Joseph was made the acting ruler or manager of Egypt.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:41–GEN 41:46

## Genesis 41:42 (id: 12804)

Pharaoh’s signet ring had a seal used for signing documents. The seal was impressed in soft clay, which hardened and left a permanent impression of the ruler’s signature, which carried his authority. Numerous seals of this type have been found in archaeological digs. • The linen clothing and gold chain signified Joseph’s new status as ruler.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:42

## Genesis 41:43–44 (id: 12805)

Pharaoh made Joseph second\-in\-command; all the people had to submit to him. Cp. [Ps 105:16–22](https://ref.ly/Ps105:16-Ps105:22).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:43–GEN 41:44

## Genesis 41:45 (id: 12806)

As token of Joseph’s new status, Pharaoh gave him an Egyptian name and a wife from a high\-ranking family. • On was a center for sun worship that came to be known as Heliopolis (“sun city”).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:45

## Genesis 41:46 (id: 12807)

He was thirty years old: It had been approximately thirteen years since his brothers had sold Joseph into slavery ([37:2](https://ref.ly/Gen37:2)). • he inspected the entire land of Egypt: As a wise manager, his first priority was to learn the scope of his responsibilities.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:46

## Genesis 41:47–57 (id: 12808)

Pharaoh’s dreams were fulfilled in keeping with Joseph’s interpretation.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:47–GEN 41:57

## Genesis 41:50–52 (id: 12810)

In spite of his position and authority, Joseph never abandoned his heritage; he gave Hebrew names to his two sons. • Manasseh: Joseph’s prosperity and success made him “forget” the misery of separation from his family. • Ephraim: In so naming him, Joseph proclaimed his gratitude to God for making him “fruitful” in Egypt.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:50–GEN 41:52

## Genesis 41:53–57 (id: 12811)

Joseph’s wisdom paid off, for the seven years of plenty were followed by seven years of severe famine, but throughout Egypt there was plenty of food. Joseph had grain to sell to the Egyptians and to people from other countries as well.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 41:53–GEN 41:57

## Genesis 42:1–44.34 (id: 12814)

Joseph did several unusual things to his brothers when they came looking for grain. The last time Joseph had been with them, they were filled with jealousy, hatred, and anger; they attempted to destroy their brother, and they deceived their father. Joseph put them through various tests, similar to the trying situations they had put him through, to see if they had changed.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:1–GEN 44:34

## Genesis 42:1–47.31 (id: 12815)

God used the famine to bring Israel to Egypt under Joseph’s rule, thus fulfilling two prophecies ([15:13](https://ref.ly/Gen15:13); [37:7–11](https://ref.ly/Gen37:7-Gen37:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:1–GEN 42:2

## Genesis 42:4 (id: 12829)

Jacob may have believed that Benjamin would not be safe with his brothers.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:4

## Genesis 42:6–7 (id: 12830)

Joseph’s first dream ([37:5–11](https://ref.ly/Gen37:5-Gen37:11)) was partially fulfilled when his brothers bowed down to him without recognizing him (see also [43:26](https://ref.ly/Gen43:26); [44:14](https://ref.ly/Gen44:14)). It was totally fulfilled in [50:18](https://ref.ly/Gen50:18). Joseph recognized his brothers immediately, but he could not reveal himself because he did not yet trust them to be the honest men they claimed to be ([42:10](https://ref.ly/Gen42:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:6–GEN 42:7

## Genesis 42:8 (id: 12831)

they didn’t recognize him: Joseph was a grown man, not a boy. He was not wearing a beard, was dressed in Egyptian clothes, and was in an unexpected position, speaking to them through an interpreter ([42:23](https://ref.ly/Gen42:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:8

## Genesis 42:9 (id: 12832)

You are spies! The brothers had considered Joseph a spy for their father and had treated him roughly ([37:2](https://ref.ly/Gen37:2), [14](https://ref.ly/Gen37:14), [18–28](https://ref.ly/Gen37:18-Gen37:28)). Joseph was putting them in a similar situation to see how they would respond.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:9

## Genesis 42:11 (id: 12816)

Joseph knew that they had not always been the honest men they claimed to be.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:11

## Genesis 42:15–17 (id: 12817)

Joseph put the brothers in jail for three days to see if they had a conscience functioning about what they had done ([42:21–23](https://ref.ly/Gen42:21-Gen42:23)). The brothers had similarly thrown Joseph into a cistern\-prison while they decided what to do with him ([37:24](https://ref.ly/Gen37:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:15–GEN 42:17

## Genesis 42:18–20 (id: 12818)

Rather than keep all but one, Joseph would release all but one to take grain home to their starving families.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:18–GEN 42:20

## Genesis 42:21–23 (id: 12819)

Clearly we are being punished: The brothers sensed that having to bring Benjamin back to Egypt against their father’s wishes was God’s punishment for their having sold Joseph to the traders. The sense of divine retribution began to awaken feelings of remorse that Joseph’s cries for mercy and their father’s tears ([37:34–35](https://ref.ly/Gen37:34-Gen37:35)) had failed to arouse.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:21–GEN 42:23

## Genesis 42:22 (id: 12820)

you wouldn’t listen: Reuben had lost the reins of leadership (see study notes on 42:37; 49:3–4).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:22
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 42:37 (ID: `12827@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Genesis 49:3-4 (ID: `12914@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 42:24 (id: 12821)

Joseph turned away from them and began to weep (cp. [43:30](https://ref.ly/Gen43:30); [45:2](https://ref.ly/Gen45:2), [14](https://ref.ly/Gen45:14); [50:1](https://ref.ly/Gen50:1), [17](https://ref.ly/Gen50:17)); perhaps he was hearing part of the story that he had never known (Reuben’s attempt to save him, [37:21–22](https://ref.ly/Gen37:21-Gen37:22), [29](https://ref.ly/Gen37:29)), or his brothers’ remorse moved him to forgiveness.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:24

## Genesis 42:25 (id: 12823)

return each brother’s payment: He was now testing them to awaken their conscience and make them face their past guilt; once again, they were going home with silver instead of a brother ([37:28–35](https://ref.ly/Gen37:28-Gen37:35)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:25

## Genesis 42:25–28 (id: 12822)

Joseph cared for his brothers’ needs; he had forgiven them (see study note on 42:24) and was fulfilling his role to provide for them. God used Joseph’s care to convict the brothers even more fully of their sin.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:25–GEN 42:28
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 42:24 (ID: `12821@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 42:28 (id: 12824)

What has God done to us? They knew that God was behind everything that had been happening, so they faced a day of reckoning for their sins.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:28

## Genesis 42:29–34 (id: 12825)

The brothers’ account focused on the accusation that they were spies and on the need to take Benjamin back to Egypt with them. They omitted their growing realization of divine retribution for their crime against Joseph.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:29–GEN 42:34

## Genesis 42:36 (id: 12826)

Filled with grief over two sons lost already, Jacob feared that he would also lose Benjamin if he went to Egypt. • You are robbing me of my children! He did not realize the full truth of his words, but they must have stung his sons’ guilty consciences.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:36

## Genesis 42:37 (id: 12827)

Reuben tried to take the lead; perhaps he thought he could get back into his father’s good favor (see [35:22](https://ref.ly/Gen35:22)), first by rescuing Joseph from certain death ([37:21–22](https://ref.ly/Gen37:21-Gen37:22), [29–30](https://ref.ly/Gen37:29-Gen37:30)) and now by keeping Benjamin safe.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:37

## Genesis 42:38 (id: 12828)

Jacob was resolute in his favoritism toward Rachel’s remaining son. Benjamin would not go to Egypt even if it meant that Leah’s son Simeon never returned. Jacob’s grief apparently weighed heavily on the brothers’ conscience ([44:18–34](https://ref.ly/Gen44:18-Gen44:34)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 42:38

## Genesis 43:1–7 (id: 12833)

As the famine continued, Jacob’s family needed more grain, but they could not return to Egypt without Benjamin ([42:16](https://ref.ly/Gen42:16), [20](https://ref.ly/Gen42:20)). Jacob realized that he was in a bind; he needed more food but was loath to lose Benjamin.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:1–GEN 43:7

## Genesis 43:8–10 (id: 12845)

Judah broke the deadlock by taking responsibility for Benjamin’s well\-being, thus succeeding where Reuben failed. His action was fitting, since it had been his idea to sell Joseph to the Ishmaelites ([37:26–27](https://ref.ly/Gen37:26-Gen37:27)) instead of killing him. Now he would secure Benjamin’s safety with his own life (see [44:18–34](https://ref.ly/Gen44:18-Gen44:34)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:8–GEN 43:10

## Genesis 43:11–13 (id: 12834)

Jacob provided everything he could to ensure a favorable reception from the Egyptian governor (the man*;* cp. [32:13–21](https://ref.ly/Gen32:13-Gen32:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:11–GEN 43:13

## Genesis 43:13–14 (id: 12835)

Jacob finally entrusted his family’s future to God Almighty*,* the divine title that stresses God’s power (see also [17:1](https://ref.ly/Gen17:1); [28:3](https://ref.ly/Gen28:3); [35:11](https://ref.ly/Gen35:11); [48:3](https://ref.ly/Gen48:3); [1 Kgs 19:10](https://ref.ly/1Kgs19:10), [14](https://ref.ly/1Kgs19:14); [Rev 21:22](https://ref.ly/Rev21:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:13–GEN 43:14

## Genesis 43:16 (id: 12836)

When Joseph saw Benjamin with them: Joseph now knew that Benjamin was well (see study note on 42:1–44:34). The feast was both a celebration and a test.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:16
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 42:1-44.34 (ID: `12814@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 43:18 (id: 12837)

*the brothers were frightened:* Compare [32:6–12](https://ref.ly/Gen32:6-Gen32:12). Their guilt stopped them from seeing that something good might happen.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:18

## Genesis 43:19–22 (id: 12838)

The brothers, completely vulnerable and feeling the weight of God’s judgment on their consciences, approached the manager in desperation.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:19–GEN 43:22

## Genesis 43:24 (id: 12839)

Joseph no longer treated his brothers harshly, but provided kind hospitality.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:24

## Genesis 43:26 (id: 12840)

For the second time, the brothers bowed . . . before Joseph in fulfillment of his first dream ([37:7](https://ref.ly/Gen37:7); see [42:6–7](https://ref.ly/Gen42:6-Gen42:7); cp. [33:3](https://ref.ly/Gen33:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:26

## Genesis 43:29 (id: 12841)

May God be gracious to you, my son: Joseph’s blessing to his full brother fulfilled Jacob’s prayer ([43:14](https://ref.ly/Gen43:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:29

## Genesis 43:30 (id: 12842)

he was overcome with emotion for his brother: Cp. [42:24](https://ref.ly/Gen42:24). Joseph’s tears were of painful memories and years of lost fellowship, as well as of joy and thanksgiving at seeing his brother again. • He went into his private room: He did not yet plan to reveal his identity.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:30

## Genesis 43:33 (id: 12843)

to their amazement: This ruler knew more about the brothers than seemed possible (cp. [44:15](https://ref.ly/Gen44:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:33

## Genesis 43:34 (id: 12844)

The brothers were confronted with generous and gracious dealings from God through Joseph, who tested their tolerance by reenacting the favoritism toward Rachel’s son that had galvanized their earlier hostility.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 43:34

## Genesis 44:1–34 (id: 12846)

The brothers appeared to have changed; they had shown remorse over what they had done to Joseph, and they showed integrity in returning the money and in bringing Benjamin. Given a chance to get rid of Rachel’s other son, Benjamin, would they do it?

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:1–GEN 44:34

## Genesis 44:2 (id: 12852)

Joseph was giving his brothers the chance to abandon Benjamin if they wanted to. Joseph was testing them to see if they were loyal to the family and faithful to their father.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:2

## Genesis 44:5 (id: 12854)

This description would make the brothers understand that the ruler knew things that others could not. •*Hydromancy* (pouring water into oil) and *oenomancy* (pouring wine into other liquids) were methods of divination used in the ancient Near East that would have required such a cup. Joseph was continuing his ruse (see [42:7](https://ref.ly/Gen42:7); cp. [30:27](https://ref.ly/Gen30:27); see also [Lev 19:26](https://ref.ly/Lev19:26); [Num 23:23](https://ref.ly/Num23:23); [Deut 18:10–11](https://ref.ly/Deut18:10-Deut18:11))—he knew that only God grants revelation (see [Gen 37:5–9](https://ref.ly/Gen37:5-Gen37:9); [40:8](https://ref.ly/Gen40:8); [41:16](https://ref.ly/Gen41:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:5

## Genesis 44:9–10 (id: 12855)

The punishment that the brothers proposed was harsher than necessary; it was normal in antiquity to stake your life on what you said. The palace manager was agreeable but enforced a punishment that matched Joseph’s intentions ([44:17](https://ref.ly/Gen44:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:9–GEN 44:10

## Genesis 44:11–12 (id: 12847)

The palace manager knew where the cup was, but he created more anxiety by beginning with the oldest.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:11–GEN 44:12

## Genesis 44:13 (id: 12848)

they tore their clothing in despair: They knew what it would do to Jacob to lose Benjamin ([42:38](https://ref.ly/Gen42:38)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:13

## Genesis 44:14 (id: 12849)

This time, the brothers did not bow politely (see [42:6–7](https://ref.ly/Gen42:6-Gen42:7); [43:26](https://ref.ly/Gen43:26)); they fell to the ground in desperation, fulfilling Joseph’s first dream for the third time (see [37:10](https://ref.ly/Gen37:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:14

## Genesis 44:16 (id: 12850)

Judah again spoke for the group. • God is punishing us for our sins: God was completing the work of repentance in their hearts. Judah again proposed that they all be punished. Benjamin was seemingly guilty of this theft, but all of the others were guilty of sin against Joseph. They preferred not returning to Jacob at all versus seeing his grief at the loss of Benjamin ([44:34](https://ref.ly/Gen44:34)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:16

## Genesis 44:18–34 (id: 12851)

Judah made good on his promise to pay for Benjamin’s safety ([43:8–10](https://ref.ly/Gen43:8-Gen43:10)). His lengthy plea to be imprisoned in place of the lad ([44:33](https://ref.ly/Gen44:33)) is one of the most moving acts of intercession in Scripture. It demonstrated Judah’s concern for their father and his willingness to give up everything for the sake of his brother. With this kind of integrity (see [John 15:13](https://ref.ly/John15:13)), Judah showed himself to be a true leader, qualified to receive the blessing of the firstborn. Through him the kings of Israel would come (see [Gen 49:10](https://ref.ly/Gen49:10)). • The brothers had fully repented, as expressed by Judah’s intercession. Because of their change, Joseph could make himself known to them ([45:1–15](https://ref.ly/Gen45:1-Gen45:15)) and arrange for the family to join him in Egypt where there was food ([45:16](https://ref.ly/Gen45:16); [47:12](https://ref.ly/Gen47:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:18–GEN 44:34

## Genesis 44:32–34 (id: 12853)

Judah was willing to give up his family, his future, and his freedom for others.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 44:32–GEN 44:34

## Genesis 45:1–15 (id: 12856)

In one of the most dramatic scenes of the book, Joseph revealed his identity to his brothers, bringing the process of reconciling with them to a climax (see also [50:14–21](https://ref.ly/Gen50:14-Gen50:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:1–GEN 45:15

## Genesis 45:2 (id: 12864)

This is the third of five times in the story that Joseph wept over his brothers (see also [42:24](https://ref.ly/Gen42:24); [43:30](https://ref.ly/Gen43:30); [45:14](https://ref.ly/Gen45:14); [50:17](https://ref.ly/Gen50:17); cp. [50:1](https://ref.ly/Gen50:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:2

## Genesis 45:3 (id: 12865)

The brothers were stunned at the news, unable to speak from amazement and fear ([45:5](https://ref.ly/Gen45:5); cp. [50:15](https://ref.ly/Gen50:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:3

## Genesis 45:5–8 (id: 12866)

God . . . sent me is the central message of the account of Jacob’s family ([37:2](https://ref.ly/Gen37:2)). As the Lord had told Abraham, he was leading the Israelites into Egypt ([15:13](https://ref.ly/Gen15:13)). God had sent Joseph to Egypt to prepare for his family’s rescue during the famine. In what has become a classic statement of God’s sovereignty, Joseph explained that God had been working through all of the circumstances and human acts to bring about his plan. The certainty of God’s will is the basis for forgiveness and reconciliation with those who do wrong, cause hurt, or bring harm. If people do not believe that God is sovereign, then they will blame others and retaliate. Those who are spiritual will trust that God is at work even through human wickedness (see also [Rom 8:28–30](https://ref.ly/Rom8:28-Rom8:30)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:5–GEN 45:8

## Genesis 45:9–13 (id: 12867)

Joseph instructed his brothers to inform Jacob. The whole family was to move to Egypt and live in Goshen, a fertile region in the Nile delta (see [47:1–12](https://ref.ly/Gen47:1-Gen47:12)). If they did not come to Egypt, they would not survive the five years of famine ahead.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:9–GEN 45:13

## Genesis 45:10 (id: 12857)

The region of Goshen was in the northeast corner of Egypt, only a few days’ walk from Canaan. Jacob’s family stayed there because there was food and water for themselves and their flocks; later, they were kept there by Egyptians who put them to slave labor.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:10

## Genesis 45:14–15 (id: 12858)

Joseph was reunited with his brothers—first with Benjamin and then with the rest. Their previous hatred and jealousy of Joseph ([37:4–11](https://ref.ly/Gen37:4-Gen37:11)) had come to an end. The brothers experienced forgiveness from God and from Joseph. Far from commanding his brothers to bow down to him (see [42:6–7](https://ref.ly/Gen42:6-Gen42:7)), Joseph welcomed them. Joseph held no grudge because he accepted what had happened as God’s work and saw the good that had resulted (see [50:14–21](https://ref.ly/Gen50:14-Gen50:21)). This is how wisdom rules: The wise leader will forgive and restore (see study note on 37:2–50:26).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:14–GEN 45:15
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 37:2-50.26 (ID: `12724@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 45:16–25 (id: 12859)

Joseph sent his brothers with instructions to bring Jacob and the entire family to Egypt. Out of gratitude for Joseph’s having saved all of Egypt ([45:18](https://ref.ly/Gen45:18); see [47:20](https://ref.ly/Gen47:20)), Pharaoh promised the best of all the land of Egypt, and Joseph gave them the finest products of Egypt.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:16–GEN 45:25

## Genesis 45:16–47.12 (id: 12860)

This section is transitional, as the family moved from Canaan to Goshen, where they would live for the next four centuries.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:16–GEN 47:12

## Genesis 45:24 (id: 12861)

Don’t quarrel about all this along the way! When they were away from Joseph, they might begin to accuse one another about the past or argue about how to explain what had happened to Jacob. It was now time to put the past behind them and enjoy the reunion.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:24

## Genesis 45:26–28 (id: 12862)

As might be expected, Jacob was stunned when he heard that his son Joseph was still alive and ruling all the land of Egypt. As he heard the details of their story and saw all that Joseph had sent him, he was convinced that it was true. He immediately prepared to move to Egypt and reunite with his son Joseph, whom he had not seen for twenty\-two years.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:26–GEN 45:28

## Genesis 45:27 (id: 12863)

their father’s spirits revived: This royal invitation to Jacob, an old man near the end of hope, and to the ten brothers burdened with guilty fears, was a turning point in their lives. It was also a fulfillment of God’s prediction ([15:13–16](https://ref.ly/Gen15:13-Gen15:16)) that they would go into seclusion in a foreign country and there become a great nation without losing their identity. The joyful news about Joseph changed the lives of everyone in this family for the good.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 45:27

## Genesis 46:1 (id: 12869)

set out for Egypt: A little over 200 years earlier, Abraham had similarly gone down into Egypt during a famine in Canaan ([12:10](https://ref.ly/Gen12:10)), and God had protected him there. • Jacob’s first stop was at Beersheba, where Abraham had sacrificed to the Lord and worshiped him after settling his land and water rights with the Philistines ([21:31–33](https://ref.ly/Gen21:31-Gen21:33)). This was where Isaac had lived, and where Jacob had lived before he fled from Esau’s anger ([28:10](https://ref.ly/Gen28:10)). • all his possessions: See [46:5–7](https://ref.ly/Gen46:5-Gen46:7).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:1

## Genesis 46:1–4 (id: 12868)

God reassured Jacob about his move to Egypt.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:1–GEN 46:4

## Genesis 46:2–4 (id: 12870)

In a night vision, the Lord repeated his promise to go with Jacob and make his family into a great nation in Egypt. The same God who led the family into Egypt promised to bring them out of Egypt to live once again in the land of Canaan.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:2–GEN 46:4

## Genesis 46:4 (id: 12878)

You will die: He would have a peaceful death, surrounded by his family and many blessings from God ([49:33](https://ref.ly/Gen49:33)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:4

## Genesis 46:8–27 (id: 12879)

This genealogy of the sons of Jacob shows that all the tribes of Israel went together to the land of Egypt; they would all leave together as well (see the book of Exodus).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:8–GEN 46:27

## Genesis 46:20 (id: 12871)

On: See study note on 41:45.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:20
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 41:45 (ID: `12806@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 46:26 (id: 12872)

The total number . . . was sixty\-six: This is the number of those who traveled with Jacob to Egypt, excluding his sons’ wives, the servants, and others attached to the household. It also omits Joseph, Ephraim, Manasseh, and Jacob.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:26

## Genesis 46:27 (id: 12873)

The total seventy includes Joseph, Ephraim, Manasseh, and Jacob. Seventy is also a symbolic number for perfection or completion (see “Symbolic Numbers” Theme Note). From these seventy (i.e., all Israel) would grow the nation of Israel that would bless the seventy nations (i.e., all the nations) of the world (see study note on Gen 10:2–32; cp. [12:3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:27
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 10:2-32 (ID: `12250@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Symbolic Numbers (ID: `500445@TyndaleStudyNotesThemes`)

## Genesis 46:28–34 (id: 12874)

Jacob finally saw his son Joseph again; their reunion was overwhelmingly joyful.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:28–GEN 46:34

## Genesis 46:29 (id: 12875)

Joseph . . . embraced his father and wept: Joseph was seventeen when he had last seen his father ([37:2](https://ref.ly/Gen37:2)); now he was thirty\-nine.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:29

## Genesis 46:30 (id: 12876)

Jacob was satisfied just to see his beloved son alive—the firstborn of his chosen wife Rachel and the designated family leader (see study note on 48:5–7; see also [1 Chr 5:1–2](https://ref.ly/1Chr5:1-1Chr5:2)). More than just a family reunion, this was confirmation that God’s plan was intact.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:30
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 48:5-7 (ID: `12895@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 46:34 (id: 12877)

In contrast to the syncretistic Canaanites, who would have absorbed the Israelites had they stayed in Canaan, Egyptians detested Semitic shepherds out of a sense of ethnic superiority and observed a strict segregation (see [43:32](https://ref.ly/Gen43:32)). When Jacob’s family settled in Egypt, this separation would allow the people to grow into a great nation without losing their identity.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 46:34

## Genesis 47:1 (id: 12882)

Goshen (see study note on 45:10) is not referred to in ancient Egyptian texts; the name it bore in later Egyptian writings was “the region of Rameses” ([47:11](https://ref.ly/Gen47:11); see [Exod 1:11](https://ref.ly/Exod1:11)). It was fertile and near to Joseph at court, which suggests that it was on the eastern side of the Nile delta.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 47:1
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 45:10 (ID: `12857@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 47:1–6 (id: 12880)

Pharaoh responded as Joseph hoped by giving Jacob’s family the best part of the land; he even gave some of the brothers oversight of his own livestock ([47:6](https://ref.ly/Gen47:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 47:1–GEN 47:6

## Genesis 47:7–10 (id: 12887)

When Jacob entered Pharaoh’s court and when he left, he blessed Pharaoh, an indication of Jacob’s position as God’s representative (see [Heb 7:7](https://ref.ly/Heb7:7)). God had promised that he would bless those who blessed Abraham’s family ([Gen 12:2–3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:2-Gen12:3)), so he now blessed Pharaoh and Egypt.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 47:7–GEN 47:10

## Genesis 47:13–26 (id: 12881)

The Lord blessed Pharaoh because Pharaoh was blessing Abraham’s descendants ([12:3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:3)). Through Joseph’s wise administration in Egypt, the Lord saved the people from starvation and prospered Pharaoh. In selling food to the Egyptians during the years of famine, Joseph accepted money, livestock, and finally land as payment, until almost all of Egypt belonged to Pharaoh. Meanwhile, God provided Israel with some of the best land in Egypt where they could live, work, and multiply.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 47:13–GEN 47:26

## Genesis 47:21 (id: 12883)

he made them all slaves: In Hebrew script, the difference is very slight between *slaves* (Hebrew ‘abadim) and *towns* (Hebrew ‘arim). Moving the people into the towns doesn’t fit the context very well, so most translations select *slaves* as the reading that makes the most sense (cp. [47:20](https://ref.ly/Gen47:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 47:21

## Genesis 47:27 (id: 12884)

God blessed his people according to his promise to Abraham that his descendants would be innumerable ([15:5](https://ref.ly/Gen15:5); [22:17](https://ref.ly/Gen22:17)). They had to wait for the fulfillment of the second promise, that they would own the land of Canaan ([17:8](https://ref.ly/Gen17:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 47:27

## Genesis 47:29–31 (id: 12885)

bury me with my ancestors: Cp. [49:29–33](https://ref.ly/Gen49:29-Gen49:33). Jacob wanted to be buried with Abraham and Isaac in the cave of Machpelah (see study note on 49:29–33; see [23:1–20](https://ref.ly/Gen23:1-Gen23:20); [25:7–10](https://ref.ly/Gen25:7-Gen25:10); [35:27–29](https://ref.ly/Gen35:27-Gen35:29)). • Put your hand under my thigh: This custom (cp. [24:1–9](https://ref.ly/Gen24:1-Gen24:9) and study note on 24:2) was a serious oath to carry on the covenant, which had as its main promise innumerable descendants in the Promised Land.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 47:29–GEN 47:31
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 24:2 (ID: `12490@TyndaleStudyNotes`); Genesis 49:29-33 (ID: `12913@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Genesis 47:31 (id: 12886)

When the oath was taken, Jacob bowed humbly in worship and thanked the Lord for ensuring that he would be buried with his ancestors in the land of promise (cp. [1 Kgs 1:47](https://ref.ly/1Kgs1:47)).

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 47:31

## Genesis 48:1–22 (id: 12888)

In blessing Ephraim and Manasseh, Jacob reached out by faith for the promise to be continued, having learned that God’s ways are not always the ways of men. Out of Jacob’s long life, the writer to the Hebrews selected the blessing of Joseph’s sons as his great act of faith ([Heb 11:21](https://ref.ly/Heb11:21)). As Jacob acted in light of God’s will, the primary blessing was again given to the younger instead of the older son, but without scheming and its bitter results.

* **Associated Passages:** GEN 48:1–GEN 48:22

## Genesis 48:3–4 (id: 12894)
